TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK
Abstract
Density Functional Theory Calculations on Phosphate Binding Peptides
by Thomas Østergaard
Phosphor is vital nutrient for humanity. Not only in a biological sense, but also because
phosphor is used in the fertilizers that sustain our food supply. All phosphorous used in
fertilizers, are mined from phosphate rock deposits, highlighting that phosphor is a fossil
resource. Recent studies suggest that it is approaching depletion, and reveal that most
of the phosphate is wasted, either in agricultural ﬁelds or in the management of livestock
waste. It is evident that techniques and practises for reusing the lost phosphorous must
be improved.
One way of capturing phosphate, that is both reversible and energy-eﬃcient, is by pro-
teins or peptides with particular aﬃnity for phosphate-binding.
A small phosphate-
binding hexapeptide has been synthesised previously in the literature. The peptide is
based on the functionality of conserved residues in a class of phosphate binding proteins,
called P-loops. As the hexapeptide binds phosphate at neutral pH, and sulphate bind-
ing was reported unfavourable, it shows great potential for biomimetic applications in
selective phosphate-capturing devices.
In this thesis, a computational study of the binding mechanism of the hexapeptide is pre-
sented. The binding mechanism is investigated using density functional theory (DFT),
and the simulations are performed with the computational chemistry software Gaus-
sian 09. The binding structure of the peptide is elucidated, and a binding conformation
proposed in the literature is found particular stable. The peptide is small, and is dy-
namically changing in between an ensemble of equilibrium structures. In combination
with previous molecular dynamics studies, this large conformation space is taken into
account, and an average gain in electronic energy is reported for phosphate binding.
The energy calculations are compared to experimental thermodynamic properties, re-
ported in the literature, and large discrepancies between computations and reported
experimental values are observed. They are explained by the speciﬁc interaction with
the surrounding of the peptide-anion complex. In addition the selectivity for phosphate
binding over sulphate binding is investigated, and conﬁrmed.
The peptide has potential in phosphate-recovery devices, and two types of capturing
schemes are proposed.

ii
Danmarks Tekniske Universitet
Resum´e
Tæthedsfunktionalteori Beregninger P˚a Fosfatbindende Peptider
Fosfor er et essentielt næringsstof for menneskeheden. Ikke kun i en biologisk forstand,
men ogs˚a fordi fosfor anvendes i kunstgødning, der opretholder vores fødevareforsyning.
Alt det fosfor der i dag anvendes i kunstgødning udvindes fra mineralsk fosfor. Dette
understreger at fosfor er en fossil ressource som der vil blive mangel p˚a. Nye studier
viser, at størstedelen af den fosfat der bruges i kunstgødning g˚ar til spilde, enten p˚a
landbrugsmarker eller under h˚andtering af animalsk aﬀald. Det st˚ar klart, at teknikker
til og praksis omkring at genbruge den spildte fosfat, skal forbedres.
´En m˚ade at indfange fosfat p˚a, der b˚ade er reversibel og energi-eﬀektiv, er ved brug
af proteiner eller peptider med særlig fosfataﬃnitet. Et lille fosfatbindende hexapeptid
er tidligere i litteraturen blevet syntetiseret. Peptidet er baseret p˚a funktionaliteten af
bevarede aminosyresekvenser i en klasse af fosfatbindende proteiner, der kaldes P-loops.
Hexapeptidet binder fosfat ved en neutral pH-værdi og det binder ikke sulfat. Derfor
viser det potentiale for biomimetiske anvendelser i selektive enheder til opfangelse af
fosfat.
I denne afhandling præsenteres et beregningsstudie af bindingsmekanismen i hexapep-
tidet. Dette undersøges ved brug af tæthedsfunktionalteori (DFT) og simuleringerne er
udført med programmet Gaussian 09. Strukturen af peptidet i fosfatbindingen præsen-
teres og en karakteristisk konformation, fundet i litteraturen, er fundet særlig stabil.
I en vandopløsning er peptidets struktur dynamisk og skifter imellem et ensemble af
ligevægtsstrukturer. I sammenhold med tidligere molekyledynamikundersøgelser (MD)
bliver disse mange konformationer taget i betragtning og fosfatbindingen viser sig forde-
lagtig i kraft af en gennemsnitlig gevinst i elektronisk energi.
Energiberegningerne sammenlignes med peptidets eksperimentelt rapporterede termo-
dynamiske egenskaber fra litteraturen. Store forskelle observeres mellem de beregnede og
rapporterede værdier. De forklares ved den speciﬁkke interaktion mellem omgivelserne
of peptid-anion komplekset. Desuden undersøges og bekræftes selektivitet for fosfat-
binding frem for sulfatbinding.
Peptidet har potentiale indefor fosfatopfangende enheder, og der foresl˚as to typer af
opfangningsmetoder.

F. Gruber. In addition to supplying peptide structures for many of the simulations in
this thesis, he has guided me in the day-to-day discussions and helped in keeping the
project on the right track.
My best of thanks goes to Co-Master student Sigurd F. Truelsen, for a great working
environment, fruitful discussions and all the fun we had.
Special thanks goes to Andrea Bordoni, who thoroughly answered my questions to the
Gaussian software, and the methods of DFT.
I thank all the group members including, PhD student Niada Bajraktari, PhD student
Elizabeth B. Wood and Assoc. Prof. Claus H. Nielsen, for involving me in the work of
the group and big thanks to the group and the Aquaporin Academy for wonderful place
to ﬁnish my studies.

==PAGE:15==
Chapter 1
Introduction
Biomimetics is the ﬁeld of employing principles of nature in design of new tecnologies.
In this project, the aim is on biomimetic applications of a phosphor-binding peptide,
focused on the prospects of recovering lost phosphorous wasted in the agriculture. Phos-
phate capture has been a subject of interest for many years with regards to waste water
treatment of phosphor pollution to avoid eutrophication. However, in recent years min-
eral phosphate has been classiﬁed as a scarce resource [2], that is essential to sustaining
a human population of current size.
Therefore the focus has shifted from removing
phosphor to capturing and reusing it.
Recent litterature [3] suggest that phosphate binding proteins has prospect of playing a
part of the global solution to the problem of phosphor recovery.
Using simulation software accurate at a quantum level of theory, the phosphate binding
of a speciﬁc hexapeptide will here be investigated. This small peptide has been reported
to bind phosphate anions [1] in a hypothesised binding structure (a nest). Using the
theoretical tools of density functional theory and molecular dynamics, it is possible to
gain access to binding dynamics of the peptide and attain an upper estimate on an
actual binding strength.
The project is in line with the idea of establishing a rational design approach by investi-
gating the fundamental principles of the binding mechanism. What is important to the
anion binding? And how can the binding strength of the peptide be improved?
1
==PAGE:16==
Chapter 1. Introduction
2
These are questions that this report seek to answer in order to contribute to the ﬁeld of
recovering phosphor using proteins.
1.1
Phosphorous is Vital, Damaging and being Depleted
Today mineral phosphorous is used in many industries, perhaps most importantly in the
fertilizer industry. Since the Green Revolution in the 1960s the production of fertilizer
has exploded and increased food availability, both regarding crops and livestock. This
development has supported the continuous growth in human population ever since the
1950s, see ﬁg. 1.1. However, like all other resources extracted from nature, mineral
phosphorous is a ﬁnite resource. In addition, phosphor is a highly localized resource
and is approaching depletion [4]. The localization is basis for international disputes and
unrest, and the depletion predicts widespread famine - especially in developing countries
[2].
Phosphor (P) is essential to organisms. It plays an important role in living cells, and is
found in molecular compounds such as phospholipid molecules, ATP, DNA and RNA, in
ways where there is no substitute [5]. In order to sustain the vitality of an organism, a
continuous supply of PO4 is needed to regenerate ATP, and we, humans, gain P through
food that ultimately is extracted from the earth by plants.
Plants and thus food requires P to grow and food is, obviously, essential to our existence.
Crop production has before the 20th century primarily relied on the natural levels of
soil P and the addition of manure or human excreta. However, in the 20th century the
human population has rapidly increased. To keep up with the exploding food demand,
increasingly amounts of guano and mineral P has been applied in the food production [2].
The historical sources for P fertilizer is hown in ﬁg. 1.2. It shows that phosphate rock
is being extensively mined from around the mid-20th century, which coincides with the
Green Revolution. In addition to introducing new crop varieties, the Green Revolution
also involved the application of chemical fertilizers, by virtue of the Haber-Bosch process.
The ”revolution” was a response to a massive famine in India, and saved millions from
starvation. It introduced new agricultural practices that now sustain our increasingly
growing population. Practices that are closely linked to the use of processed mineral P
fertilizers [2]. Today, other undernourished regions such as Sub-Sahara Africa call for a
==PAGE:17==
Chapter 1. Introduction
3
new Green Revolution and additionally meat and dairy products which require higher
P input, are becoming increasingly popular in China and India [2]. This increasingly
puts pressure on the demand for phosphate rock and fertilizers, and in 2007-2008 the
prices on P sky-rocketed by 700% [5]. Interestingly, increasing awareness and concern
for fossil-fuel depletion and global warming has lead to an increase in biofuel production
that signiﬁcantly contributes to the depletion of fossil mineral P [2].
Figure 1.1: World population since 1950. Since the Green Revolution around 1940-
1970 the human population has almost increased threefold. From [6].
Figure 1.2: Historical phosphorous sources for fertilizers. From the 1940s the ex-
ploitation of fossil P is rapidly increasing. From Cordell et al. [2].
Phosphate rock is highly localized. Nearly 90% of the current estimated phosphorous
reserves are under control of ﬁve countries: Morocco, China, South Africa, Jordan and
==PAGE:18==
Chapter 1. Introduction
4
the United states.
It is estimated that 50-100 years remain of current reserves [2].
An alarming fact considering that all modern agriculture today depends on phosphate
fertilizer derived from mined phosphate rock.
Naturally this sparks the question of
securing food security. China with the second largest reserves imposed export restrictions
in response of the 2008 fertilizer price crisis which eﬀectively eliminated all exports. The
restriction was lifted as the food crisis faded, but it indicates the sort of international
tension that may become regular occurrence should P supplies fall in short [4]. US has
approximately 25 years left of domestic reserves, and are importing signiﬁcant amounts
of phosphate rock from Morocco which has the largest phosphate rock reserves. However,
many of their mines are in Western Sahara, an independent territory that is occupied
by Morocco and is site for growing international human rights concerns.
If current agricultural practices continue, a humanitarian disaster seems inevitable. The
demand for phosphate rock have to be reduced, and one way to achieve this is to recover
and recycle the phosphorous discharged from fertilizers.
In fact, due to ineﬃcient agricultural practices, much of the fertilizer used is lost, and
ends up in rivers, lakes and oceans. This introduce a huge environmental issue as it
causes eutrophication. Eutrophic lakes and rivers are subject to excessive algal growth,
that is reducing their suitability for recreational use and for drinking water. Eutroph-
ication can lead to oxygen depletion that in turn lead to ﬁsh kills. It has also been
linked to corral reef death and the so called ”dead zone” in the Gulf of Mexico. In
short eutrophication in both fresh and salt waters causes loss of aesthetic, ecological,
and economic value of aquatic ecosystems [7].
Given also the increasing demand for, and the eventual depletion of, mineral phosphate,
this highlights the non-sustainable way mineral P, derived from phosphate rock, is being
managed and used. It points to the fact current agricultural practices cannot be sus-
tained. Thus, it is paramount to dramatically reduce the demand for phosphate rock by
eliminating our wasteful practices. This will require both low- and high-tech solutions
as well as a restructuring of the phosphorous cycle. Fortunately P can be used over and
over again.
In order to achieve a sustainable and more nature-friendly utilization of P, the fertilizer
must be used more eﬃciently and practices and technologies for P recycling must be
advanced [5].
==PAGE:19==
Chapter 1. Introduction
5
1.1.1
Recovery Technologies
This project will investigate a proposed method or technology for recycling, that is
capturing and releasing, phosphate anions in a solution. To understand the context of
such technology, where it is valid and to what extend it may be useful, it is important to
understand how P is being lost. According to Rittmann et al. [3] and Cordell et al. [2]
about 80 % of the mined P is applied to the agricultural ﬁelds, but only about 40 % is
absorbed or used by the crops. Of that only 23% is found in human food and 16% is being
consumed. Most of the consumed P is subsequently conveyed as sewage to municipal
wastewater-treatment plants. The two largest ﬂows of lost P are in agricultural run-oﬀ
and erosion and in animal wastes [3]. Agricultural run-oﬀand erosion is the eﬀect of
wind and weather on the ﬂow of phosphorous applied to crop ﬁelds. For instance the
rain can wash fertilizer oﬀan agricultural ﬁeld and into the aquatic ecosystem. This
type of phosphorous loss ﬂow is responsible for the loss of about 46% of the mined P
globally, and is characterised by a high volumetric ﬂow rate, but a low concentration of
P. Almost all P in erosion and runoﬀgoes directly into surface waters and accelerates
eutrophication.
Animal waste is responsible for about 40 % of the P loss ﬂow. Some P from animal
waste is reapplied to agricultural ﬁelds as fertilizer, but most is discharged to surface
waters. This is due to a lack of regulations on animal waste control. The ﬂow loss is
characterized by a low volumetric ﬂow rate but high P concentration. In the work of
Rittmann et al. [3], the two ﬂows has been analysed, and recovering from animal wastes
is found both technologically and economically the most viable strategy.
The ﬁrst step in the strategy for recovering the large ﬂow of lost P in animal waste,
is to ensure that the P is in a recoverable form. That is, to convert organic P into
inorganic P, which is used in fertilizers. The authors of [3] suggests a treatment of the
animal waste stream by digestion of the organic solids, in anaerobic energy-conversion
processes that releases inorganic P. The second step is to remove the inorganic P, now
present as phosphate, from the water stream in a form that can be reused.
They outline four methods of capturing the inorganic P: precipitation, adsorption, ion
exchange and biological uptake.
==PAGE:20==
Chapter 1. Introduction
6
Precipitaion
also called crystalization, is a well-established practice from the 1950s.
The P becomes bound to a metal ion such as Mg2+, Ca2+, Al2+ or Fe3+. Magnesium-
and calcium-based precipitated P are commonly reused as fertilizer. Aluminum- and
iron-based products are unsuitable for recovery purposes, as aluminium is toxic to many
plants and iron phosphates are unavailable to plants. Phosphate recovery as struvite
(MgNH4PO4 · 6 H2O) is common practice and can be used for fertilization.
Adsorption
is the transfer of liquid-phase solutes to solid-phase adsorbents. A widely
used method in wastewater treatment plants has since the 1950s been adsorption during
coagulation, followed by removal through sedimentation and ﬁltration.
A variety of
metal-based adsorbents can be used.
Ion Exchange
refers to a method where ions can be exchanged for solid-phase ions
based on ion aﬃnity. In contrast to precipitation and adsorption, ion exchange is gener-
ally a reversible process which makes it especially promising for P recovery. This method
is, however, not as established as separation and recovery of inorganic P by precipitation
or adsorption.
Biological Uptake
is a common denominator for a number of methods. In enhanced
biological phosphorus removal, the P is stored as polymerized phosphate within het-
erotrophic biomass. It is commonly used in treatment of domestic wastewater. The
product is organic P and may require treatment prior to being reapplied as fertilizer.
A second method is the use of phototrophic microorganisms which, together with sun-
light and CO2, can uptake P. Lastly the idea of using the high-aﬃnity phosphate-binding
protein PstS, that is expressed in a variety of microorganisms in low-P environments,
is introduced. They suggest that such proteins could be used in engineered systems to
bind soluble inorganic phosphate anions.
The methods of phototrophic microorganisms and phosphate-binding proteins both re-
quire further research and adaptation, and it is exactly the aim of this project to inves-
tigate the phosphate binding aﬃnity of a synthetic hexa-peptide. The most
developed phosphate recovery method is by precipitation. However, this method suﬀers
the drawback that it is not a reversible process. For struvite both energy and ammonium
is required and spent. A protein system that captures phosphate from a solution, and
==PAGE:21==
Chapter 1. Introduction
7
releases it subsequently in a controlled manner would truly be a reversible system that
would be energy and material eﬃcient and not be harmful to nature.
1.2
P-loops and a Synthetic Hexapeptide
A P-loop (phosphate-binding loop) is a protein motif associated with phosphate binding.
It is also known as a Walker-A motif as it was ﬁrst reported in ATP-binding proteins
by Walker and co-workers in 1982 [8]. It is part of many proteins that utilize and bind
the β-phosphate of ATP or GTP [1].
P-loops are recognized by the amino-acid pattern GXXXXGK(T/S), where G, K, T
and S denote glycine, lysine, threonine and serine residues respectively, and X denotes
any amino acid. As P-loops often occur at the loop before an α-helix, the phosphate
binding has been widely regarded as the β-phosphate would bind the the N-terminus of
the α-helix. However it is also the view that the P-loop acts like a nest that binds the
phosphate in a highly conserved manner [1], where the lysine (K) residue, together with
the main chain NH atoms, are crucial for binding β-phosphate.
In 2012 Bianchi et al. [1] published a P-loop peptide sequence that was shown to bind in-
organic phosphate anions. The sequence was SGAGKT, and it was designed to have the
phosphate-binding properties, observed in the β-phosphate binding P-loops of proteins.
This hexapeptide, shown in ﬁg. 1.3, is a short peptide and cannot form an α-helix. As it
binds phosphate, this suggested that it is the nest, rather than being at the N-terminus
of an α-helix, that is the main phosphate binding feature. The group experimentally
showed that this peptide would on its own bind HPO4
2– with a Gibbs free energy of 4.0
kcal/mol.
The study of this small peptide, with demonstrated aﬃnity for phosphate-binding, will
be the ﬁrst step in the investigation of a biomimetic phosphate-recovery device based
on proteins.
==PAGE:22==
Chapter 1. Introduction
8
Figure 1.3: Linear conformation of the Ser-Gly-Ala-Gly-Lys-Thr hexapeptide in zwit-
terionic protonation state. The lysine side chain is expected to form a nest, together
with the main chain. The image is made in Avogadro [9, 10]. Hydrogen is white, carbon
is grey, oxygen is red and nitrogen is blue.
1.3
Thesis Outline
This thesis will constitute a computational study of the phosphate binding mechanism
of the hexapeptide of Bianchi et al. [1]. For such a study one can choose between a wide
variety of theoretical frameworks and simulation software.
In previous work of supervisor and PhD student Mathias Felix Gruber [11], the con-
formational space of the SGAGKT-peptide has been studied using molecular dynamics
(MD) in Amber14 [12]. The study concludes that the peptide indeed is a phosphate
binder. However it is uncertain whether the interaction between the peptide and the
anion is described accurately enough, and a quantitative binding energy cannot either
be extracted from the simulations.
This study will, based on the structures of the MD simulation, use a more accurate, yet
computationally more expensive, quantum mechanical description of the peptide-anion
interaction. These methods allows for an estimation of an upper limit, to the phosphate
binding strength of the SGAGKT-peptide.
Density functional theory (DFT) will be the theoretical framework for an accurate quan-
tum mechanical description of the system. The simulations of the peptide will performed
==PAGE:23==
Chapter 1. Introduction
9
with the Gaussian 09 rev. D [13] software suite, and a list of the most important soft-
ware used in this work is found in app. A. The thesis includes:
Theory section
that introduce the electronic structure methods used in the simu-
lation software Gaussian 09. First the Schr¨odinger equation and how to apply it to
a molecular system is discussed. This also includes the approximations necessary for
a computationally viable framework. The Hartree-Fock (HF) theory and the density
functional theory (DFT) follows. Both these theories for describing the ground-state
of a molecule are used in the simulation software. This constitutes a formal abstract
theory that has to be practically applied to a system. Therefore a section on how to
apply the theory to a large molecule is included.
The last part of the theory section is an introduction to Gaussian, and the diﬀerent
jobs and calculations that are used in this work.
Presentation of the peptide and the MD results
includes an introduction to the
class of phosphate binding proteins that are called P-loops. Inspired by the functional
groups P-loops, Bianchi [1] and co-workers have synthesised a hexapeptide that according
to their experiments binds phosphate.
Characterization of the binding
is comprised of a series of investigations on the
peptide-anion complex. First a test on the modelling options to choose from in Gaus-
sian is presented. This section helps to ensure an accurate, yet cost-eﬃcient, description
of the peptide-anion complex. Next follows an investigation where the binding nest is
demonstrated solely by DFT methods. The peptide is build in a linear conﬁguration,
as shown in ﬁg. 1.3, with an HPO4
2– anion in the vicinity. By letting this structure
relax, a number of nest-like conﬁgurations are attained. With this encouraging result for
modelling the binding, the next investigation on the binding strength of the nest conﬁgu-
ration is presented. The binding is indeed found favourable, however, this single energy
calculation is very uncertain, and a large-scale calculation using many conﬁgurations
must be performed for an accurate result.
In the simulation of the system, water is included in a so called implicit model. This
model aﬀects the structures that the Gaussian simulations ﬁnd, and this in turn aﬀect
==PAGE:24==
Chapter 1. Introduction
10
the binding energy calculated. So before a large-scale calculation of the binding energy
can be performed, this eﬀect must be evaluated. Thus a section investigating the water-
model is introduced.
The ﬁnal part of the binding characterization consists of great number of binding energy
calculations. The structures used are results of MD simulations. We obtain a binding
energy with a quantiﬁed associated uncertainty, and can therefore deﬁnitely conclude
that the peptide has aﬃnity for binding phosphate. The binding energy is around 25
kcal/mol - a far stronger binding than reported by Bianchi et al. [1], and consequently
a discussion of the merit of the calculations follows. The discrepancies are related to
the thermodynamics of the peptide-anion complex. It is proposed that the interaction
with the nearest molecules are highly important for the binding mechanism by an eﬀect
called desolvation.
Investigation of the capability to bind sulphate
presents a quick calculation
of the sulphate binding energy. It turns out to be very close to the phosphate binding
energy. Again this suggests that the electrostatic interaction energy between the peptide
and the anion is not the full story of the binding mechanism.
Device suggestions
The peptide has potential in phosphate-recovery devices, and
two types of capturing schemes are proposed.
==PAGE:25==
Chapter 2
Theory
The binding mechanism of phosphate anions for the Bianchi hexapeptide [1] (introduced
in sec. 1.2 and 3.1) can be studied with many diﬀerent tools. The main branches of
study methods are the experimental, the computational and/or the theoretical. Each
has its sub-methods. In a computational study, it is typically the size of the system that
determines what tool to use. For a very large system with thousands of atoms, one can
employ molecular dynamics (MD) (bio-molecules, organic compounds and polymers) [14,
15] or Monte Carlo simulations (solids) [16]. For instance, in MD one can compute the
potential energy of large bio-molecules such as proteins and nucleic acids in a solution.
The impressive system size comes at the cost of a coarse description. The inter-atomic
interaction is described with an accuracy that is only valid under very speciﬁc conditions,
and MD is incapable of capturing the formation, and breaking, of molecular bonds [15].
A hexapeptide is much smaller than a protein (typically a peptide of more than 50
amino acids is called a protein).
However, as for proteins, its structural properties
(conformational space) is expected to be highly governed by the solution around it. The
motion of individual water molecules will constantly perturb and change the structure
of the peptide. Both when it is alone and when it is binding a phosphate anion. The
phosphate binding is expected to be governed by hydrogen bonds. In a hydrogen bond
the original molecular orbitals becomes distorted (polarized), and the electronic structure
of the peptide is rearranged in such a manner, that the interaction with the anion
becomes favourable.
This kind of interaction requires a description of the electrons
in detail and can only be described by quantum mechanics, using electronic structure
11
==PAGE:26==
Chapter 2. Theory
12
calculations. In order to model the peptide properly, on one hand one needs an explicit
description of the environment of the peptide; how water molecules at room-temperature
changes the structure of it. Such description requires thousands of water molecules,
which only is attainable through MD. On the other hand, the peptide is expected to
be highly polarized in the interaction with a negative anion. This interaction is only
accurately described by explicitly calculating the electronic structure of the peptide-
anion complex. Thus the best of both tools are needed.
The project supervisor Mathias Felix Gruber has performed an exhaustive MD analysis
of both the peptide and of the peptide-anion complex [11]. From his results, an overview
of many conformations of the structures are obtained. These MD ”snapshots” provide
a good starting point for electronic structure calculations, and adequately span the con-
formational space of the system. As the work of this thesis employs electronic structure
calculations of the phosphate-binding peptide, it is in a way a natural extension of the
MD analysis.
Hopefully, it may serve as a veriﬁcation of the more coarse empirical
methods of MD. See sec. 3.6 for an introduction to MD and a discussion of MD as a
technique for conformational sampling.
This chapter will introduce the theory of electronic structure calculations. Several good
text-book references to the theory are: Jensen [17], Atkins and Friedman [14] and Ko-
hanoﬀ[18].
In order to solve the Schr¨odinger equation, an essential simpliﬁcation, the Born–Oppenheimer
(BO) approximation, must be introduced. The BO approximation justiﬁes the neglect
of the coupling between the nuclei and electron motion. The electronic energy can then
be solved with the nuclear positions as parameters, which results in a potential energy
surface (PES) that the nuclei move according to. The major computational eﬀort is in
solving the electronic Schr¨odinger equation for a given set of nuclear coordinates, and
this is called an electronic structure calculation [17].
The theory of electron structure calculations traditionally takes starting point in Hartree
Fock (HF) theory (introduced around 1930 [19]) in the chemistry society. The HF theory
is an ab initio (ﬁrst principles) method that introduce several approximations to the
Schr¨odinger equation and enables the calculation of the wavefunctions of the system.
==PAGE:27==
Chapter 2. Theory
13
However, a theory from the 1960s, that was traditionally favoured by physicists [20],
called density functional theory (DFT), has in the 21st century become the most well-
suited theory for electronic structure calculations on large molecules [21]. When system
size is so limiting in electronic structure methods, it is because of the scaling properties
of the theories. DFT methods scale favourably [21] with system size compared to HF
that scales with N4 [22] (N being the number of electrons). Besides, DFT includes
electron correlation energy, which is an energy contribution that HF neglects.
DFT
calculations can cope with system sizes as large as 100 atoms and frequently yields results
that agree more closely with experiments than HF calculations [14]. It can be used to
calculate molecular properties such as, equilibrium geometry, bond lengths and angles,
ground-state vibrational frequencies, electron aﬃnities, ionization potentials, interaction
energies, conformational energies, and reaction barrier heights [21]. According to Riley
et al. [21], DFT is the method of choice for the ”average” problem to achieve a particular
level of accuracy at lowest cost. In this work, DFT calculations will be used to optimize
peptide geometries and for energy calculations in order to predict binding energies.
It is out of scope to write an exhaustive review of the theory of electronic structure
calculation. Therefore this chapter will mainly serve to establish the notation and to
present and discuss the essential results and physics. First the theoretical framework is
presented, starting from the full Schr¨odinger equation. Then, the theory of Hartree Fock
is presented, which is a part of the general subject of wavefunction based methods. The
HF theory is indirectly employed in the work of this thesis. DFT methods fall into many
categories of which functional they employ, and in this work, the B3LYPfunctional will
be used. This functional works by a mixture of HF and DFT energy, and therefore
the HF theory is introduced. The last part of the theory section discuss the practical
implementation of DFT and the simulation software used (Gaussian 09 rev. D [13])
2.1
The Framework of Quantum Mechanics
In principle (neglecting relativistic eﬀects), all properties of a collection of electrons and
nuclei, interacting through Coulombic forces, are governed by a Hamiltonian, H, in the
general form:

The Hamiltonian takes into account the kinetic energies of the nuclei and the electrons,
the electron-nucleus attractive potential energy, the electron-electron and the nucleus-
nucleus repulsive potential energies. The sums running over i and j, and I and J excludes
the self-interaction terms i = j and I = J. Notice that any energy terms related to
the spin or the magnetic moment of particles are omitted because the non-relativistic
molecular Hamiltonian does not depend on spin-like variables, and no magnetic ﬁeld is
assumed to be present [19].
A natural simpliﬁed notation of the Hamiltonian is
H = TN + Te + VNN + Vee + +VeN
(2.2)
where TN and Te are the kinetic energies of the nuclei and the electrons respectively,
VNN and Vee are the nuclear-nuclear and electron-electron repulsion terms respectively
and VeN is the electron-nuclear attraction potential.
The properties, in particular the energy, of the system is found by solving the time-
independent Schr¨odinger equation:
HΨn(R, r) = EnΨn(R, r),
(2.3)
==PAGE:29==
Chapter 2. Theory
15
where En are the energy eigenvalues and Ψn(R, r) are the corresponding many-body
wavefunctions, which according to the Pauli principle must be antisymmetric with re-
spect to exchange of electron coordinates r.
Many atomic and chemical properties can be extracted from the solution to eqn. (2.3).
The binding energy of a complex is calculated from the ground state energy. In practice
however, this equation pose a formidable problem, since the full Schr¨odinger equation
requires us to handle 3(P + N) coupled degrees of freedom, which is beyond what is
computationally feasible.
Exact analytical solutions are limited to a very few cases,
such as the hydrogen atom. Even numerically, exact solutions are limited to very small
molecules. The diﬃculty of the equation stems from the fact that it is a multi-component
many-body system, interacting through the two-body Coulomb interaction. Thus the
choice of action is to resort to reasonable approximations, and the theoretical treatment
of this is the subject of the following sections.
2.1.1
The Born-Oppenheimer Approximation and the Classical Nuclei
Approximation
A solid or molecule consists of electrons and nuclei in mutual interaction, of which the
dynamics in general cannot be considered separately. This constitutes a computational
problem of massive scale. However the Born-Oppenheimer (BO) approximation allows
us to separate electronic and nuclear coordinates and consider the nuclei as a source of
static potential in a ﬁxed conﬁguration. The argument is elaborate, and is well described
in Kohanoﬀ[18]. The foundation is the fact that even the smallest nuclei, a proton, is
103 times heavier than the electrons. Under a classical scheme, we would say that the
timescales involving the electrons would be much shorter (higher velocity) than those of
the nuclei, and therefore the electrons would instantly adjust accordingly to the motion
of the nuclei. The BO is very reliable for ground electronic states, but less for excited
states [14].
A particular simple approach to the formulation to the BO approximation is found
in Atkins and Friedman [14], where the hydrogen molecule-ion is considered.
Even
for this, the simplest of all molecules, the full Schr¨odinger equation cannot be solved
analytically because it consists of three particles. By use of the BO approximation, the
nuclei particles are regarded as ﬁxed, and then the Schr¨odinger equation is solved for
==PAGE:30==
Chapter 2. Theory
16
the electrons in the static potential from the nuclei. One can solve the equations for
diﬀerent arrangements of the nuclei. From the set of solutions obtained, a potential
energy curve of the diatomic molecule can be constructed. This shows the energy of
the molecule as function of the inter-nuclear distance. For larger molecules with more
internal coordinates, this becomes a potential energy surface (PES), and the global
minimum of the PES identiﬁes the equilibrium conformation of the molecule.
Now we return to the hydrogen molecule-ion, we conﬁne all motion to the z-axis and
the full Hamiltonian, H, of for the problem is

V (z, Z1, Z2) is the potential of the system, including (but not limited to) nuclear-nuclear
repulsion, electron-electron repulsion and nuclear-electron attraction. Put more simply
H = Te + TN + V,
(2.5)
for the kinetic energy of the electron, the nuclei and the potential of the system, respec-
tively. The Schr¨odinger equation is
HΨ(z, Z1, Z2) = EΨ(z, Z1, Z2),

and in the spirit of the BO approximation, we attempt a solution ansatz of the form

(2.9)
The term W is non-zero and arise because ψ depends on the nuclear coordinates. Thus ψ
is not separable from the nuclear kinetic energy operator and W contains all the diﬃculty
of the equations. However, as the nuclei masses are in the denominator W, it is assumed
to be small and consequently neglected. This is called the adiabatic approximation [18].
We now seek the solution of
χTeψ + ψTNχ + V ψχ = ψTNχ + (Teψ + V ψ)χ = Eψχ.
(2.10)
In order to solve this equation, we introduce
Teψ + V ψ = Ee(Z1, Z2)ψ
(2.11)
for ﬁxed values of nuclear coordinates. The solution of this equation is the electronic
wavefunction ψ, and we deﬁne the eigenvalue Ee(Z1, Z2) as the electronic contribution
to the total energy plus the potential energy contribution at the preselected nuclear
arrangement. When plotted against nuclear coordinates, Ee(Z1, Z2) gives the potential
energy curve, see ﬁg. 2.1.
Now we substitute eqn. (2.11) into eqn. (2.10) and obtain
ψTNχ + Eeψχ = Eψχ ⇒TNχ + Eeχ = Eχ,
(2.12)
where ψ is cancelled in the step. Eqn. (2.12) is the Schr¨odinger equation for the nuclei
wavefunction χ, and it is evident that Ee(Z1, Z2) is the potential energy curve that the
nuclei move on. E is the total energy of the molecule within the BO-approximation.
This is the essence of the approximation. However we went very lightly over the step
where W was neglected, eqn. (2.10). In the original work [24], a more thorough calcula-
tion with pertubative consideration regarding the position coordinates of the nuclei was
performed. If W could not be neglected, the time-evolution of the system would involve
mixing of the electronic states. However the analysis showed no mixing. Therefore this
approximation is formally sound and this is also the reason why it is called the adiabatic
==PAGE:32==
Chapter 2. Theory
18
Figure 2.1: Potential energy curve of a diatomic molecule. The minimum determines
the equilibrium bond length. From [23].
approximation. Of course the BO approximation can only be applied under appropri-
ate conditions. If for instance the nuclear rotational energy become comparable to the
spacing of the electronic energy levels, the nuclear and electronic states may couple, and
exotic phenomena arise.
The example above was in one dimension. A three-dimensional analogy with P nuclear
particles, is now presented with an overview of the deeper quantum mechanical consid-
erations, involved in the approximation. We seek an equation of motion (EOM) for the
nuclear particles, and the presented results are from [18].
Having obtained the PES, Ee(R), the total energy including the kinetic energy of the
nuclei can be computed
[Tn + Ee(R)] Φ = EΦ,
(2.13)
where E is the total energy of the system or molecule, Φ is the total nuclear wavefunction
and R is the coordinates of the P nuclear particles. By considering the nuclei as classical
particles, an EOM can be derived. The derivation relies on the Ehrenfest theorem that
connects the time derivative of the expectation values of the position and momentum
operators, to the expectation value of the force, F = −∇Ee(R). However, the collection
of nuclei is a quantum object with a wavefunction associated to it, so we cannot simply
decide to treat the nuclei as classical. But by considering the thermal wavelenght at
==PAGE:33==
Chapter 2. Theory
19
room temperature, λT = (ℏ2/2MkBT)1/2, it turns out that in the worst case scenario of
hydrogen, λ is of the order 0.2 ˚A. This justiﬁes treating the nuclei as point-like charges.
The gradient of the electronic energy ∇Ee(R) determines the force on and motion of
the nuclei. It can be expressed as the expectation value of the gradient of the electronic
Hamiltonian, by virtue of the Hellmann-Feynman theorem. The EOM for the classical
nuclei can then be expressed as [18]:

Solving the stationary problem, ∇Ee(R) = 0, or ﬁnding the

sively in the work of this project. In order to obtain Ee(R), it is necessary to solve the
time-independent electronic Schr¨odinger equation, (2.11). This is called an electronic
structure calculation, and it is the subject of the following sections.
2.2
The Electronic Structure Problem - Wavefunction Meth-
ods
The Born-Oppenheimer approximation rearranged the full Schr¨odinger equation to an
electronic and a nuclear Hamiltonian. The nuclear particles can be described as classical
particles that move according to the PES of system, governed by the electronic Hamil-
tonian. Thus the focus from now on will be on the solution to the electronic Schr¨odinger
equation
Hψ(r, R) = E(R)ψ(r; R)
(2.17)
==PAGE:34==
Chapter 2. Theory
20
where the positions of the nuclei R are ﬁxed, and the electronic wavefucntion ψ depends
on the electronic coordinates r parametrically on R. E(R) is the electronic energy. The
electronic Hamiltonian is deﬁned in eqn. (2.15) and from now on it is denoted H. The
nuclear-nuclear repulsion term is excluded from the electronic Hamiltonian, but it still
remains included in the PES.
One can adopt several approaches to solving the electronic Schr¨odinger equation (2.17),
including ab initio wavefunction based methods, density functional theory and semi-
empirical methods. This section presents the ab initio theory of Hartree-Fock, which
attacks the problem by approximation of the wavefunction, and as mentioned in the
introduction to this chapter, is incorporated in the density functional B3LYP (see sec.
2.3.5.3). In ab initio (ﬁrst principles) wavefunction based methods, an approximation or
model is chosen for the electronic wavefunction, and the wavefunction is solved using only
the values of the fundamental constants, and the atomic numbers of the nuclei [14]. The
accuracy of the method is determined by the accuracy of the model wavefunction. Thus
by systematically increasing the accuracy of the wavefunction, the solution approaches
the real solution to the electronic Schr¨odinger equation (within the BO approximation).
The wavefunction methods, listed in increasing accuracy, are: Hartree theory (app. B),
Hartree-Fock (HF) theory (sec. 2.2.1), Post Hartree-Fock methods (sec. 2.2.2). The
latter including: Conﬁguration interaction (CI) methods, Møller-Plesset (MP) many-
body perturbation theory and the coupled-cluster (CC) method.
2.2.1
Hartree-Fock Theory
In Hartree-Fock (HF) theory, the many-electron ground-state of a system of N inter-
acting electrons is described by a single so-called determinantal state of spin-orbitals.
A spin-orbital is a wavefunction containing both spatial and spin components, and the
best possible choice of spin-orbitals is found by minimizing the energy, using the Euler-
Lagrange variational principle. This will lead to a set of integro-diﬀerential equations
that must be solved in a self-consistent manner to obtain the optimized spin-orbitals.
Thus in the HF formalism, the lowest energy is found within the restriction of the wave-
function being a single Slater determinant. An insightful review of the HF theory can
be found in Echenique and Alonso [19].
==PAGE:35==
Chapter 2. Theory
21
Consider a complete set of orthonormal spin-orbitals {ψi(rσ)} composed of a complete
set of orthonormal one-electron spatial orbitals {φi(r)} and the spin functions χi(σ)
(either spin up, α, or down, β). That is ψi(rσ) = φi(r)χi(σ). A shorthand notation
will be used where the arguments are suppressed. In Hartree theory (see app. B), the
electronic wavefunction is assumed to have the form of a simple product wavefunction
Ψ0(r1σ1, r2σ2, ..., rNσN) = ψ1(r1σ1)ψ2(r2σ2)...ψN(rNσN).
(2.18)
Hartree theory was of great historical importance, but regarding quantitative results it
is not too useful. The wavefunction does not inherently satisfy the Pauli principle and
does not posses the correct symmetry.
In the HF model, each electron is described by an orbital, and the total wave function
is given as a product of orbitals.
However, since electrons are fermion, the overall
wavefunction of the of N electrons must be antisymmetric under exchange of particles,
which is achieved in the simplest form by arranging the orbitals in a Slater determinant
[17].
Ψ0(r1σ1, r2σ2, ..., rNσN) =

In Hartree theory, only the classical (Coulombic) electrostatic

compared to Hartree theory, the exchange contribution lowers the energy. This reﬂects
the fact, that a determinantal wavefunction is a better approximation to the real many-
body wavefunction. The exchange energy causes electrons with parallel spins to be kept
apart in real space [25].

The Fock operator is deﬁned in terms of the spin-orbitals, which in turn is deﬁned by
the Fock operator (2.30-2.32). Thus the spin-orbitals, {ψi}, must be determined with a
self-consistent iteration method:
1. The Coulomb and exchange potentials are estimated from a reasonable initial guess
of the spin-orbitals ψ1, ψ2, ...ψN.
2. A new set of wavefunctions are found from the solution to (2.34), from which new
Coulomb and exchange potentials are found.
3. And so a cycle of iterations continues until the the input wavefunctions matches
the output.
Within the approximation of a single-determinantal wavefunction, the HF theory reduce
the full many-electron problem to a feasible one-particle problem. However, HF neglects
an energy contribution called correlation, which is deﬁned below. We can recover the

2.2.1.1
Restricted and Unrestricted HF-Theory
So far we have not considered spin of the ground-state wavefunction. In closed-shell
states (with an even number of electrons), it is customary to assume that the spatial
components of the spinorbitals are identical for each member of an electron pair (with
opposite spins) [14]. There are 1
2N spatial orbitals φi(ri) and the HF spatial wavefunc-
tion is
Φ0 =

(2.37)
where φs

restricted Hartree-Fock (RHF) wavefunction. The eqns. (2.30-2.34) are converted to
their spatial analogue by integrating over the spin functions and using the orthonormality
of opposite spins [14].
There are two procedures to describe open-shell states of atoms (systems with un-paired
spin states) [14].
In the restricted open-shell formalism all electrons are forced

2.2.2
Beyond Hartree-Fock Theory
The Hartree-Fock theory is an important achievement in computational physics and
chemistry, and is still extensively employed. However, due to the fact that even the best
single determinantal state, is a crude approximation of the true many-body ground-state
wavefunction, corrections beyond HF have to be considered. As the only constraint in
HF was the form of the wavefunction, many natural extensions to the theory has been
made. In this way HF model is a branching point, see ﬁg. 2.2, where either additional
approximations can be introduced which leads to semi-empirical methods, or where the
HF wavefunction can be improved by adding additional determinants [17]. Here we will
brieﬂy introduce one of the more accurate methods, as they are often used to asses or
benchmark the performance of DFT methods.
HF theory only accounts for the average electron–electron interactions, and consequently
neglects the correlation between electrons. Correlation stems from the fact that ﬁnding
an electron at r depends on the location of the Z −1 other electrons, and in most cases
it is responsible to about 1 % of the total energy. In order to include electron corre-
lation, a multi-determinant wavefunction is required. Multi-determinant methods are
computationally much more heavy than the HF model, but can generate results that sys-
tematically approach the exact solution of the Schr¨odinger equation [17]. The methods
include conﬁguration interaction (CI), Coupled Clusters (CC) and second-order
Møller-Plesset (MP2) perturbation theory. For a summary of the methods see Atkins
and Friedman [14], for a review see Kohanoﬀ[18] and for a more detailed introduction
see Jensen [17].
==PAGE:41==
Chapter 2. Theory
27
Figure 2.2: The HF model is the basic method for electronic structure calculations.
More approximate or more accurate treatments are extensions of the HF theory. From
Jensen [17].
Conﬁguration Interaction
(CI) describes a method where a linear combination of
Slater determinants, are used to variationally solve the Schr¨odinger equation. CI in-
cludes correlation, but is limited to small systems as it is computationally heavy. The
wavefunction is a linear combination of conﬁguration state functions (CSFs) built from
spin orbitals [14]
Ψ = C0Φ0 +
X
a,p
Cp
aΦp
a +
X
a<b
p<q
Cpq
abΦpq
ab +
X
a<b<c
p<q<r
Cpqr
abc Φpqr
abc + ...,
(2.38)
where Φ0 is the HF ground-state wavefunction build from the spin-orbitals ψi, and Ci
are the variationally optimized expansion coeﬃcients. Φp
a is a singly excited determinant
from which an occupied spin-orbital ψa has been promoted to a virtual spin-orbital ψp. A
virtual orbital is an orbital lying higher than the N/2 lowest ones, which are occupied in
the HF ground-state. Φpq
ab is a doubly excited determinant from which two electrons has
been promoted. These excited determinants are illustrated in ﬁg. 2.3. The limits in the
summation indices (a < b) ensure that we sum over unique pairs. Each determinant,
or a linear combination of a few of them, that are constructed to posses the correct
electronic symmetry is called a CSF [14]. A CSF is an eigenfunction of all operators
that commute with the electronic Hamiltonian.
Truncating the CI-space is an important way to save computation time. For example,
the method CID is limited to double excitations only. The method CISD is limited to
single and double excitations.

2.3
Density Functional Theory
The sections above described the ab initio methods for solving the electronic Schr¨odinger
equation. The emphasis was on HF theory, and a brief introduction to post-HF methods
was also included.
Common to all these methods are that the spin-orbitals of the
wavefunctions are sought to solve the Schr¨odinger equation and obtain the energy and
other molecular properties of interest.
However, the accuracy of these methods are
limited by the computational resources available [26]. While the HF theory can treat
systems of a 100 or more atoms, it lacks a description of the electron correlation energy.
The post-HF methods are so computational expensive that they are only suitable for
small molecules [14]. An alternative to these methods is the density functional theory
(DFT). In contrast to the wavefunction-based methods above, it takes starting point in
the electron density. DFT has the capability of taking electron correlation into account,
while having favourable scaling properties over HF [21]. The scaling properties of interest
is how the computation time and memory consumption increases with system size. Thus
DFT can handle large systems and agree more closely with experiments than HF [14].
In this study, we wish to describe the interaction between a phosphate anion and a
hexapeptide. The binding is expected to be non-covalent and governed by hydrogen
==PAGE:43==
Chapter 2. Theory
29
bonds. Hydrogen bonding is an interaction described by the correlation energy of elec-
trons, which is not captured by HF [27], and as we are considering a hexapeptide,
post-HF methods are too expensive to be feasible. However, according to [27], certain
DFT methods can attain hydrogen bonding results within an accuracy of 1 kcal/mol,
which is comparable to the MP2 level. DFT has been reported suitable for studies of
large biological structures, even proteins [21], due to its favourable scaling properties
and inclusion of electron correlation. Thus, DFT is most well-suited ab initio technique.
However, the DFT method that will be employed in this work does not include dis-
persion energy. This constraint is responsible for some discrepancies between DFT and
post-HF methods.
The foundation of DFT is that the ground-state energy of a system can be written
in terms of the electron density n(r), which is proven in the Hohenberg-Kohn theorem.
Thus the energy is a functional of the electron density, E[n(r)]. An excellent review of the
theory and the history, obstacles and achievements in electronic structure calculations
can be found in the Nobel prize lecture of Kohn [26], from which this theory section also
draw many points. For an informal review of DFT, see [28].
2.3.1
The Hohenberg-Kohn Theorem
Since early in the 1920’s the ground-state energy had been related to the electron density
through Thomas-Fermi theory. However, a strict connection between the two was not
proven until 1964 by the Hohenberg-Kohn theorem [26, 29]:
The ground-state density n(r) of a bound system of interacting electrons in
some external potential v(r) determines this external potential uniquely.
The theorem is illustrated in ﬁg. 2.4 and the rest of this section presents the proof
of the theorem. The proof of the theorem will bring understanding and appreciation
for DFT. It will be proved for a nondegenerate ground-state, however this is only for
the sake of simplicity, and the requirement can be lifted. In this proof and the sections
following it, a change of notation will occur for the terms in the electronic Hamiltonian
H. The electronic kinetic energy operator will now be denoted T, the electron-electron
interaction operator will now be denoted U and external potentials (usually from the
electrostatic attraction of the nuclei) will now be denoted by the operator V .
==PAGE:44==
Chapter 2. Theory
30
Figure 2.4: Illustration of the Hohenberg-Kohn theorem. The left box illustrates the
space of external potentials and the box to the right represents the space of electron
densities. The arrows indicate that there is a one-to-one correspondence between a
speciﬁc external potential and a speciﬁc density.
Let n(r) be the ground-state density of N electrons in an external potential v1(r),
corresponding to the nondegenerate ground-state Ψ1 = Ψ1(r) and the energy E1. We
have the relation:
E1 = ⟨Ψ1|H1|Ψ1⟩= ⟨Ψ1|T + U + V |Ψ1⟩=
Z
dr v1(r)n(r) + ⟨Ψ1|T + U|Ψ1⟩,
(2.39)
where we have used that
⟨Ψ1|V |Ψ1⟩=
Z
Ψ∗
1(r, r2..., rN)v1(r)Ψ1(r, r2..., rN)drdr2...drN
(2.40)

(2.41)
Consider now a second potential v2 that is not equal to v1 + a constant. To v2 we
assume there exists a ground-state (that could be degenerate) Ψ2 ̸= exp (iθ)Ψ1, giving
rise to the same density n(r). The energy associated with Ψ2 is:
E2 = ⟨Ψ2|H2|Ψ2⟩=
Z
dr v2(r)n(r) + ⟨Ψ2|T + U|Ψ2⟩.
(2.42)
From variational theory, the Rayleigh-Ritz approximation gives the inequality:
E1 < ⟨Ψ2|H1|Ψ2⟩=
Z
dr v1(r)n(r) + ⟨Ψ2|T + U|Ψ2⟩= E2 +
Z

(2.43)
and
E2 ≤⟨Ψ1|H2|Ψ1⟩= E1 +
Z

(2.44)
==PAGE:45==
Chapter 2. Theory
31
Adding Eqn. (2.43) and (2.44) gives the contradiction:
E1 + E2 < E1 + E2.
(2.45)
Thus we can conclude that the second potential v2 giving rise to the same density n(r) as
the ﬁrst potential v1 cannot exist, and that the ground-state density therefore uniquely
deﬁnes the external potential v. The density determines the number of electrons N and
the external potential, and therefore the full hamiltonian for the electronic system. Hence
n(r) implicitly determines all the properties derivable from H through the solution of
either the time-dependent or time-independent Schr¨odinger equation. One can of course
ask the question, of whether any well-behaved positive function n(r) that integrates to a
positive integer N, is a possible ground-state density corresponding to some v(r). Such
a density is called v-representable (VR). While mathematically there do exist densities
that are not VR, for all practical reasons this has not been in issue or appeared as a
limitation to the application of DFT [26].
2.3.2
The Hohenberg-Kohn Variational Principle
The HF approach to calculating the ground-state energy was to use variational theory,
with respect to the single-particle spin-orbitals, to ﬁnd the minimum in energy;
E = min˜Ψ⟨˜Ψ|H|˜Ψ⟩,
(2.46)
where ˜Ψ is a trial wavefunction (a single determinantal wavefunction) for the N electrons.
In DFT, the focus shift from the ground-state wavefunction, to the ground-state density,
and accordingly a formulation of the minimal principle in terms of trial densities ˜n(r),
rather than trial wavefunctions ˜Ψ are presented [26]. The idea was ﬁrst published by
Hohenberg and Kohn in 1964 [29], however the derivation has been reﬁned to the so-
called Levy and Lieb constrained search method [30]. As every trial wavefunction, ˜Ψ,
corresponds to a trial density, ˜n(r) we can carry out the minimization in eqn. (2.46) in
two steps that follows the layout of [26]. First ﬁx a trial density, ˜n(r). One can imagine
that many diﬀerent wavefunctions would correspond to a speciﬁc density, and this class
of wavefunctions are denoted by ˜Ψα
˜n. The energy minimum of a system subjected to a
==PAGE:46==
Chapter 2. Theory
32
potential v(r), constrained to having the trial density ˜n(r) ﬁxed, is now deﬁned as
Ev[˜n(r)] ≡minα⟨˜Ψα
˜n|H|˜Ψα
˜n⟩=
Z
v(r)˜n(r)dr + F [˜n(r)] ,
(2.47)
where
F [˜n(r)] = minα⟨˜Ψα
˜n(r)|T + U|˜Ψα
˜n(r)⟩.
(2.48)
The functional F [˜n(r)] minimizes the energy with respect to the class of wavefunctions,
α, that corresponds to the density ˜n(r). It contains no reference to v(r) and is therefore
a universal functional of the density. The second step is to minimize the energy in eqn.
(2.47) with respect to all trial densities.
E = min˜n(r)Ev [˜n(r)] = min˜n(r)
Z
v(r)˜n(r)dr + F [˜n(r)] .
(2.49)
The ground-state energy of the system corresponding to v(r) is obtained in eqn. (2.49)
when the trial density ˜n(r) is the ground-state density.
In the case of a degenerate
ground-state, ˜n(r) can be any of the corresponding ground-state densities.
The problem of ﬁnding the ground-state energy, that is the minimum of ⟨˜Ψ|H|˜Ψ⟩, for
a wavefunction of 3N dimensions has formally been reduced to ﬁnding a minimum of
Ev[˜n(r)], with regards to the 3-dimensional density. Of course the same 3N-dimensional
problem still remains in F[˜n(r)], that depends on the wavefunctions. The impact of
the result of the Hohenberg-Kohn (H-K) variational principle lies in the rigorous refor-
mulation of the problem, namely that the ground-state can be described solely by a
density distribution, ˜n(r), and of a well-deﬁned, yet unknown, functional of the density,
F[˜n(r)]. A functional that represents the sum of the kinetic energy and the energy of
the electron-electron interaction, (T + U). It is however diﬃcult to ﬁnd a proper func-
tional form of F[˜n(r)]. Especially the representation of the kinetic energy T has proven
diﬃcult. This deﬁciency is largely improved in the so-called self-consistent Kohn-Sham
(KS) equations [26], discussed in the next section.
2.3.3
The Self-Consistent Kohn-Sham Equations
The Kohn-Sham equations are a set of self-consistent equations that draws on the con-
cept of non-interacting electrons, also considered in Hartree theory (app. B). These
==PAGE:47==
Chapter 2. Theory
33
equations give a speciﬁc iterative computational method to ﬁnd the ground-state, and
guides the approximation of the unknown functional F[˜n(r)].
The Hartree eqns. (B.8-B.10) give a set of single-particle equations to approximate the
ground-state electronic structure of a system. The Hartree equation, (B.9), has the form
of a Schr¨odinger equation for noninteracting electrons moving in an eﬀective external
potential VH. If we treat a system of noninteracting electrons within the DFT formalism
and compare to the case of interacting electrons, we can learn a great deal of how to
approximate F[˜n(r)]. For noninteracting electrons, the H-K variational principle in eqn.
(2.49), takes the form [26]

Z

with density distribution ˜n(r). The technique of Lagrangian multipliers is employed, as
in HF theory (sec. 2.2.1), while using that the ground-state energy is stationary with
respect to the trial density, and keeping the constraint that the number of electrons
is ﬁxed. Now considering the change in the Lagrangian functional with respect to the
density, we get [26]

presented in app. C.

These self-consistent single-particle equations are called the Kohn-Sham (KS) equations
[26], and the singe-particle wavefunctions are called the KS wavefunctions.
The KS
equations must be solved in a self-consistent manner. The usual approach is ﬁrst to
provide a good initial guess of the density distribution n(r), from which the eﬀective
potential veff is constructed. Then the single-particle orbitals are calculated from the
KS single-particle equation, and used to construct a new density distribution. This circle
continues to iterate until the input density is equal to the output density, see ﬁg. 2.5.

(2.63)
So far DFT has been an exact theory, without any approximations (except for the BO-
approximation). All the many-body eﬀects of the electrons have simply been separated
and included in Exc and vxc. The strength of the theory lies in the fact that we now
know what to approximate, namely Exc and vxc, and thus any diﬃculty in calculating
ground-state properties has been conﬁned to ﬁnding a reasonable approximation to Exc.
The usefulness of DFT depends on ﬁnding approximations that are both accurate and
simple (fast to calculate).
A useful view of the theory can extracted by comparing to the H-K theorem (sec. 2.3.1).
Consider a system of interacting electrons in external potential v(r) with ground-state
density n(r). We can now think of a system of non-interacting electrons, having the
same density n0(r) = n(r). As the H-K theorem applies to any system, interacting or
not, there will exist a unique corresponding external potential generating the density
n0.
This potential is exactly the eﬀective potential, veff(r), from the KS equations
above.
Thus the H-K theorem ensures us that for any real interacting system, we
can always imagine a system of non-interacting electrons whose ground-state density
equals the ground-state density of the interacting system.
And therefore the single-
particle wavefunctions, {ψj}, of the non-interacting system, which are simple to ﬁnd,
will generate the ground-state density, n0(r) = P
j ψ∗
j (r)ψj(r), that are equal to that of
the interacting system, n(r). Although the ground-state single-particle wavefunctions
of the non-interacting system will not correspond to those of the interacting system. In
the same manner, the single-particle energies, {ϵj} of eqn. (2.61) remain interpreted
as Lagrangian multipliers without physical meaning. Except that the magnitude of the
highest occupied ϵj, relative to vacuum, equals the ionization energy [26].

So far we have considered restricted DFT, where the ground-state density was assumed
to be a closed-shell system in a singlet state. DFT can however be unrestricted and

DFT is to calculate the spin-densities using the KS wavefunctions and the methods are

2.3.5
Approximations to the Exchange-Correlation Functional
The formal achievements so far would have no practical use, if not a workable approx-
imation to the exchange correlation energy could be found. There are several diﬀerent
approaches to determine the form of the functional Exc, and one class of functionals, the
hybrid-GGA’s (Generalized Gradient Approximation), are largely considered the best
choice for hydrogen-bonded systems [21]. This class of functional relies on a mixture of
exact exchange from HF-theory and exchange-correlation from GGA functionals, which
in turn is a generalization of the Local Density Approximation (LDA) functional. The
hybrid-GGA’s in general perform very well for many organic molecules [21] , although
they exclude dispersion [26], i.e. van-der-waals forces, which become increasingly im-
portant in large bio-molecules [21]. As the hybrid-GGA functionals are extensions of
the LDA functionals, this section will introduce the LDA, the GGA and ﬁnally the
hybrid-GGA.
2.3.5.1
The Local Density Approximation
The historically most popular (in the physics community) and simple exchange-correlation
(xc)-functional is the local density approximation (LDA). It takes starting point in the
homogeneous electron gas, which is a system where the energy (and the xc-energy) can
be calculated exact [18]. In the LDA, the xc-energy density for the system under consid-
eration is then approximated at each point in space, as the xc-energy of a homogeneous
electron gas of the same density as in that point. Therefore it is performing best in
systems with reasonably slowly varying spatial density that locally resemble the homo-
geneous electron gas, which is, in general, not the case in organic molecules. In the LDA,
the xc-energy, deﬁned in eqn. (2.56), takes the form [26]
ELDA
xc
[n(r)] =
Z
ϵxc(n(r))n(r)dr,
(2.65)
where ϵxc(n(r)) is the xc-energy per electron of a homogeneous gas of interacting elec-
trons of density n(r). Diﬀerent forms of ϵxc(n(r)) has been reported, and one expression
==PAGE:53==
Chapter 2. Theory
39
is given by J.P Perdew and A. Zunger in 1981 [31], who parametrized numerical results
from Ceperley and Alder [32], obtained with Monte Carlo calculations.
ϵxc(rs) = ϵx(rs) + ϵc(rs)
ϵx(rs) = −0.4582
rs

The LDA has been the basis for the success of density functional theory.
It works
particular well in solids state calculations, where the slowly varying density is a fair
approximation for electrons delocalized through the solid [26]. However, the weak inter-
molecular bonds (hydrogen bonds) that are crucial in biological systems, are poorly
reproduced. This is because the density is small and dominated by inhomogeneities in
the bonding region [18]. Thus more sophisticated functionals are needed.
2.3.5.2
The Generalized Gradient Approximation
The LDA was a tremendous success to DFT, but many systems are out of reach as the
approximation of treating systems as locally homogeneous is rather crude. Naturally
all real systems have spatially varying electric ﬁelds and densities due to the nuclei and
11 Hartree = 27.2 eV = 627.5 kcal/mol which is twice the ionization energy of a hydrogen atom.
==PAGE:54==
Chapter 2. Theory
40
the screening of the electrons. A natural expansion of the LDA would be not only to
consider the density in each point, but also to consider the gradient of the density. An
xc-energy of the form
EGGA
xc
=
Z
exc(n(r), |∇n(r)|)n(r)dr,
(2.68)
is called the generalized gradient approximation (GGA) [18]. The derivation is rather
cumbersome and involves several concepts that are out of scope of this report to intro-
duce. For an easy-to-read account of the theoretical derivation of the gradient expansion
and the generalized gradient approximation, see [26]. App. D sums up the most impor-
tant aspects of the derivation. Much work has been put into deriving successful GGA’s,
and an important contribution comes from A. Becke (1988)[33]. He introduced numerical
ﬁtting parameters to an exchange functional, and ﬁtted the parameters to experimental
molecular data. This work was complemented, also in 1988, by a correlation function by
Lee, Yang and Parr [34], which gave rise to the widely used BLYP functional. Another
popular functional is the PBE functional (Perdew, Burke and Ernzerhof, 1996) [35],
which was revised in 1999.
Compared to the LDA, the GGAs tend to improve binding energies and equilibrium
geometries, and in general they improve the description of hydrogen-bonded systems
[18].
2.3.5.3
Hybrid HF-KS Approaches
A combined approach to capture the exchange and correlation energy is the hybrid
method, introduced by A. Becke in 1993.
It was observed that the LDA and GGA
trends are in general opposite to those of HF, which motivated an approximation which
combined the two approaches. The method involves mixing DFT-exchange with HF-
exchange, where the exact HF-exchange is calculated from the KS-wavefunctions. The
hybrid xc-functional is deﬁned as
Ehyb
xc
= αEHF
x
+ (1 −α)EDFT
x
+ EDFT
c
,
(2.69)
where the coeﬃcient α is either chosen to assume a speciﬁc value or ﬁtted to molecular
properties. Although the hybrid schemes actually have rigorous formal justiﬁcation, the
==PAGE:55==
Chapter 2. Theory
41
approach of ﬁtting parameters to molecular data is not compatible with the ab initio
approach.
Many diﬀerent hybrid functionals are available, amongst the most popular are PBE0,
HSE and B3LYP, where the latter is the most commonly used in the organic compu-
tational chemistry community [36–39]. The hybrid scheme improves the prediction of
many molecular properties, such as atomization energies, bond lengths and vibration
frequencies [18, 21].
B3LYP
(Becke, 3-parameter, Lee-Yang-Parr) [40] is one of the most popular hybrid
xc-functionals, and is deﬁned as
EB3LYP
xc
= ELDA
x
+ α0(EHF
x
−ELDA
x
) + αx(EGGA
x
−ELDA
x
) + ELDA
c
+ αc(EGGA
c
−ELDA
c
),
(2.70)
where α0 = 0.20, αx = 0.72 and αc = 0.81. The B3LYP parameters are chosen to
best ﬁt properties from a molecular database. It reproduces the geometries and binding
energies of small molecular systems, to the same accuracy of second order Møller-Plesset
pertubation theory (MP2), and sometimes even at a higher level such as coupled clusters
(CC) or conﬁguration interaction (CI) methods at a signiﬁcantly lower computational
cost [18, chapter 5]. Several studies conclude that for organic molecules, including pep-
tides, the B3LYP is overall a good choice for low-cost calculations[41, 42]. Others also
note that B3LYP has unsatisfactory performance regarding dispersion energy [36]. Dis-
persion forces are important in van der Wall bound complexes, and become increasingly
important with system size. In the interaction between a charged anion and a hexapep-
tide, the dispersion forces are not expected to signiﬁcantly contribute to the overall
binding conformation or energy. The merit of B3LYP is further discussed in sec. 3.2.
Now we have a satisfactory approximation to the xc-correlation functional. In principle
the theory of DFT is complete, and we can now calculate the ground-state electronic
density and energy of a system. From this we can obtain the PES and ﬁnd the equilib-
rium geometry. For our anion-peptide complex, we can ﬁnd the binding conformations,
and ﬁnd the equilibrium conformations of the constituent molecular species. We can
calculate the energy of the peptide, the anion and the complex, and with these energies
we can calculate the binding energy. However, so far we have not been paying atten-
tion to the representation of the KS wavefunctions. The wavefunctions are described
==PAGE:56==
Chapter 2. Theory
42
by basis functions and stored on a computer, and thus, the basis set will have to be
ﬁnite - in fact, severely ﬁnite. In practice, a major part of the electronic energy will
not be captured (on the order of a 100 kcal/mol) [17]. Fortunately the interest lies in
energy diﬀerences, and therefore the goal is to make the errors as constant as possible.
Thus we need eﬃcient ways to represent the KS wavefunctions, and a large part of a
computational study is to choose the level of approximation.
2.4
Practical Implementation
The full 3N-dimensional many-body problem in the Schr¨odinger equation, has in DFT
and HF theory been simpliﬁed, by approximations, into a tractable scheme.
In the
single-particle approximations of HF and KS-DFT, the central mathematical problem is
to solve a set of N coupled three-dimensional, partial diﬀerential equations. In the KS
formulation of DFT, the eigenvalue equation to solve is given in eqn. (2.61)

−1
2∇2 + veff(r) −ϵj

ψj(r) = 0.
Solving the KS equation requires a mathematical representation of the KS-orbitals. That
means a choice must be made for the basis set for the Hilbert space. One could choose to
represent the orbitals on a three-dimensional grid in real space. However, more eﬃcient
alternatives have been explored, and these can be separated into two types [18]:
Extended basis sets
are useful when investigating solids or liquids. The basis func-
tions are delocalized and cover all space. This is not necessary in molecular systems,
where this type of basis set tend to be ineﬃcient.
Localized basis sets
are mostly used for molecular systems, but can be employed
to periodic systems as well. They are usually localized around the center of the nuclei
positions, but may also be centred in bonds, or even ﬂoating, i.e.
centred at ﬁxed
positions instead of nuclei coordinates.
Basis sets also exists that mix or augments the two types mentioned above. A compre-
hensive description of each type of basis set is found in Kohanoﬀ[18, Chapter 6]. The
==PAGE:57==
Chapter 2. Theory
43
work in this thesis is focused on peptides, and thus molecular systems, which makes the
basis sets of interest to be the localized basis sets.
2.4.1
Localized Basis Sets
Solving the electronic structure problem and the KS equation in practice, requires a
mathematical representation of the one-electron KS-orbitals. The methods presented
are directly applicable to the solution of the HF equation, by simply substituting KS
with HF. Here the approach of atom-centred basis sets, linear combination of atom
orbitals, Slater-type orbitals and, in particular, Gaussian-type orbitals are presented.
We can expand the one-electron spatial KS-orbitals, φi, in a real-space representation
of a general basis set, {ϕj(r)}, and expand the spatial orbitals as a linear combination
of these basis orbitals
φi(r) =
M
X
j=1
cijϕj(r),
(2.71)
where i is the orbital quantum number, cij is the expansion coeﬃcients, and the sum
runs over all the basis functions up to the size of the basis set M. We can then formulate
the solution as a generalized linear eigenvalue problem. By inserting the basis function,
ϕk, in the eigenvalue energy solution to a single-particle Schr¨odinger-like equation Hφi =
ϵiφi we get
⟨ϕk|H|φi⟩−⟨ϕk|ϵi|φi⟩=
M

(Hkj −ϵiSkj)cij = 0,
(2.72)
or in matrix notation
Hc = ΛSc,
(2.73)
where Λ is the diagonal matrix of the eigenvalues, matrix c has the expansion coeﬃcients
of the wavefunctions (the eigenvectors), and H and S are the Hamiltonian and overlap
matrices, respectively. The matrix elements of the Hamiltonian and the overlap matrix
are given by
Hkj = ⟨ϕk|H|ϕj⟩,
Skj = ⟨ϕk| ϕj⟩.
(2.74)
The overlap matrix takes into account the possibility that the basis functions are not
orthogonal. In DFT, the Hamiltonian operator is the one-electron eﬀective Hamiltonian
of the KS-equation given above and in eqn. (2.61). As H in the KS equations includes

terms. These terms are called 2-electron 4-center integrals and are, computationally, the

The same matrix approach is valid for the HF theory, when the basis set is an atom-
centred basis set. The equations then assume the matrix form
Fc = ΛSc,
(2.75)
where F is the Fock operator, deﬁned in Eqn. (2.33). This is called the Roothaan-Hall
equation.
2.4.1.1
Atom-Centred Basis Sets and Atomic Orbitals
In quantum chemistry, the most common approach to represent the one-electron wave-
functions or molecular orbitals (MOs) is to expand them in terms of atomic orbitals
(AOs) [18]. The AOs are the solutions to the atomic problem (see below), however,
often what is called AOs are the actual basis functions used to expand the MOs. The
AO-basis functions are centred on the atomic nuclei and are designed partially to re-
semble actual AOs. The atom-centred basis sets pose the issue of completeness and
orthogonality; there is no reason why e.g. two s-orbitals on nearest neighbour atoms
should not overlap. However, the non-orthogonality can be handled, and the only sac-
riﬁce is that it is diﬃcult to systematically improve the basis set. This is because the
basis functions may become linearly dependent as the basis set becomes larger [18].
The atomic problem, i.e. ﬁnding the one-electron eigenstates in a hydrogen-like Coulomb
ﬁeld, starts by solving the Schr¨odinger equation in spherical coordinates
"
ℏ2
2m

(2.76)
==PAGE:59==

45
where ˆL is the angular momentum operator, and often veﬀ= −Z/r. In contrast to
earlier notation, here r denotes the radial coordinate and r denotes the 3 dimensional
spherical coordinates r = (r, θ, ϕ). The angular momentum operator is deﬁned as
ˆL2 = −

(2.77)
As the operators ˆL2 and ˆLz commute with the Hamiltonian in the atomic problem, the
wavefunctions (AOs) are separated in a radial and an angular part
φnlm(r) = χnl(r)Ylm(θ, ϕ),
(2.78)
where Ylm(θ, ϕ) are spherical harmonics with eigenvalues l(l + 1) and m, respectively.
These functions are well documented [43]. Separation of variables leads to a diﬀerential
equation for the radial variable
d2unl(r)
dr2

ℏ2

veﬀ(r) + l(l + 1)
