unl(r) = 0,
(2.79)
where the radial part of the wavefunction is given by χnl(r) = unl(r)/r. One could
numerically integrate this equation, and use the solutions as basis for the electronic
structure problem. However, with such a numerical basis set, the overlap matrix ele-
ments in eqn. (2.74) would have to be computed numerically as well. This would not
be viable for large systems as especially the three- and four- center Coulomb integrals
are computationally costly. This motivates the search for more sophisticated methods,
relying less on ’brute-force’. Two types of basis set that are commonly used in com-
putational chemistry are the Slater type orbital (STO) and the Gaussian type orbital
(GTO). As Gaussian basis sets has been employed in this project, STOs will only brieﬂy
be accounted for.
2.4.1.2
Slater-Type Orbitals
The structure of the radial wavefunction is such, that for the principal quantum number
n, the leading factor in the exact orbitals of the hydrogen atom, will be of the form
rn−1e−ζr. Therefore basis functions with this behaviour will likely represent the orbitals
==PAGE:60==
Chapter 2. Theory
46
well. The radial part of an STO is of the form [17]
χζ,n,l,m(r, θ, ϕ) = CYl,m(θ, ϕ)rn−1e−ζr,
(2.80)
where the coeﬃcient, C, in front ensures normalization. The STOs has the disadvan-
tage that they do not posses any radial nodes. The nodes are of course part of the real
wave-function (for n ≥1) and must be introduced through linear combinations of STOs.
This is not too restrictive as the exponential dependence ensure fast convergence with
increasing number of functions - the major drawback of STOs is in the calculation of
three- and four-centre two-electron integrals, as these cannot be performed analytically.
Therefore the primary use of STOs is in atomic an diatomic systems where they show
great accuracy. They are also used with DFT methods that do not include exact ex-
change (non-hybrid) and where the Coulomb energy is calculated by ﬁtting the density
to a set of auxiliary functions [17].
2.4.1.3
Gaussian Basis Sets
The downfall of the STOs is that the analytical expressions involved in calculating
the three- or four-center Coulomb integrals are too complicated.
Consequently they
are solved numerically, which is computationally much more expensive than evaluating
an analytical expression. This leads back to the same issues that arise in the above
mentioned ’brute-force’ method [18]. In 1950, Boys proposed an alternative solution,
based on Gaussian functions to replace to exponentials in STOs [44]. Gaussian-type
orbitals (GTOs) allow for an analytical evaluation of the 2-electron 4-center integrals
that arise in the Coulomb and exchange terms in HF and DFT (exchange terms are only
found in hybrid-DFT schemes), which is an enormous computational advantage. The
natural representation for the GTO is in polar coordinates [17]
φζ,n,l,m(r, θ, ϕ) = CYl,m(θ, ϕ)r2n−2−le−ζr2,
(2.81)
where C is a normalization constant and the exponent, ζ, is variationally optimized to
match the corresponding atomic wavefunctions. However, a more convenient form for
representing the Hamiltonian matrix-elements, is with the Cartesian GTOs
φζ,lx,ly,lz(x, y, z) = Cxlxylyzlze−ζr2,
(2.82)
==PAGE:61==
Chapter 2. Theory
47
where r2 = x2 + y2 + z2, and the sum l = lx + ly + lz determines the type of orbital
that the GTO represents.
l = 0 describes an s-orbital, l = 1 describes a p-orbital
and so on. A consequence of using Cartesian GTOs is that for l ≥2 there are more
Cartesian GTOs than independent polar GTO components. For a d-type GTO (l = 2)
there are 6 Cartesian components (x2, y2, z2, xy, xz, yz), but only ﬁve spherical functions
(Y2,2, Y2,1, Y2,0, Y2,−1, Y2,−2). Fortunately it turns out that the six Cartesian GTOs may
be transformed into the ﬁve spherical d-orbitals and an addition s-orbital (x2 + y2 +
z2). In the same way, there are ten Cartesian f-orbitals that can be transformed into
seven spherical f-orbitals and three spherical p-functions. This property of the Cartesian
functions may cause problems of linear dependence between the basis functions, and
sometimes the additional lower-l functions are eliminated from the basis set [17, 18].
Cartesian GTOs are more convenient for the computation of the Hamiltonian matrix, as
molecular integrals factorize into the three Cartesian variables [18]. This compensates
the above mentioned diﬃculties.
The most important property of Gaussian functions is that the product of two Gaussians
with diﬀerent centers become a new Gaussian with a diﬀerent center and exponent. This
is called the Gaussian product rule and will be shown below. First, let us introduce an
unnormalized primitive Cartesian Gaussian centred at RI and with exponent ζ
Gijk(r, ζ, RI) = (x −xI)i(y −yI)j(z −zI)ke−ζ(r−RI)2
(2.83)
The product of such two Gaussians then read
Gi1j1k1(r, ζ, RI)Gi2j2k2(r, τ, RJ) =e−γ(RI−RJ)2e−κ(r−RK)2(x −xI)i1(x −xJ)i2
× (y −yI)j1(y −yJ)j2(z −zI)k1 + (z −zJ)k2,
(2.84)
where
γ =
ζτ
ζ + τ ,
κ = ζ + τ,
RK = ζRI + τRJ
ζ + τ
.
(2.85)
The product can further be transformed into a more convenient form, expressed as a
linear combination of Cartesian Gaussians centred at RK. Figure 2.6 illustrates a simple
example of the product of two Gaussian functions.
==PAGE:62==
Chapter 2. Theory
48

Figure 2.6: Product of two Gaussians functions on a one-dimensional grid, centred at
x = 4(blue) and at x = 6(green). Both are of the type i = 0 (s-type) and ζ = 1. The
product Gaussian (red) is centred at x = 5.
Notice that the diﬀerence between the STO and GTO is in the r dependence in the
exponential. The GTOs depends on r2 in the exponential, whereas the STOs depends
on r. The STOs posses a ’cusp’ (discontinuous derivative) at origin which is the proper
behaviour near the nucleus. The GTOs on the other hand has zero slope at the nucleus.
Also the GTOs fall oﬀtoo rapidly, and the ’tail’ of the wavefunction is consequently
represented poorly. Therefore more GTOs are needed to achieve a certain accuracy, as
compared to the STOs [17]. However, the GTOs has the advantage that the product of
two primitive Gaussians becomes a new Gaussian. This property enables an analytical
evaluation of the molecular integrals, which makes the required increase in the number of
GTO basis functions more than compensated for. In terms of computational eﬃciency,
GTOs are therefore preferred and are almost universally used as basis functions in
electronic structure calculations [17]. An eﬃcient basis is in fact to expand the STOs in
GTOs. In this way the shape of the true AOs are better captured, as compared to purely
using Gaussians, whilst keeping the computational eﬃciency of working with Gaussians.
This type of basis set is the subject of sec. 2.4.1.5.
2.4.1.4
Size and Classiﬁcation of the Basis Set
The formalism and theoretical tool for representing the atomic orbitals, that in turn span
the KS- or HF-wave-functions, has been presented in the previous sec. 2.4.1.1-2.4.1.3 .
==PAGE:63==
Chapter 2. Theory
49
When it comes to implementing a Gaussian basis set, one must make a choice on what
kind, and how many, basis functions to include.
The minimal basis set use only a single basis function per atomic orbital. The atomic
orbital is classiﬁed by the angular momentum l = lx + ly + lz, and the kind of atom it
associates with. The ζ-coeﬃcient (see eqn. (2.82)) is ﬁtted to each atom, and such basis
set is called either minimal or single-ζ (SZ). It is remarkably accurate for isolated atoms,
but fails to describe molecular systems where chemical bonding polarizes the electrons
and distorts the AOs [17, 18]. The natural step to improve this is to use additional basis
functions per atomic orbital. Adding a single basis function results in the double-ζ (DZ)
basis set. This gives more ﬂexibility and of course further ﬂexibility can be obtained
by adding more basis functions. This leads to triple-ζ (TZ), quadrouple-ζ (QZ) and
n-tuble-ζ (nZ) in general. The ζ-coeﬃcient are variationally optimized in each basis set,
for instance with regards to minimize the HF energy. Thus a 1S-DZ(l = 0) AO would
be represented as
ϕ1S(x, y, z) = (1 −α)ϕζ1,1S(x, y, z) + αϕζ2,1S(x, y, z),
(2.86)
where α describes the mixing of the two basis functions and is also variationally opti-
mized. The total wavefunction of the molecule is then described as a linear combination
of atomic orbitals (LCAO), which results in the Hamiltonian matrix equations (see eqn.
(2.73)).
The basis functions can, however, be distributed in a smarter fashion. The core states
are fairly insensitive to outside perturbations, such as bonding, and resemble the AOs
quite well [17, 18], while the outer electrons may be described best by an orbital that
is very diﬀerent from the atomic ones. Therefore it is common practice to separate the
basis set and describe the inner orbitals by a SZ basis set and valence electrons by a
split basis set such as DZ. These are called split valence basis set and are denoted by a
preﬁx V, e.g. VDZ.
A split-valence basis set is very eﬃcient for solving the atomic problem, however adding
more basis functions of the same symmetry does not accurately describe the distortion
of the AOs in molecular systems [17, 18]. This is better described by orbitals of higher
angular momentum. It turns out, from perturbation theory, that the most contributing
orbitals involved in describing the polarization of the AOs with angular momentum l, is
==PAGE:64==
Chapter 2. Theory
50
orbitals of angular momentum l ± 1 [17, 18]. Thus to eﬃciently describe the molecular
orbitals (MOs), polarization basis functions corresponding to the ﬁrst unoccupied orbital
in the isolated atom, are included in the basis set. Polarization functions can be added
in diﬀerent ways, with more than only the ﬁrst unoccupied angular momentum. When
polarization functions are added, a P is added to the abbreviation of the basis set,
thus a double-ζ plus polarization split valence basis is denoted DZVP. Polarizing basis
functions are usually included with a radial distribution similar to the isolated AOs. In
neutral molecules, polarization functions in general provide a good description of the
MOs. However, when choosing the size of a basis set, the best balance is achieved by
doubling ﬁrst, and then polarizing [17, 18].
A second augmentation to a split valence set is the inclusion of diﬀuse functions. These
are valence orbitals with a low exponent in the radial distribution, thus producing large
orbitals. They are designed to account for negatively charged anions, where the Coulomb
repulsion between the electrons produce quite spread AOs [17, 18]. They are indicated
in the basis set, either by a plus sign ’+’, or by ’aug’ for augmentation. They are also
useful for describing non-bonding interactions, such as hydrogen bonds.
2.4.1.5
Contracted Gaussian Type Orbitals
The shape of the Gaussian functions does not have the proper shape for describing the
AOs, as they are not solutions to the atomic problem. The true AOs has a cusp (non-
zero derivative) at the nucleus, whereas the Gaussians are smooth (zero slope) [17]. This
feature is better described by the exponential functions in STOs, and to mitigate this,
a common solution is to represent the STOs as a ﬁxed linear combination of GTOs [18].
φSTO =
k
X
i=1
aiφGTO
ζi
,
(2.87)
where k is the number of GTOs in the combination with exponents ζi and ai is the
expansion coeﬃcients. In this way, the shape of the basis-functions are improved, as
compared to purely having Gaussians, while retaining the mathematical convenience of
working with Gaussians. Notice that the combination is ﬁxed, so that no extra degrees of
freedom are introduced. The exponents are ﬁtted to best mimic the shape of the actual
==PAGE:65==
Chapter 2. Theory
51
STOs. A widely used minimal basis set is the STO-3G, where one STO is assigned to
each occupied AO, and 3 GTOs make up one STO.
This approach is limited by the accuracy of a minimal basis set (one basis function per
occupied AO) as well as the accuracy of the STOs. A higher accuracy can be achieved
by a more general and ﬂexible approach. As seen, a new set of basis functions can be
constructed from GTOs and such general basis function is known as a contracted GTO
(CGTO)
ΦCGTO =
k
X
i=1
aiφPGTO
ζi
,
(2.88)
where ai now is denoted contraction coeﬃcients. The primitive GTOs (PGTOs) are
introduced in Eqn. (2.83). An account of the development of CGTOs and why it is
called a contraction scheme can be found in Jensen [17, Chapter 5]. The CGTOs are
ﬁxed linear combinations of PGTOs and therefore only count as a single basis function.
The selection of contraction coeﬃcients and exponent coeﬃcients has been developed
over time. It generally relies on using variational methods [17, 18] e.g. with regards to
minimizing the HF-energy.
The CGTOs are good for describing the chemically inert inner orbitals, as they capture
the cusp of the wavefunction near the nucleus. In a split valence scheme, one can use
few (or a single) well tailored, but inﬂexible CGTO(s) constructed from many PGTOs,
and then for the chemically active valence orbitals, one can instead beneﬁt from having
more valence CGTOs contracted from few PGTOs. In this way the inner orbitals are
well described, but also not susceptible to changing and polarizing, while the outer
valence orbitals are ﬂexible which enables the description of chemical bonding.
The
shape of the valence basis functions are not as important as for the inner orbitals,
because the valence MOs usually diﬀers much from the AOs of the neutral atom. Thus
the split-valence contraction scheme adds ﬂexibility to the basis set, while capturing the
energetically important contribution of the inner orbitals.
Many contraction schemes exists, such as the Dunning-Huzinaga basis set for ﬁrst-row
atoms [18], where out of 9 s-type PGTOs, 6 are contracted to represent a 1s inner orbital
and the remaining three are left uncontracted. Out of 5 p-type PGTOs in this scheme,
4 are contracted to 1 outer p-type orbital and 1 remains uncontracted. It is an example
of a DZP basis set, and the contraction scheme is denoted by: (9s5p1d)/[4s2p1d]. Thus
==PAGE:66==
Chapter 2. Theory
52
a d-type PGTO is also included uncontracted (a polarization function). Another, more
eﬃcient basis set, is the split-valence Pople-style basis set as described in the following
sec. 2.4.1.6.
2.4.1.6
Pople Style Basis Sets
The Pople style basis sets are employed in the work of this thesis, and this section
provides a short description of the diﬀerent types of sets, along with examples on the
carbon atom.
STO-nG basis sets
Are widely used minimal basis sets [17], where n PGTOs are
contracted to ﬁt the Slater type orbitals. The exponents of the PGTOs are variationally
optimized to best ﬁt the Slater type orbitals. It has been found that having n > 3 gives
little improvement, and consequently STO-3G is the basis set of choice. For carbon
in the STO-3G basis the PGTOs are distributed as (6s, 3p) →[2s, 1p], meaning that 6
s-type PGTOs are contracted to 2 s-type CGTOs and 3 p-type PGTOs are contracted
to 1 p-type CGTO.
k-lmnG basis sets
These basis sets of Pople and his co-workers, are of the split-
valence type [17, 18]. The k denotes the number of primitives used for the core orbitals.
The number of letters following the dash denotes how many functions the valence orbitals
are split into. (lm) denotes double splitting, (lmn) denotes triple. The numbers l, m, n
denotes the number of primitives used to represent each of the functions that the valence
orbitals are split into. The valence orbitals considered here are only s- and p-orbitals,
and if orbitals of higher angular momentum are considered (polarization functions) these
are denoted after the G. If diﬀuse functions are included, these are denoted by crosses in
front of the G. As an example, a basis set widely used in this work is the 6-31++G(d,p)
basis set, which is elaborated below. By convention, the largest standard Pople style
basis set is 6-311++G(3df,3pd) [17].
The k-lmnG basis treats the radial distribution of the valence s- and p-orbitals as the
same, by using the same exponent for both the s- and p-functions.
This restriction
reduces the ﬂexibility of the basis set, but increases the computational eﬃciency. The
exponents and contraction coeﬃcients have been optimized at the HF-level for atoms,
==PAGE:67==
Chapter 2. Theory
53
and may not at ﬁrst hand be justiﬁed as optimal for DFT-calculations. However, it is the
most widely used DFT-basis set in the literature [45–47] and Riley et al. [21] concludes
that it is amongst the best performing basis sets.
It is worth noting that although the 2s and 2p orbitals in carbon are described by
Gaussian functions with the same exponent, they do not loose the character of their
angular orbital momentum. The zeta coeﬃcient only describe the radial exponential
decay of the basis functions.
The s or p character is handled by the lx, ly and lz
exponents in eqn. (2.82).
3-21G basis set
The 3-21G basis set serves well as an example of a split valence
Pople basis set. Three PGTOs are contracted to form the inner orbitals, and the valence
orbitals are constructed by two CGTOs. The ﬁrst CGTO is contracted by two PGTOs
and the second is simply formed by a single PGTO. Thus for carbon the set would be
represented by (6s, 3p) →[3s, 2p]. This basis set is in fact constructed by the same
amount of PGTOs as the STO-3G basis set, but it gives more ﬂexibility because it
has twice the amount of valence basis functions to form the molecular orbitals. In the
computational chemistry program called Gaussian [13], this basis set is implemented
as shown in tab. 2.1.
From table 2.1 we can explicitly write the representation of each orbital

This basis set use 6 PGTOs contracted to one CGTO to represent
the core orbitals of the atoms. The valence orbitals are spanned by two CGTOs, one

Polarization and diﬀuse functions
In the Pople-style basis set, polarization func-
tions on ﬁrst row atoms (from Li to Ne) are denoted by the suﬃx d. Thus the 6-31G(d)
basis included extra d orbitals on the ﬁrst row atoms. One can further augment the
basis set with polarization functions on the hydrogen (and helium) atoms, which is de-
noted by a p-suﬃx. The 6-31G(d,p) thus both include d-orbitals on ﬁrst row atoms and
p-orbitals on hydrogen atoms.
In many systems, the hydrogen atoms play a passive role, and often one can leave
out polarization or diﬀuse functions on them.
This usually only induce a constant
error in the energy that tends to cancel out, as it is mostly energy diﬀerences that are
of interest. Neglecting the polarization or diﬀuse function can amount to signiﬁcant
saving of computation cost. However, this is naturally not justiﬁed in systems where
the hydrogen atoms play an active part, such as in hydrogen-bonded systems [17].
==PAGE:69==
Chapter 2. Theory
55
As polarization functions are not used to describe higher lying orbitals, but rather a
distortion of the occupied orbitals, they are included with the same exponents as the sp
orbitals.
Diﬀuse functions are introduced to the basis set in the same manner: a single preﬁx
plus sign ’+’ denotes diﬀusive s- and p-functions on the heavy atoms, e.g. 6-31+G,
and double preﬁx plus signs ’++’ denotes diﬀuse s-functions are added to hydrogen and
helium atoms, e.g. 6-31++G.
6-31++G(d,p) basis set
This is the 6-31G basis augmented with diﬀuse functions
on all atoms (H and He included) and augmented with polarizing d-orbitals on heavy
atoms and p-orbitals on hydrogen and helium atoms. Tab. 2.2 shows the exponents and
contraction coeﬃcients of the PGTOs comprising the CGTOs for the carbon basis set
in Gaussian 09 [13].

6-311++G(3df,3pd) basis set
This is, by convention, the largest of the standard
Pople basis sets [17]. It is a triple split-valence set with diﬀusive s and p-functions on
heavy atoms and diﬀusive s-functions on H and He. The heavy atoms are augmented
with three polarizing d-functions and one f-function, while H and He are augmented
with three polarizing p-functions and one d-function.
2.4.1.7
Correlation Consistent Basis Sets
The exponents in Pople basis sets are optimized with respect to HF-theory. Usually
towards providing solutions to the atomic problem. However, this is not optimal for
calculations at MP2 or CI level since the correlation energy is neglected in HF. A basis
set has been developed to better account for the correlation energy by Dunning, which
is indicated by a ”cc” preﬁx, for correlation consistent, e.g. cc-pVDZ for a correlation
consistent polarized split-valence double-ζ basis set. One might think that this type
of basis set would be the best choice for a DFT calculation, where correlation energy
is included. But very often the Pople-basis sets perform better than the correlation
consistent basis sets, and in practice one must perform a convergence test to ﬁnd what
type of basis set best ﬁt the case under investigation [21]. This is the subject of sec. 3.2.
The merit of the Pople basis sets can be supported by the fact that a DFT calculation
in practice is a single-particle problem.
We have now introduced the application of DFT to molecular systems, and will turn
to practical aspects of calculating a binding energy. This is essential in estimating the
phosphate aﬃnity of the hexapeptide that we wish to study.
2.4.2
Binding Energy, Basis Set Superposition Error and Counterpoise
Correction
The binding energy of a complex is deﬁned as the diﬀerence between the energy of the
complex and its isolated fragment species. It is the work required to pull the species
to inﬁnite separation distance. In our case, the complex consists of two monomers: a
peptide and an anion, and the binding energy is [48]
∆Ebind = E∗
complex −(Epeptide + Eanion),
(2.90)
==PAGE:71==
Chapter 2. Theory
57
where E∗
complex is the energy of the complex in its relaxed geometry, which is denoted by
the asterisk *. Epeptide and Eanion is the energy of the peptide and the anion respectively,
both in the relaxed geometry of the isolated species.
When the energy calculations are performed using an atom-centred basis set, the size of
the basis set must be (severely) limited for the practical calculations to be feasible. For
most systems this means that absolute errors in the energy from basis set incompleteness
may be of several hartree (thousands of kcal/mol) [17]. Fortunately the interest is usually
in energy diﬀerences, as in this case of the binding energy, and therefore errors should be
as constant as possible. The errors from incompleteness will naturally vary from basis
set to basis set, which is why one can never compare energies calculated with diﬀerent
basis sets.
The atom-centred basis sets oﬀer a very compact and computational eﬃcient basis set.
However when comparing energies at diﬀerent geometries, the spatial distribution of the
basis set (positioned at the nuclei) in the geometries will be diﬀerent. For a molecule,
the quality of the basis set will not be the same at all geometries or conformations.
This is because the electron density around one nucleus, may be described by basis
functions centred at another nucleus. This can be very problematic when calculating
small energy diﬀerences, such as energies of van der Waals complexes and hydrogen
bonds. Augmenting the basis set with diﬀuse functions further add to this error, called
the basis set superposition error (BSSE) [17]. The our case of the peptide, the peptide
forms hydrogen bonds to the anion, and when we calculate the binding energy of this
interaction, using eqn. (2.90), two types of BSSEs arise. The ﬁrst is the one described
above; the peptide will change its conformation in the binding, and consequently be
described diﬀerently by its own basis functions. This type of intramolecular BSSE is
very diﬃcult to account for, and the usual practice is to ignore it [17]. The second type
of BSSE in the complex is the fact that the basis functions on the anion will describe the
peptide and vice versa. Therefore the peptide fragment in the complex, as compared to
the isolated peptide, will have a more complete basis set and consequently a lower energy.
The error thus tends to artiﬁcially stabilize a binding and this type of intermolecular
BSSE can approximately be accounted for.
The Counterpoise (CP) correction is an approximate way to estimate how much of
the binding energy is attributed to the BSSE [17].
The CP correction requires four
==PAGE:72==
Chapter 2. Theory
58
additional calculations of the system. Consider a complex AB of the two monomers A
and B, each having a nuclear centred basis set, a and b respectively. The complex AB
has the combined basis set ab. The complex is relaxed to a geometry where the structure
of the monomers usually will diﬀer from the isolated species, and again we denote the
complex geometry with an asterisk *. In order to estimate the BSSE, we calculate the
the energy of the the monomers A and B, with the geometry they have in the complex
AB. This is done both using their respective basis sets a and b, and additionally with
the full complex basis set ab. This means that the energy of A is calculated in the
presence of the b basis functions, located at the nuclear coordinates of the geometry of
the fragment B, but without the nuclei (or electrons) present. This will lower the energy
of the fragment A, since the basis set becomes more complete. The CP correction is
deﬁned as
∆ECP = E(A)∗
a + E(B)∗
b −E(A)∗
ab −E(B)∗
ab,
(2.91)
where E(A)∗
ab is the fragment energy of the monomer A in the geometry of the complex,
calculated with the basis set, ab, of the complex. E(A)∗
a is the fragment energy of the
monomer A in the geometry of the complex, calculated with the basis set, a, of the
monomer A. The signs in eqn. (2.91) are reversed, compared to the convention in most
textbooks, as the BSSE is deﬁned as positive in Gaussian. The CP corrected binding
energy then becomes [17, p. 227]
∆ECP
bind = ∆Ebind + ∆ECP.
(2.92)
Note that ∆ECP is an approximation to the BSSE that neither provides an upper or
lower limit to the eﬀect. The correction destabilizes a binding.
2.5
The Gaussian Software Suite
We have now seen that equilibrium geometries are described by a PES, which is deter-
mined by the electronic energy of a system. The electronic Hamiltonian can be solved in
a number of ways, and for molecules the problem is formulated numerically using local-
ized AOs. These AOs are in turn described by an appropriate set of Gaussian functions.
The machinery is ready for a quantum mechanical study of the peptide-phosphate com-
plex, and the software that will be used to solve the presented equations is Gaussian
==PAGE:73==
Chapter 2. Theory
59
09.
Gaussian 09 is a computational chemistry computer program of the Gaussian R⃝[13]
series. It was released in 1970 by John Pople [49] (1925-2004) and the name originates
from its use of Gaussian functions, which is also employed in the Pople basis set. Since
the 1970s, many revisions and editions have been released and Gaussian 09 is now a
popular and widely used research tool [50]. Some of the main capabilities of the software
are [51]
• Molecular Mechanics including AMBER and UFF force ﬁelds.
• Semi-emperical calculations
• SCF Methods
– Hartree Fock and Møller Plesset
– DFT methods, including many widely used exchange and correlation func-
tionals, as well as hybrid functionals.
• Coupled Cluster calculations
and much more. Gaussian 09 is able to predict energies, molecular structures, vibra-
tional frequencies and molecular properties of molecules using many build in job-types
[51]. In this work, the job types used are:
• Single point energy (SP), where a single SCF energy calculation is performed at a
speciﬁed level of theory,
• Geometry optimization (Opt), where the nuclear coordinates are allowed to relax
according to the electron density, determined by an SCF calculation.
• Counterpoise correction (Counterpoise), where the BSSE is estimated.
• A frequency (Freq) calculation to verify whether a geometry is in a minimum on
the PES, and to calculate thermodynamic quantities.
Gaussian 09 can predict NMR spectra, explore photo-chemistry and more. Also relevant
for this work is the capability to include solvent eﬀects. Below the considered job types
are elaborated, but ﬁrst an example of a Gaussian input script is described. It would be
==PAGE:74==
Chapter 2. Theory
60
redundant to account for the entire use of the Gaussian software, since a good overview of
the theory, job types and keywords can be found in the Gaussian online User’s Reference
[52], in the Gaussian manual [51], and in online tutorials, see for instance [53]. An excerpt
of the Gaussian input scripts used in this work can be found in app. E.
2.5.1
Gaussian Input Script
A Gaussian input ﬁle is an ASCII text ﬁle with information in diﬀerent sections [52].
Here follows a short description of the diﬀerent sections.
The ﬁrst section is the link 0 commands, where computational options are speciﬁed and
scratch ﬁles are located and named. These ﬁles are where raw computational data are
saved. link 0 commands are preﬁxed with a ”%”-sign.
The second section is the route section, where the calculation type and model chemistry,
along with diﬀerent optional parameters are speciﬁed. For instance, this is where one
speciﬁes that the desired calculation is a DFT energy calculation, using the B3LYP
hybrid functional with a 6-31+G(d) basis set. The route section is preﬁxed by a ”#”-
sign.
The third section is the title section, where a brief description of the calculation is given.
This is only to distinguish between diﬀerent calculations.
The fourth section is the molecule speciﬁcation. The ﬁrst line of this sections speciﬁes
the charge and spin-multiplicity of the system (see the paragraph below). Then follows
lines where the atoms and their respective coordinates are speciﬁed. It can either be
in form of a Z-matrix [54] or as Cartesian coordinates. The molecule speciﬁcation is
terminated by a blank line.
Additional sections may follow the molecule speciﬁcations, which for instance could be
a speciﬁcation of a specialized basis set. The input ﬁle is terminated by a blank line.
An example of a script is shown below.
==PAGE:75==
Chapter 2. Theory
61
#n B3LYP/6-31G Opt
A geometry optimisation on water
0 1 ! Charge and multiplicity
O
0.000000
0.127170
0.000000
H
0.757997
-0.508679
0.000000
H
-0.757997
-0.508679
0.000000
Spin multiplicity
Spin multiplicity is used to distinguish between several degenerate
wavefunctions that diﬀer only in the orientation of their angular spin momenta [55]. The
spin multiplicity of a molecule is directly related to the number unpaired electron spins,
and therefore it must be speciﬁed in the Gaussian input ﬁle. If spin multiplicity is 1, the
system will be in a so-called singlet state, where all spins are paired and restricted DFT
methods will be employed. On the other hand, if the spin multiplicity is larger than 1,
the system will have unpaired electron spins and the un-restricted DFT formalism must
be employed.
The rule of thumb that should be used to specify the spin multiplicity relies on Hund’s
rule [55], which favours the ﬁlling of degenerate orbitals. If n is the number of unpaired
electrons, then the spin multiplicity of the system is simply S = n + 1.
When all
electrons are paired, S = 0 + 1 = 1 and there is no degeneracy - a singlet. If the system
has an uneven number of electrons then n ≥1 and thus S ≥2. For a molecule with
an even number of electrons, the ground-state will typically be the closed-shell singlet
state. However there are cases, such as molecular oxygen [56], where the ground state
is a triplet. In a triplet state, S = 2 + 1 = 3 where 2 electrons are unpaired, and usually
it corresponds to an excited state in a closed-shell molecule.
Naturally this rule of thumb has a ﬁrm theoretical foundation. It stems back to the
coupling of multiple angular momenta, of which an excellent description can be found
in Atkins and Friedman [14]. For Hund’s rule see Atkins and De Paula [55].
Below the diﬀerent job types of Gaussian are described.
==PAGE:76==
Chapter 2. Theory
62
2.5.2
Single Point Energy
Almost all the theory introduced so far builds up to performing a single point (SP)
energy calculation. It calculates the ground-state energy of a molecule or system, at
a certain level of theory.
Both the molecule and the level of theory are speciﬁed in
the input ﬁle. The level of theory refers to both the theoretical model, in this case
either restricted/unrestricted HF or DFT and the basis set to form the Hamiltonian
and overlap matrices. If DFT is chosen, then it is the self-consistent KS equations that
are iteratively solved. If it is HF, then the Roothaan-Hall equations are solved. The
common denominator is that the equations are self-consistent ﬁeld (SCF) equations, and
the algorithm for solving such equations can be speciﬁed by the keyword ”SCF” in the
Gaussian input ﬁle [57]. It is usually ﬁne to use the default options [51], but diﬃcult
convergence cases can be solved by switching to a more reliable and computationally
heavy algorithm. The keyword for a single point energy calculation is SP.
2.5.3
Geometry Optimization
Once the electronic energy can be obtained, numerous molecular properties become
available and perhaps the most important is the equilibrium molecular geometry [14].
In this project, the calculation of the equilibrium geometry of the peptide-anion complex
and the isolated peptide will be important for the calculation of a binding energy. It
is important because the equilibrium structures will have the lowest energy possible.
Having a geometry that is slightly diﬀerent than the optimal one, can result in in a
ground-state energy much higher than the actual minimum, and if the energy of both
the complex and the isolated peptide are not calculated at their minimum, it will result
in high uncertainties in the binding energy, that cannot be assessed.
The equilibrium geometry is obtained by calculating the forces on the nuclei and moving
them accordingly until all forces are within a tolerance close to zero. The forces on the
nuclei are determined by the gradients of the potential energy landscape in which they
move, and the potential energy is the electronic energy plus the inter-nuclear repulsion
(see sec. 2.1.1). For a diatomic molecule, the molecular potential energy, E, depends
only on the internuclear distance, R, and therefore we need to locate the zero of dE
dR to
ﬁnd the minimum (or stationary point). For polyatomic molecules the search is more
==PAGE:77==
Chapter 2. Theory
63
complicated because the potential energy is a function of many nuclear coordinates qi.
At the equilibrium geometry, the forces fi on the nuclei vanish
fi = −∂E
∂qi
= 0.
(2.93)
A geometry optimization calculation is the search for a stationary point in the energy
landscape. Before such calculation, a coordinate system must be chosen to represent
the potential energy surface (PES), shown in ﬁg.
2.7, and the molecular structure.
The optimization will typically be facilitated by the use of internal coordinates [14],
where the molecule is represented by bond lengths, bond angles and torsion angles.
The PES shown in ﬁg. 2.7 maps the potential energy as function of such two internal
coordinates (degrees of freedom).
The ﬁgure has three minima, each corresponding
to an equilibrium conformation of the system. As we search for stationary points in
a geometry optimization, there is the risk of ending in a maximum or a saddle point.
Saddle points corresponds to transition structures connecting two equilibrium structures
[51]. An optimization algorithm can attempt to look for transition structures, however
the usual interest is in the minima. Optimization to minima are called minimizations
[51].
Figure 2.7: A PES of two degrees of freedom within a molecule. The PES maps out
the energy of the molecule as function of these internal coordinates. Thus each point
on the surface corresponds to a particular structure, and the height of the surface is
the corresponding energy. The ﬁgure shows three minima, two maxima and one saddle
point. The ﬁgure is from [58] and also found in [51]
A minimization in Gaussian begins at a molecular structure speciﬁed as an input,
and steps along the potential energy surface. At each step it computes the energy and
gradient to determine how far and in which direction to take the next step [51]. The
gradient can be determined numerically or analytically [14]. Most optimization algo-
rithms also estimate or compute the second derivatives of the energy with respect to
==PAGE:78==
Chapter 2. Theory
64
nuclear coordinates. The second derivatives are related to the force constants and vibra-
tional frequencies [14] and specify the curvature of the surface. The matrix comprised
of the second derivatives
∂2E
∂qi∂qj
is called the Hessian matrix, and it provides useful
information for determining the next step in the minimization procedure. Additionally
the nature of a stationary point can be determined from the eigenvalues of the Hessian.
A minimum is characterized by only positive eigenvalues, a ﬁrst-order saddle point has
one negative eigenvalue and so on.
A minimization job is ﬁnished when is has converged, which is when the forces essentially
are zero. To quantify this, Gaussian use the following convergence criteria [51] (atomic
units)
• The maximum component of the force must be below the cutoﬀvalue 0.00045.
• The root-mean-square (RMS) of the forces must be below 0.0003.
• The maximum component of the displacement for the next step must be smaller
than 0.0018.
• The RMS of the displacement for the next step must be below 0.0012
For large molecules the optimization procedure may be challenging, and many keywords
and tricks are available to facilitate the convergence.
Most are documented in the
Gaussian manual [51] and the in the online user reference [52]. The keyword for an
optimization job is Opt.
2.5.4
Counterpoise Correction
A CP correction calculation can be used in an energy calculation to estimate the BSSE
(see sec. 2.4.2). The Gaussian keyword is Counterpoise=N, where N is a positive
integer for the number of fragments (monomers) to calculate the BSSE in between. A CP
correction calculation on a water dimer using the B3LYP functional with the 6-31G(d)
basis set would be

After each atom speciﬁer follows a fragment speciﬁer that denotes which molecules
to estimate the BSSE between.
When fragments are speciﬁed, the total charge and
multiplicity of the system is speciﬁed along with the charge and multiplicity of each
molecule in fragment number order. Refer to the online Gaussian 09 user’s reference
[52] for additional information.
2.5.5
Frequency Job
The frequency job computes force constants and the resulting vibrational frequencies.
The vibrational frequencies are computed by determining the second derivatives of the
energy with respect to the Cartesian nuclear coordinates. It can be used to predict
IR and Raman spectra of molecules and obtain thermodynamic quantities of a system.
A property of the frequency calculation is, that if negative frequencies appear in the
output, the geometry does not correspond to a stationary minimum in the PES. Thus
the frequency job can be used to verify the optimization calculations.
The keyword is Freq, and it must be computed at an optimized geometry. It is common
practice, in a frequency calculation, to use an empirical scaling factor for higher accuracy.
For more information on the theory of molecular vibrations see [14], on the Gaussian
frequency calculation see [51] and for the frequency input ﬁle and its options see [52].
==PAGE:80==
Chapter 2. Theory
66
2.6
Modelling Water
The conformation and folding of proteins and peptides in solutions are highly governed
by the solvent [59, 60]. For a peptide, the pH of the solvent determines the protonation
state [1], and the dielectric constant aﬀects the inter and intra molecular hydrogen bonds
[60]. As this study investigates the aﬃnity of a peptide for capturing phosphate anions
in a water solution, it is only natural to include a water model in the simulations. The
most accurate way to do this, would be to include enough water molecules in system
to model the water solution. However, this would require hundreds of water molecules,
which would require massive CPU times for ab initio calculations [59], and therefore
we use an implicit solvent model instead.
In an implicit solvent model, the solvent
is modelled as a dielectric material outside a cavity that contains the solute. In this
continuum-like approximation, the microscopic description of the solvent is lost. This
approximation makes it computationally feasible to include a solvent, and sometimes it
is a very successful approach [61]. However, in other cases it also give misleading results
[59]. Several continuum models can be employed [62], and amongst these, the polarizable
continuum model (PCM) is one of the most succesful and widely employed [61].
Figure 2.8: Solvent accessible surface of the SGAGKT peptide. The ﬁgure is gen-
erated in PyMOL [63] by increasing the van der Waals spheres of the atoms to 1.4
(appropriate for water) of their original size. Some of the peptide is not visible due to
the spheres. Hydrogen is white, oxygen is red, carbon is green and nitrogen is blue.
==PAGE:81==
Chapter 2. Theory
67
The basic formulation of continuum models takes its starting point in the solution of a
classical electrostatic problem, and results in a charge distribution, σ(−→s ), smeared out
at the surface of a molecular cavity of proper shape [61]. The charge distribution is the
response of the dielectric to the charge distribution of the solute, ρ(−→r ), inside the cavity.
The cavity is usually constructed by interlocking spheres, centered around the atoms of
the solute, and the actual shape of the spheres and the resulting cavity will depend on
the algorithm used in the PCM [61]. For water, the spheres will approximately be a
sphere with a volume equal to the van der Waals volume of the corresponding atom.
Such a cavity is shown for the SGAGKT peptide in ﬁg. 2.8.
The PCM is implemented in Gaussian and employed with the keyword SCRF=PCM.
==PAGE:82==
Chapter 3
A Phosphate Binding Peptide
The theoretical foundation for a DFT study has now been established. With the simula-
tion software Gaussian 09 [13], the theory can be applied, and the electronic structure
of the peptide-anion complex calculated. Gaussian 09 oﬀers several diﬀerent jobs, al-
lowing for the calculation of the binding energy of the SGAGKT peptide. With these
tools, we can study the binding mechanism, and evaluate the potential of this peptide
for a biomimetic phosphate-recovering device.
This chapter presents studies of the binding conformation and energy. First, the peptide
is presented in more detail than in the introduction. The SGAGKT peptide is expected
to form a nest structure that stabilize the binding of the phosphate anion. However,
before the study of the nest formation can proceed, a series of calculations are presented,
in order to determine the options to use in the simulations. In order to have reliable
results, we must verify that the basis functions, that are employed, adequately represents
the MOs and captures the electronic energy. A series of investigations on the peptide-
anion complex follows, where the nest conformation is veriﬁed. The nest is found to
correspond to a deep minimum in the PES and the binding energy of a nest conformation
is calculated as well. However, this binding energy may not be representative for the real
system. Therefore, many diﬀerent binding conformations will be used to calculate the
binding energy. This allows for an average binding energy with an associated uncertainty.
The binding energy is found far stronger than what was reported by Bianchi et al. [1],
and consequently a discussion of the merit of the calculations follows. The discrepancies
are related to the thermodynamics of the peptide-anion complex, and it is proposed
68
==PAGE:83==
Chapter 3. A Phosphate Binding Peptide
69
that the interaction with the nearest molecules are highly important for the binding
mechanism by an eﬀect called desolvation.
The sulphate binding is investigated, and the peptide is conﬁrmed as a selective phos-
phate binding peptide, with higher aﬃnity for phosphate than for sulphate. The peptide
has potential in phosphate-recovery devices, and two types of capturing schemes are pro-
posed.
3.1
Introducing the Peptide
In 2012 Bianchi et al. [1] published a synthetic P-loop peptide sequence that was shown to
bind phosphate anions. The peptide sequence for P-loops is usually Gly-Xxx-Xxx-Xxx-
Xxx-Gly-Lys-(Ser,Thr). P-loops are found in a variety of phosphate binding proteins
[64], and they bind anions either via a positive charged side-chain such as the lysine,
and/or via the main chain amides.
In many P-loops, the peptide conformation, when binding a phosphate ligand, is highly
conserved [64]. It forms a nest by surrounding the anion with the main chain and the
lysine side chain. A nest is deﬁned as a three amino acid feature where the partially
positive NH groups forms a concavity around the negatively charged anion [65]. The
anion, also called an egg, becomes bound by hydrogen bonds to the amino acid molecule,
as shown in ﬁgure 3.1a. Depending on the main chain dihedral angels of the ﬁrst two
residues, the nest can either be in RL or LR conﬁgurations. RL nests are characterized
by mean values of φ1, ψ1, φ2 and ψ2 of −94◦, −3◦, 76◦and 22◦. In the LR nest they are
74◦, 16◦, −77◦and −20◦. A consequence of the angles is that nests can form compounds
that form a wider cavity, such as LRL or even LRLR. P-loops typically form LRLR nests
when bound to a phosphate group, and the nest is typically formed by the Xxx-Xxx-
Gly-Lys part of the sequence.
In the work of Bianchi et al. [1], a hexapeptide was synthesized with the objective
of forming an LRLR nest. The idea was that a synthetic peptide, with an enforced
LRLR conformation might bind phosphate as a stand-alone peptide. This is in contrast
to canonical P-loops, which often are part of larger protein complexes, and typically
occur before an α-helix [1].
The synthesized sequence was Ser-Gly-Ala-Gly-Lys-Thr
==PAGE:84==
Chapter 3. A Phosphate Binding Peptide
70
(SGAGKT), and the peptide is shown in linear conformation with the zwitterionic pro-
tonation state in ﬁg. 1.3. From their experiments, they hypothesised a binding confor-
mation for the fully deprotonated peptide that is shown in ﬁg. 3.1b.
The synthetic hexapeptide was characterized by Bianchi et al. [1], and the protona-
tion constants was determined by potentiometric titration (aqueous solution with 0.1M
NME4Cl at 298.1◦K). The peptide was found to undergo three protonation equilibria
regarding; the protonation state of the lysine ϵ-amino group (high pH, log K = 10.92),
the terminal threonine carboxylate group (acidic pH, log K = 2.91 and the N-terminal
amino group of serine (intermediate pH, log K = 7.01), see ﬁg. 3.2. They found that at
neutral pH, the zwitterionic protonation state, with the lysine chain protonated, showed
aﬃnity for binding PO4
2– and HPO4
–, see ﬁg 3.3.
(a) An LR binding nest.
(b) The proposed binding structures of Bianchi
et al.
Figure 3.1: (A) Illustration of an RL nest. A carbonyl oxygen (sphere) is bound by
hydrogen bonding (dots) to the nest (sticks). In the nest, only the main chain atoms
are shown for the ﬁrst two residues. For the third residue, only the main chain nitrogen
is shown. Hydrogens and side chains are omitted. The egg oxygen is hydrogen bonded
to the NHs of the ﬁrst and third residue. The oxygens are red, the nitrogens are blue
and the carbons are grey. The image is made in Pymol and it is modiﬁed from [66].
(B) The ﬁgure shows the structure of the synthetic hexapeptide bound to HPO4
2– as
proposed by Bianchi et al. [1]. The ϵ-NH2 of of the lysine is uncharged, which is not the
case in the zwitterionic protonation state of the peptide. Hydrogen bonds are shown
as dashed lines, and the circles represents the main chain CαHCO groups of the amino
acid residues. The only atoms shown, are the main chain atoms, as well as the lysine
chain. The ﬁgure is from [1].
==PAGE:85==
Chapter 3. A Phosphate Binding Peptide
71
Figure 3.2: Table of thermodynamic parameters from Bianchi et al. [1]. It shows the
protonation of the peptide (HL is the zwitterionic form with charge +1) and phosphate
and sulphate occurring at diﬀerent pH. The fully deprotonated peptide (L−) is the
strongest binder of HPO4
2– with a Gibbs free energy ∆G◦= −6.5 kcal/mol. However,
as seen in ﬁg. 3.3, it is not present at neutral pH. Thus the peptide of interest is the
HL with ∆G◦= −4.0 kcal/mol. The enthalpy is determined by isothermal titration
and the entropic contribution is derived.
Figure 3.3: Results of potentiometric titration
from Bianchi et al. [1]. The ﬁgures show isolated
and anion-bound ionized species of the hexapep-
tide HL. Anions present: (a) phosphate, (b) sul-
phate, (c) both anions.
The aim of this report is to study the
phosphate aﬃnity of the hexapeptide of
Bianchi et al, as this stand-alone peptide
has potential in biomimetic phosphate-
recovering devices. Such a device would
likely have to work at neutral pH and cap-
ture phosphate in a water solution.
At
neutral pH, the peptide is in the zwitte-
rionic protonated form (HL, charge=+1),
as shown in ﬁg. 3.3. Therefore the zwit-
terionic form of the peptide is the one of
interest for this, early-stage, study of a re-
covery device.
From ﬁg. 3.2 we ﬁnd that Gibbs free en-
ergy of the binding reaction,
HL + HPO4
2−= [(HL)(HPO4
2−)],
is -4 kcal/mol and thus favourable. How-
ever, the enthalpy of the binding is +1
kcal/mol and thus unfavourable. Thus ac-
cording to Bianchi et al. [1], the binding
==PAGE:86==
Chapter 3. A Phosphate Binding Peptide
72
is favourable due to the entropic contri-
bution from the surrounding. If we could conﬁrm these numbers using DFT, it would
validate the computations, and make it more viable to test the binding of other small
peptides. Of course one has to be critical and not blindly compare to the experiments.
The gain in electronic binding energy, which is part of the enthalpy in the reaction, is
the starting point for this study. Using DFT, we will investigate how and if the peptide
binds HPO4
2–. In the deprotonated form, the lysine residue is hypothesised to play an
important part in capturing the anion, as shown in ﬁg. 3.1b. We will investigate the
role of the lysine residue in the zwitterionic protonated form as well.
An interesting ﬁnding of Bianchi et al. of the zwitterionic HL peptide, is that it does
not bind sulphate. According to their results, sulphate binding only occurs at the lowest
pH values, which means that it is only the most highly protonated hexapeptide species
(H3L2+), that has sulphate aﬃnity. As selectivity is important in a device for recovering
phosphate, a study in reproducing this feature is also conducted (chapter 3.9).
A ﬁnal remark will fall on the term, ”nest”. The deﬁnition of a nest, in this hexapeptide,
is the formation of an LRLR conformation of the peptide backbone.
The proposed
structure of Bianchi et al. (ﬁg. 3.1b) will henceforth be denoted the hypothesised nest
structure. However, in the simulations, many structures will emerge that resembles this
proposed structure, but may diﬀer and may not posses the correct backbone angles to
qualify as a nest. These general types of structures, where the backbone and the lysine
folds around the anion, will be called a cage.
The following sections presents the DFT study of the binding energy and mechanism.
3.2
Choosing the Optimal Basis Set
The SGAGKT peptide is a large and complicated molecule to simulate. It has many de-
grees of freedom and its equilibrium conformations are determined by weak and strong in-
tramolecular forces. Diﬀerent functionals and basis sets capture the energy, correspond-
ing to these forces, with diﬀerent accuracy and diﬀerent computational cost. Therefore,
before we begin a DFT simulation, we must make a choice on which functional and
what basis set to use. The hybrid functional B3LYP is by far the most widely used
==PAGE:87==
Chapter 3. A Phosphate Binding Peptide
73
functional [41] for modelling molecular systems. Studies comparing B3LYP to newer
functionals conclude that it still performs well, also in calculating binding energies and
describing hydrogen bonds [21, 41]. However, hybrid functionals does not account for
dispersion [36], which includes the long-range van der Waals forces acting between atoms
or molecules. The dispersion is partly responsible for the formation of hydrogen bonds,
and becomes energetically important as molecules become larger and larger. This lack
of ability to describe medium-range xc-energy limits the B3LYP applicability for biolog-
ical systems where medium-range dispersion-like interactions play vital roles. It could
pose a problem, and indeed Holroyd and van Mourik [67] reports that B3LYP fails to
recover a conformational minimum of the dipeptide Tyr-Gly, that was found using MP2
calculations. They attribute this to the inclusion of dispersion in MP2 [36].
However, as post-HF methods, such as MP2, are computationally out of scope for a
hexapeptide, we must resort to DFT methods. No functional performs perfectly, and
the B3LYP is an eﬀective well-performing and widely used functional with demonstrated
ability to describe organic molecules, hydrogen bonds and is cheap enough to handle large
compounds [68]. Therefore in this work we will ”play it safe” and employ the all-around
performing and well known B3LYP functional for our calculations, knowing that not all
properties are captured. It should be mentioned that dispersion can in fact be included
in DFT, and has been empirically introduced in some functionals (see e.g. [36, 69]).
For a given functional to properly describe the peptide-anion system, we must supply a
suitable basis set. The basis set will be a Pople split-valence basis set, where we have
the possibility of a single/double/triple zeta description of the valence electrons and the
possibility of including polarization functions and/or diﬀuse functions. As we wish to
describe an anion, diﬀuse functions are a priori required, and when selecting the size
of a basis a rule of thumb is double ﬁrst - then polarize [18]. Therefore the basis set
should at least be of the size 6-31+G for an accurate description of the anion. As for
the peptide-anion complex, we expect the interaction between the two specimens to be
described by hydrogen bonds. In such binding, the hydrogen atom becomes polarized,
and therefore we should require at least a 6-31G(d,p) basis set on the peptide. However
one could argue that the spread out diﬀuse basis functions on the anion could assist in
describing the polarization of the peptide hydrogen atoms.
With this starting point, we have an idea of the minimum quality of an acceptable basis
==PAGE:88==
Chapter 3. A Phosphate Binding Peptide
74
set, and the 6-31++G(d,p) basis set would seem particularly good for a description of
the entire complex.
However, one can never be certain that increasingly large basis
sets give increasingly more accurate results, and there might as well be a smaller basis
set that gives equally good results at lower cost. The accuracy of a DFT simulation
can never supersede the accuracy of the chosen functional, and therefore it is expected
that simulation results (energy, geometry, frequencies) will converge as an increasingly
large basis sets are used. To investigate the reliability of the 6-31++G(d,p) basis set,
a set of convergence tests are presented below, and the structures used in the basis
set investigation will be the SGAGKT peptide isolated and the HPO4
2– anion isolated.
Ideally one should also test the basis sets for the peptide-anion complex as well and also
test a range of diﬀerent functionals too. This was, however, out of the frame of time.
Besides, the literature ﬁrmly validates the B3LYP for organic systems, and the test of
the anion and the peptide should give an idea of what to use for the combined system.
Additionally a test of the numbers of CPUs used is presented.
3.2.1
Basis Set Convergence test for the Peptide
In this section a basis set convergence test for the geometry optimization job of the
SGAGKT peptide is presented. The measure of convergence will be how the ﬁnal mini-
mized structures diﬀers from a structure minimized at a large basis set. This is quantiﬁed
by the root-mean-square deviation (RMSD) which is the measure of the average distance
between the atoms of superimposed molecules. RMSD is deﬁned as
RMSD =
v
u
u
t 1
N
N
X
i=1
δ2
i ,
(3.1)
where δi is the distance between N pairs of identical atoms (C, N, O etc.).
The the isolated peptide is used for the convergence test, and the structure comes from
a molecular dynamics simulation (for more details, see sec. 3.6). The peptide will be
minimized in water (SCRF=PCM) as this is also the case when calculating the binding
energy in the following sections. As the choice of structure might inﬂuence the result
of the convergence test, a second equivalent test has been performed using a diﬀerent
initial conformation of the peptide, and the result this second test is shown in app. F.
The result of this sections convergence test is shown in ﬁg. 3.4 as a plot of RMSD as
==PAGE:89==
Chapter 3. A Phosphate Binding Peptide
75
function of basis sets. A large number of basis sets have been tested including the Pople
basis sets, the STO-3G and the Dunning correlation consistent basis sets. The largest
basis set for which the geometry optimization converged, was the 6-311++G(d,p) and
the resulting geometry of this calculation is used as the reference or ”true” geometry.
Thus for a basis set X, RMSD between the geometry minimized with 6-311++G(d,p)
and the geometry minimized with X is calculated.
Figure 3.4: Basis set convergence test quantiﬁed as RMSD (˚A) between the geometry
minimized with the 6-311++G(d,p) basis set and the geometry minimized with basis
set on the x-axis. As the basis sets become larger and larger the RMSD converges
towards zero. It should be noted that the basis sets are sorted by descending RMSD
value and NOT by size. The basis set size cannot be quantiﬁed uniquely as it depends
on the molecule at hand.
The plot has the general trend that RMSD converges towards zero as larger and larger
basis sets are employed. The STO-3G minimal basis set is a far outlier and is unsuitable
for describing the stand-alone peptide, and the very large basis sets of triple zeta seems
to dominate the lowest RMSD values. However, the plot contains many surprises;
• The performance of the 6-311G(d) has an RMSD of 1.46 which is comparable to
the result of 3-21+G.
• The 6-21G** (** means (d,p)) performs worse than the 3-21G.
• 3-21G performs better than 3-21+G.
In addition, as we compare RSMD to the geometry optimized with the 6-311++G(d,p),
we would expect the 6-311G basis set to perform better than the 6-31G. This, however,
==PAGE:90==
Chapter 3. A Phosphate Binding Peptide
76
is not the case. 6-311G scores an RMSD of ∼1.5 while 6-31G scores ∼0.6. And both
6-31G(d) and 6-31(d,p) score higher RMSD than 6-31G. It seems that the addition of
diﬀusive functions is important, however we ﬁnd a big gab from 6-311+G (RMSD=0.3)
to 6-311+G(d) (RMSD=0.04). These discrepancies are diﬃcult to explain. From the
plot we can, however, conclude that all basis sets larger than the 6-31G provide a
reasonable description of the peptide. By looking at the high RMSD valued basis sets,
we also ﬁnd that none of the basis sets are including diﬀuse functions (except 3-21+G).
Thus diﬀuse functions have higher priority than polarization functions. As polarization
functions are expected to be important in describing the hydrogen bonds, it seems that
the choice of basis set must fall on the 6-31++G(d,p) basis set.
The rationale for
using a double-zeta basis set with diﬀuse functions is strengthened by work from Lynch
et al. [46], where it was found that the addition of diﬀuse functions to a double-zeta
basis set was more important than increasing to a triple-zeta basis when calculating
conformational energies with hybrid DFT methods.
The Dunning cc basis sets where also included in the basis set convergence test, however
only one, the cc-pVDZ, reached conversion. As the Pople basis sets are ﬁne for describing
the peptide, this was not investigated or pursued further. In the following sec. 3.2.2,
we shall see that the simulation time increases signiﬁcantly for the Dunning basis sets,
which is another reason for not employing them in this work.
The range of RMSD values is from about 2.3 to 0. To get a feel for the diﬀerence in the
resulting minimized structures, the plot in ﬁg. 3.5 was made. It shows all the resulting
structures as lines, and the 6-311++G(d,p) reference structure is shown with balls and
sticks. From the ﬁgure, we see that all the resulting structures are in fact very similar,
which reﬂects that a geometry optimization job is quite insensitivity to the choice of
basis set. This is because the overall shape of the PES is insensitive to the choice of
basis set. It is more the heights and depths of the hills and valleys that are sensitive to
the basis set [17]. The geometry optimization job attempts to locate the minimum, and
that is achieved quite well for most of the basis sets tested.
Error regarding linear dependence.
During the work of the convergence test, a
rather odd error was encountered; as larger and larger basis sets was employed the
simulations began to fail. It seems counter-intuitive that as the molecular orbitals of the
==PAGE:91==
Chapter 3. A Phosphate Binding Peptide
77
Figure 3.5: The ﬁgure shows the resulting structures of the basis set convergence
test, shown in ﬁg. 3.4, aligned to the resulting reference structure minimized with the
6-311++G(d,p). This reference structure is shown with balls and sticks, and all the
other structures are shown as lines. For the reference structure, the carbon atoms are
teal, the hydrogen atoms are white, the nitrogen atoms are blue and the oxygen atoms
are red.
peptide are better and better represented, it becomes increasingly diﬃcult to ﬁnd SCF-
solutions or minima in the potential energy landscape. It turns out that the discrepancy
arise from a bug in Gaussian 09 rev.D.01. This was resolved by help from the Gaussian
Technical Support, and the email correspondence is shown in app. G where the ”oﬃcial”
Gaussian response and answer to the problem is given. The convergence issues appears
when one ends up with a diﬀerent number of basis functions for diﬀerent steps in the
geometry optimization procedure. Gaussian creates an initial guess for the MOs at the
beginning of a new geometry (i.e. new step). This guess is an interpolation between
SCF-solutions from former geometries, and the bug arise when the number of basis
function are diﬀerent for these diﬀerent geometries.
This problem originates from a
deeper issue of using atomic centred basis sets. When the basis functions overlap, it
may cause linear dependence between the basis functions and thus the SCF will not
have a unique representation. This is especially a problem when using diﬀusive functions
with a far reaching tail. To avoid this issue and to improve computation time, Gaussian
==PAGE:92==
Chapter 3. A Phosphate Binding Peptide
78
orthogonalize the Hilbert space by removing some of the linear dependent basis functions,
which is why a diﬀerent number of basis functions are used for diﬀerent geometries.
According to the Gaussian Technical Support, this bug will be ﬁxed in the next revision.
Singlet or Triplet?
In the set-up of a Gaussian input script, we need to specify the charge and multiplicity
of system. The charge is naturally predetermined, and for the zwitterionic protonation
state of the peptide, the charge is +1. Spin multiplicity on the other hand cannot be
known beforehand. The singlet state is usually the most stable state for molecules [14],
but as mentioned in sec. 2.5.1, there are exceptions. Therefore we must test whether
the singlet or the triplet state is the most stable state. A structure attained from a
molecular mechanics simulation was used to this purpose, and it was optimized in water
at a level of B3LYP/6-31++G(d,p) - both in the singlet and in the triplet state. The
structure is the same as the one used in the second peptide basis set convergence test,
and is shown in ﬁg. F.2 in app. F. The result is shown in tab. 3.1, and the singlet state
is found more stable by 0.140 hartree or 87.85 kcal/mol compared to the triplet state.
The peptide is clearly in its singlet state.

3.2.2
Anion Energy Dependence on Basis Set
In the last section, the inﬂuence of diﬀerent basis sets on the description of the peptide
was evaluated.
As we seek to describe the interaction between the peptide and an
anion, it is also relevant to study the basis set required for an accurate description of
the anion.
In the work of Bianchi et al. [1], the SGAGKT peptide was reported to
have aﬃnity for binding HPO4
2– anions, and this is the anion we will use here. The
anion is negatively charged, which makes it spatially large.
The outer electrons are
be weakly bound as the inner electrons screens the attractive potential of the positively
charged nuclei, which consequently makes the outer orbitals large, see ﬁg. 3.6. Therefore
==PAGE:93==
Chapter 3. A Phosphate Binding Peptide
79
basis sets for describing negatively charged anions must be equipped with large diﬀuse
functions [17]. As the anion is negatively charged, the MOs will likely not resemble
atomic orbitals of isolated atoms, and polarization functions are as well expected to be
energetically important. However, as seen in the previous section, we cannot be certain
how well diﬀerent basis sets describe the system, and therefore a basis set convergence
test of the HPO4
2– anion is presented here.
The criteria will, however, be diﬀerent
here than for the peptide convergence test. It would not be meaningful to compare
RMSD to a reference structure for diﬀerent geometry optimizations, as the PES is not
nearly as complex as for the peptide, and the same structure would be obtained in the
minimization by almost every basis set. Instead the energy in a SP energy calculation
will be used as a converge parameter, along with the simulation time. The assumption
is that as the energy will converge as we increase basis set size, and the simulation
conversely will increase.
Figure 3.6: Highest occupied molecular (KS) orbital (HOMO) of the HPO4
2– an-
ion. The orbitals are obtained from a ”.fchk” ﬁle (Formatted Checkpoint File) [70]
which is part of the output of a Gaussian geometry optimization job when the Form-
Check keyword is included in the route section. Other keywords used was B3LYP/6-
31++G(d,p), Opt and SCRF=PCM. The visualization program Avogadro can extract
the MOs from the fchk-ﬁle and provide ﬁgures as this one. What is actually plottet is
the iso-surface that contains 98% of the HOMO-wavefunction. As the wavefunction is
a KS-wavefunction, it does not have any physical representation and does not represent
the true HOMO. Yet the MO is quite spread, illustrating the need for diﬀuse functions.
The approach was ﬁrst to build the HPO4
2– anion in Avogadro [9, 10] and run a
geometry optimization calculation in Gaussian, using B3LYP/6-31++G(d,p).
The
anion was relaxed in water, and the resulting geometry was used for the basis test. The
==PAGE:94==
Chapter 3. A Phosphate Binding Peptide
80
basis set test is performed with a PCM water model and the route section of the input
ﬁle is
#n B3LYP/BASIS SP SCF=(XQC,MaxCycle=512) Pop=Minimal SCRF=PCM
where BASIS is varied. The result of the energy calculations is shown in ﬁg. 3.7.

that polarization functions are energetically more important than the diﬀuse functions.
All in all, the 6-31++G(d,p) is a good compromise between accuracy and simulation
time.
From the plot, the general trend is that the energy is gradually converging, as larger and
larger basis sets are employed. The simulation time signiﬁcantly increases for the Dun-
ning cc basis sets and when (3df,p) and (3df,3pd) polarization functions are employed.
The energy starts to converge at the 6-31G basis set, however the 6-31++G basis set
still provides an unsatisfying description. It is surprising that the 6-311++G basis set
is higher in energy than the 6-31G(d,p) basis set, and thus it seems that polarization
functions are energetically more important than the diﬀuse functions. In the ﬁgure, the
6-31++G(d,p) is situated just before the simulation time begins to increase. It seems
that the 6-31++G(d,p) basis set is a good compromise between accuracy and simulation
time.

As for the peptide, we should test whether the ground-state of the HPO4

singlet or a triplet state. The anion is optimized in vacuum at B3LYP/6-31++G(d,p),
both as a singlet and as a triplet, and the resulting energies is shown in tab. 3.2. The
ground-state is a singlet and the energy diﬀerence between the states is 0.080 hartree or
50.20 kcal/mol.

3.2.3
Analysis of the Results
The peptide convergence test in ﬁg. 3.4 reveals that the geometry optimization is quite
insensitive the the chosen basis set, which is also seen in the literature [45, 71] and
illustrated in ﬁg. 3.5. The geometry optimized with the 6-311++G(d,p) basis is not
objectively best or the target geometry. However, the only rational choice is to use the
structure optimized at the highest level of theory as the benchmark. In the test the
overall tendency is that the RMSD is converging for larger and larger basis sets, and
the main conclusion is that a geometry optimization of the peptide will give reasonable
results, as long as the basis set is larger than 6-31G. The diﬀuse functions seems to have
higher priority than the polarizing functions.
From the anion convergence test we ﬁnd that the energy begins to converge above the
6-31G basis set. For basis sets larger than 6-31++G(d,p) the simulation time steeply
increase. From the test it seems that the addition of polarization functions is energet-
ically more important than the addition of diﬀuse functions. As the literature [17, 18]
reports that diﬀuse functions are very important for describing anions the choice of basis
set should be the 6-31++G(d,p) basis set.
In modelling the peptide-anion complex, the HPO4
2–-oxygens are expected to form hy-
drogen bonds to the peptide. This will require diﬀuse functions on the oxygens, as the
HPO4
2– is charged, and require polarization functions on the hydrogen atoms of the
peptide. The peptide convergence test favoured the addition of diﬀuse functions, rather
==PAGE:96==
Chapter 3. A Phosphate Binding Peptide
82
than polarizing functions and therefore, again, the best choice course of action seem to
be to use the 6-31++G(d,p) basis set.
The anion is charged, and therefore it requires a larger basis set than the peptide for a
description of same accuracy. Therefore one could decide to employ the 6-31++G(d,p)
basis set on the atoms of the anion, and use a smaller basis set for the peptide. This could
provide a more balanced description of the system, where the regions that requires more
basis functions are given so, and thus the quality of the description of each subunit of the
system is equal. On the other hand, this would also artiﬁcially favour the binding because
the peptide-wavefunction would become better represented in the vicinity of the diﬀuse
basis functions of the anion. Thus the peptide-wavefunction would be better described
and the peptide would gain energy by being closer to the anion. This approach will be
used in a preliminary study of the binding mechanism in sec. 3.3, whereas otherwise the
6-31++G(d,p) basis set will be used on the entire peptide-anion complex.
3.2.4
Optimizing the Number of Cores
The Gaussian jobs are calculated at a High Performance Computing (HPC) Cluster at
DTU. The jobs are submitted via a script and a guide to use Gaussian on the HPC
cluster can be found at [72]. An example of a submitting script is shown in app. E,
along with Gaussian input scripts. In setting up a Gaussian job on the HPC cluster
at DTU, one has the select the amount of cores (CPUs) to use for the job. The amount
to use both depends on the availability of cores, and on the ability of Gaussian to
runs jobs in parallel. An overview of the clusters and the available cores is found at
[73]. By running jobs in parallel, Gaussian can delegate subtasks to diﬀerent cores and
complete a job faster than using a single CPU. However, the simulation time cannot
continue to improve with increasing amount of cores. At some point it simply becomes
ineﬃcient to delegate jobs and the individual cores will not be used fully. Here a test
regarding the ability of Gaussian to runs jobs in parallel is presented and the result
is shown in ﬁg. 3.8. The test consists of several identical geometry optimization jobs,
each a using diﬀerent amount of cores. All the calculations use the same structure of
the peptide-HPO4 complex. We ﬁnd that the time the calculation takes to ﬁnish, called
the walltime, as function of the number of cores, almost saturate at 10 cores and the
gain from going to 16 cores is not signiﬁcant.
Therefore 10 cores will be used.
A
==PAGE:97==
Chapter 3. A Phosphate Binding Peptide
83
second concept, the CPU-time is related to the walltime and the number of cores by
CPUtime = walltime · #cores.
In this analysis, one should keep in mind that the ability of Gaussian to runs jobs in
parallel might perform diﬀerent for molecules of diﬀerent sizes, or for diﬀerent Gaussian
jobs. However, the geometry optimization job is the most widely used, and computation-
ally heavy, job considered in this work. We must also remember that a certain degree of
chaos is involved in every SCF calculation. While all jobs end up in the same minimum,
they do use a diﬀerent number of iteration steps, and thus a diﬀerent amount of time,
to locate the minimum.

Figure 3.8: Several identical geometry optimization jobs of the peptide-HPO4 anion
complex. The jobs uses a diﬀerent number of cores, to investigate the eﬃciency of
running parallel jobs in Gaussian. In both ﬁgures the blue curve shows the walltime,
and the green curve show the CPU-time. Both in seconds. We see in ﬁgure 3.8a, that
using 16 cores only give a slight gain in walltime, and actually increases the CPU-time.
Thus, one could be led to think that parallelization to 16 cores rather than to 10 cores
results in a less eﬃcient use of the cores. But here we must investigate ﬁgure 3.8b. It
shows the wall- and CPU-time per core in each job, and we ﬁnd that the increase in
CPU-time in ﬁgure 3.8a is caused by a saturation in the simulation time per core. The
conclusion is to use 10 cores in subsequent calculations.
The options to use in the Gaussian jobs of the peptide, the anion and the complex are
now established . Thus we are ready to proceed to the study of the phosphate binding.
==PAGE:98==
Chapter 3. A Phosphate Binding Peptide
84
3.3
Nest Conﬁguration from DFT
This section presents the ﬁrst study of the aﬃnity for phosphate binding of the SGAGKT
peptide. It serves as a preliminary ”proof-of-concept” investigation, in order to get an
idea of whether anion binding is favourable or not. We also wish to investigate whether
it forms a binding nest at all. In the following sections 3.4-3.7, the starting point will be
a structural minimization from a structure that already resembles a binding nest. This
might introduce bias to the conclusions, as it cannot be known beforehand whether the
binding nest, is actually representative for the conformational space of the peptide. It
could be that the binding nest only occurs in rare situations. This section will present
an ”unbiased” investigation of the binding structure. By this is meant an investigation
where the binding nest structure is not imposed beforehand. It turns out that a binding
nest is a deep minimum in the PES of the anion-peptide complex, and is likely to
represent a major part of the conformational space.
3.3.1
Method
We wish to investigate whether a binding nest can be the result of a geometry opti-
mization of a non-nest initial structure. This would serve as a strong indicator of the
validity of the binding nest hypothesis of [1]. Using the peptide builder of Avogadro
[9, 10] the amino acid sequence SGAKGT is easily build in a linear geometry. The next
step in setting up the calculation is to distribute the 24 HPO4
2– anions evenly around
the peptide, as shown in ﬁg. 3.9. Each anion position was constructed by placing the
24 anions evenly around the peptide on the surface of a square box with a side length
of 100 ˚A. The anions are subsequently translated towards the coordinate center of the
peptide until the distance from each anion to a peptide atom is less than 10 ˚A.
The basis set convergence test in sec. 3.2.3 suggests that the optimal basis set to use is
the 6-31++G(d,p). However, as this is a preliminary test and mostly serves as a proof
of concept, a smaller basis set will be employed for a lower computation time. The
calculations are performed in vacuum, because in water the PCM model tends to lock
the structure in the closest local minimum. This was observed by trial and is discussed
more in detail in sec. 3.5. As this section investigates the many possible diﬀerent binding
conformations, they must be performed in vacuum. The most important parts of the
input ﬁle is
%

The charge is −2 + 1 = −1 since the peptide is assumed to be in its zwitterionic
protonation state, and the complex is assumed to be in a singlet state as this was
the case for the isolated species.
The anion is represented by the 6-31++G(d,p) basis set and the peptide by 6-31G. This
choice was made for low computation time, but it may not be optimal for an ”unbiased”
study. As discussed in sec. 3.2.3 the larger basis set on the anion may artiﬁcially favour
a ﬁnal structure where the peptide is close to the anion. As the peptide is comes in the
vicinity of the anion, the diﬀuse functions on the anion will take part in describing the
peptide wavefunctions. Thus the closer the peptide is to the anion, the better the peptide
wavefunctions will be described, and this will lower the ground-state energy. However,
the actual impact of this eﬀect is unknown - it could be major or minor. Besides this
kind of basis set distribution could be more balanced than using the 6-31++G(d,p) basis
set on all atoms, as the anion requires a better description than the peptide due to its
negative charge. Also the basis set chosen for the peptide has been shown to provide an
accurate description in sec. 3.2.1.
3.3.2
Results
The result of this investigation is the minimized geometries of the geometry optimization
jobs. 20 out of the 24 geometry optimization calculations successfully converged. From
the 20 converged structures, 12 of them resulted in a structure that resembled a binding
cage. Five of such structures are shown in ﬁg. 3.10. In all ﬁve it is evident that the
lysine residue play a large role in capturing the anion. The general trend is that the
backbone of the peptide folds in a circle-like structure behind the anion, while the lysine
chain folds in a third dimension over the anion.
From this ﬁrst test, we have found that for some of the initial anion positions, the
complex resulted in a binding cage, which supports the hypothesis of Bianchi et al.
[1], see ﬁg. 3.1b. The energies of the relaxed structures are shown in app. H, and it
turns out that the cage structure in ﬁg. 3.10d has the second lowest minimum. This
