A ﬁnal remark is to the errors that occurred in the remaining four calculations:
1. In one of the optimizations, the last step the search did not lower the energy. As
the requirements for convergence was not fulﬁlled and the search had ”nowhere to
proceed”, the calculation aborted.
2. In three optimizations, an error occurred in the last step, because one of the
angles, describing a hydrogen bond, became linear. This is related to the internal
coordinate representation of the complex. App. I includes a description of this
error along with an email correspondence with the Gaussian Technical Support.
The error terminates through link 103 (see [75] for Gaussian error links)

In the following two sections, the binding energy of two diﬀerent nest conﬁgurations will
be calculated.
3.4
Binding Energy: The Hypothesised Binding Confor-
mation
In the previous section, the result of 24 geometry optimization jobs of the SGAGKT pep-
tide in a linear conformation with an HPO4
2– anion at diﬀerent positions were presented.
The anions were positioned in the vicinity of the peptide, and for some of the positions
the relaxation resulted in a binding cage, that resembles the hypothesised binding nest
from Bianchi et al. [1], shown in ﬁg. 3.1b. The nest conformation is thus the result of
ab initio minimizations and also the experimental ﬁnding of Bianchi et al. [1]. Therefore
it would be interesting to calculate the actual binding strength of this conformation.
We calculate the binding energy from the energy diﬀerence between the complex and
the isolated peptide and anion (see sec. 2.4.2)
∆Ebind = E∗
complex −(Epeptide + Eanion),
and the accuracy of the binding energy is improved by the CP correction
∆ECP
bind = ∆Ebind + ∆ECP.
The ﬁrst step, in order to calculate the binding energy of the hypothesised nest structure,
was to build a structure that resembled it. The structure was then relaxed in Gaussian
using the 6-31++G(d,p) basis set, and including the PCM water model. The resulting
relaxed binding nest is shown in ﬁg. 3.11a where the anion forms 5 hydrogen bonds to
the peptide; four to the backbone and one to the lysine terminus. A frequency job on
the relaxed conformation revealed that it is indeed a stable minimum (see app. J for
the lowest frequencies), and not a transition state, see sec. 2.5.5.
The energy of the isolated peptide must also be estimated. This is diﬃcult as the energy
of the peptide will depend on the conformation it relaxes to, which in turn is highly
dependent the initial structure. Here the approach was simply to remove the anion from
==PAGE:103==

A calculation of the binding energy, along with the relevant numbers is shown in tab.
3.3, and the binding energy is −25.24 kcal/mol. According to Fleming and Rose [76],
the energy of a hydrogen bond amounts to about 5-6 kcal/mol, and thus the binding
energy here corresponds to about 4-5 hydrogen bonds, which is also what is observed
in ﬁg. 3.11a. For further information on hydrogen bonds investigated using DFT at
==PAGE:104==
Chapter 3. A Phosphate Binding Peptide
90
B3LYP level, see Herbert et al. [68] for an investigation of hydrogen bonds in DNA, and
Paul and Guchhait [47] for general insight into intramolecular hydrogen bonds. In the
work of [68], binding energies for phosphate groups are reported along with a discussion
of the solvation eﬀect on the binding energy for diﬀerent PCMs.
In the fully deprotonated form of the peptide, Bianchi et al. [1] proposed a binding
conformation where the lysine was highly active in capturing the phosphate. It was
attributed to a hydrogen bond between the lysine nitrogen and the phosphate hydrogen
N · · · H −O −P. In this case, with the zwitterionic peptide, the lysine is still active,
but plays a diﬀerent part in binding the phosphate. As seen in ﬁg. 3.11a, it forms a
hydrogen bond to the phosphate oxygen, N −H · · · O −P, in cooperation with the N
terminus of the peptide. The partially positive phosphate-hydrogen, points away from
the binding cage.
Thus the phosphate-oxygens are surrounded by partially positive
charge - except at the C-terminus of the peptide, in the right side of the ﬁgure. The
O-C-O terminus is partially negative and points away from the cage. One could imagine
that the hexapeptide could increase its binding strength by addition of more partially
positive residues. This could help forming a more surrounding cage.
A low barrier hydrogen bond
The hydrogen bonds visualized in ﬁg.
3.11 are
estimates from a build-in method in Avogadro, and it is unclear how exact or repre-
sentative the hydrogen bonds shown really are. For instance, in Bianchi et al. [1], the
H · · · O distance in the N −H · · · O hydrogen bond is deﬁned to be less than 2.5 ˚A. The
hydrogen bonds can however be directly investigated using the iso-surface of the electron
density, obtained by the DFT SCF calculation. An iso-surface containing 93% of the
electron density for the relaxed binding nest is shown in ﬁg. 3.12, and it is obtained from
the ﬁnalized checkpoint (fchk) ﬁle [70] of the Gaussian optimization calculation, and
visualized using Avogadro. The electron density of the anion is closest to the electron
density of the peptide in the regions where the hydrogen bonds are depicted. When a
covalent bond is formed between two atomic nuclei, it is by sharing of electron density.
For one of the hydrogen bonds, pointed out with an arrow, the electron density is actu-
ally overlapping. This suggests a strong hydrogen bonds between the peptide and the
phosphate. The indicated hydrogen bond could have the characteristic of a low barrier
hydrogen bond (LBHB) [77]. The hydrogen is originally bound to a nitrogen atom on
the peptide, and in the interaction, it forms a hydrogen bond to an oxygen atom on the
==PAGE:105==
Chapter 3. A Phosphate Binding Peptide
91
phosphate. In a traditional hydrogen bond, this would be shown as N −H · · · O, where
the distance between the N −H bond would be signiﬁcantly smaller than the distance
of the H · · · O bond. However here the distance seems to be approximately equal, and
electron density is shared unbrokenly from the nitrogen over the hydrogen and to the
oxygen (this naturally depends on the iso-surface chosen). In the LBHB the proton is
free to move between the two atoms, which is why it is called a low barrier bond. The
LBHB is proposed to to play a critical role in enzyme biological catalysis [78]. The
LBHB we see here, could have a signiﬁcant role in the phosphate binding of the peptide.
The formation of a LBHB might be dependent on the pH of the environment, and for
further information on LBHBs in proteins and phosphate binding peptides, see [79, 80].
Figure 3.12: Electron density of the hypothesised binding nest. The electron density is
an iso-surface containing 93% of the electron density. It is obtained from the ﬁnalized
checkpoint (fchk) ﬁle [70] of the Gaussian optimization calculation, and visualized
using Avogadro. The black arrow indicates a hydrogen bond that might characterize
as a low barrier hydrogen bond. Phosphor is yellow, carbon is grey, hydrogen is white,
nitrogen is blue and oxygen is red.
The hypothesised binding nest is indeed a stable binding conﬁguration. However, it may
not be a representative conformation for the peptide-anion complex in a real solution,
and thus we should yet be careful of jumping to conclusions. There may be conformations
of the isolated peptide that are lower in energy, which invoke great uncertainty to the
binding energy. Note that Bianchi reports a Gibbs free energy of about -4 kcal/mol. As
the Gibbs free energy takes the environment into account, deviations to the the binding
energy are expected. This is discussed more in detail in sec. 3.8, where an average
over a representative ensemble of peptide and complex conformation will be used to
calculate a binding energy. This binding energy turns out to be remarkably close to
==PAGE:106==
Chapter 3. A Phosphate Binding Peptide
92
the -25 kcal/mol reported here. Before we approach this conclusive investigation, the
next sec. 3.5 will present an investigation of how the solvent model aﬀects the attain
minimum conformation in a geometry optimization.
3.5
Binding Energy: A Structure from Molecular Dynam-
ics and the Eﬀect of Solvent
So far it has been established that the binding cage conformation naturally occur, in
the sense that it is found amongst the 24 structures minimised from an initial linear
conformation. It has also been established that the binding nest has aﬃnity for binding
the HPO4
2– anion. At least when compared to the isolated peptide conformation in
ﬁg. 3.11b. However, it is well known [81–83] that a small hexapeptide will, over time,
attain multiple conformation states in a solution, and it is expected that the ground-
state energy of the peptide will signiﬁcantly vary with conformation. We have also seen
in ﬁg. 3.10 that energy the peptide-anion complex varied signiﬁcantly in energy. Sec.
3.6 will describe methods for sampling these many conformations, and in sec. 3.7 we
shall use the sampled conformations to obtain an average binding energy that better
represents the binding energy of the real physical system.
However, before we turn to calculating the average binding energy, this section will
present a test on how including a solvent, aﬀects the result of a geometry optimization.
In the experiments of Bianchi et al. [1], the peptide binds phosphate anions in a water
solution, and this would also be the case for most practical technological applications of
the peptide. Additionally the solvent often has a signiﬁcant role in bio-systems [62] and
therefore the SGAGKT phosphate binding peptide should be simulated in water.
The water model used is the Polarizable Continuum Model (PCM), introduced in sec.
2.6. It models the solvent as a dielectric constant, introduced outside a volume containing
the molecule of interest. The dielectric medium responds to the electric charges of the
molecule with screening charges on the surface of the cavity. The molecule interacts
with these surface charges, and they will screen and dampen the electric ﬁeld of the
molecule. In broad terms it therefore shortens the interaction range of intramolecular
electric forces. In a geometry optimization, the PCM could therefore tend to lock the
molecule in a geometry close the input structure. The risk that a relaxation job does
==PAGE:107==
Chapter 3. A Phosphate Binding Peptide
93
not ﬁnd the best attainable minimum, and rather settles in the closest local minimum
may therefore worsen.
The investigation on how including a solvent aﬀects the result of a geometry optimiza-
tion, will be performed with the complex of the SGAGKT peptide and the HPO4
2–-anion.
The peptide is in the zwitterionic protonation state, and the complex structure is from
an an accelerated molecular dynamics (aMD) simulation that ran for about 0.5 µs, see
sec. 3.6 for details. The structure occurred about 58% of the time in the simulation, and
is therefore a good representation of a binding conﬁguration. The complex is optimized
in a thorough optimization path, where it is ﬁrst relaxed in vacuum, and subsequently in
water, at larger and larger basis set. The resulting structure is compared to a structure,
obtained by a single direct optimization of the complex in water, to evaluate the eﬀect
of including solvent. Thus the complex was optimized in the following steps:
1. B3LYP/3-21+G in vacuum.
2. B3LYP/6-31G in water using the structure of (1).
3. B3LYP/6-31+G(d) in water using the structure of (2).
4. B3LYP/6-31++G(d,p) in water using the structure of (3).
The initial structure is shown in ﬁg. 3.13a and the ﬁnal optimized structure of step 4 is
shown in ﬁg. 3.13c. Two additional calculations were performed using this structure:
• B3LYP/6-31++G(d,p) optimization in water using the initial structure.
• B3LYP/6-31++G(d,p) frequency calculation in water using the optimized struc-
ture of step 4.
The frequency calculation revealed that the structure had settled in a true minimum and
not a saddle point, which was veriﬁed by checking that no frequencies were negative.
The optimized structure from the direct optimization in water is shown in ﬁg. 3.13d
aligned to the ﬁnal structure of step 4. The energies at level of B3LYP/6-31++G(d,p)
is shown for each step in tab. 3.4.
To be able to calculate a binding energy, the BSSE of the complex and a relaxation of the
isolated peptide was performed as well. The procedure here was to simply remove the
==PAGE:108==
Chapter 3. A Phosphate Binding Peptide
94
anion from the initial structure and use the resulting geometry as the initial geometry.
The peptide was ﬁrst relaxed at a level of B3LYP/3-21+G in vacuum, and subsequently
using B3LYP/6-31++G(d,p) in water. The resulting energy is shown in tab. 3.4 and
the minimized geometry is shown in ﬁg. 3.13b.
Table 3.4: Single point energy (SP) calculations at B3LYP/6-31++G(d,p) of the re-

From this analysis it is concluded that, at least in this case, a direct minimization in
water does constrain the relaxation algorithm. By ﬁrst doing a relaxation in vacuum
and subsequently in water, the complex relaxes to a deeper minimum. The 4-step opti-
mization route may, however, be too thorough. From tab. 3.4 we see that the change in
energy and geometry between step 2 to step 4 is minor. Therefore an initial geometry
optimization using B3LYP/3-21+G in vacuum, followed by a geometry optimization us-
ing B3LYP/6-31++G(d,p) in water should suﬃciently describe the minimum attainable
from the particular initial geometry. From the PCM and vacuum energy calculation of
the initial structure, we see that the PCM stabilizes the conformation by about 0.16
hartree or 100.4 kcal/mol. How this aﬀects the binding energy is unclear. It could be
that it evens out, as the binding energy is calculated by energy diﬀerences.
==PAGE:109==
Chapter 3. A Phosphate Binding Peptide
95
The frequency analysis showed that the ﬁnal geometry of ”step 4” did describe a mini-
mum. As the diﬀerence in energy between the direct optimization and the 4-step opti-
mization was only about 1.3 kcal/mol, it seems unnecessary to verify whether or not a
relaxed complex is in a true minimum in the following section.
The RMSD of the peptide is high as compared to the RMSD values of the complex.
This is probably due to the fact that the initial guess for the complex was good, as it
was a structure that often occurred in an MD simulation. The initial peptide structure
was, however, not a good guess as it was generated by removing the anion in the initial
structure for the complex. The minimization then changes the peptide structure more
dramatically.
The binding energy is calculated using the energy of the complex in step four, the BSSE
of the complex, the energy of the isolated peptide and the energy of the anion. It is
found to be −0.0442 hartree or −27.74 kcal/mol, and thus the binding is favourable.
This was also the case in the previous sec. 3.4 of the hypothesised binding nest, where
the binding energy was = −0.040 hartree or −25.24 kcal/mol. The increased binding
strength in this section could be attributed to the more thorough geometry optimization
”path” that was tested here. It could also be attributed to a better initial geometry.
Three studies on phosphate binding has now been presented. They all point towards that
the SGAGKT in zwitterionic protonation state has aﬃnity for binding phosphate, and
with a binding strength of about 25-27 kcal/mol, which corresponds to about 5 hydrogen
bonds. Up till now, we have used a rather arbitrary reference energy for the peptide in
calculating the binding strength. In fact, the peptide is ﬂoppy with many local minima,
and exists as a dynamic ensemble of conformations. In order to draw conclusions to
the binding energy, we must make sure that the peptide and the complex energies are
representative, to the potentially vast amount of diﬀerent conformations, that could
be attained. Therefore we need to calculate energies of many diﬀerent conformations,
both for the isolated peptide and for the complex. We can only make inferences to the
energy when we know that the energies actually represents the conformational space
and captures the many local minima.
So how do we sample all these conformations? One could map the energy-landscape by
systematically varying all the internal degrees of freedom, that is, inter atomic distance,
dihedral angles and so on. However, such a systematic approach would only work for

97
very small molecules. For larger systems, methods exists that relies on perturbing a
geometry from one local minima to another. Such conformational sampling techniques
include [17]
• Stochastic and Monte Carlo methods.
• Molecular dynamics.
• Simulated annealing.
• Genetic algorithms.
• Diﬀusion methods.
• Distance geometry methods.
A light introduction to these methods can be found in Jensen [17, Chapter 12]. In this
project the conﬁgurations are sampled using Molecular Dynamics (MD).
We have found that the PCM does in fact constrain the optimization calculation, and
a lower minimum is found by a pre-relaxation in vacuum. It has a stabilizing eﬀect and
should be included in the calculations.
3.6
Sampling by Molecular Dynamics Simulations and Clus-
ter Analyses
The molecular dynamics (MD) method solves Newtons equation of motion for a system
of molecules interacting through an empirical forceﬁeld [12, 15]. Compared to ab initio
methods, this is computationally a very cheap method, which enables the simulation of
the time evolution of large systems from ps to µs time-scales. The time evolution of
a system is calculated by integrating the equation of motion and MD simulations can
generate trajectory ﬁles containing millions of frames with information on the atomic
positions and velocities.
The quality of the result and the computation time of an
MD simulation depends strongly on the quality of the force-ﬁeld description.
Using
statistical mechanics, MD can be used to obtain macroscopic observable such as pressure,
temperature and heat capacity.
MD simulations are employed in many branches of
science, including protein folding pathways in solution [84].
==PAGE:112==
Chapter 3. A Phosphate Binding Peptide
98
A disadvantage of the force-ﬁeld is that the system is restricted to a single molecular
connectivity, which means that it cannot describe the formation and breaking of bonds
in chemical reactions [15].
Another drawback is that the electrostatic properties of
molecules are represented using point charges at the nuclear sites [11].
An example of an inter-atomic potential is the Lennard-Jones potential
V (r) = 4ϵ
σ
r
12
−
σ
r
6
,
(3.2)
where ϵ is the cohesive energy well depth and σ is the equilibrium distance. The (σ/r)12
term describes the repulsive force due to overlapping of electron orbitals (Pauli repulsion)
and the (σ/r)6 term describes the attractive Van der Waals force.
An important aspect in MD simulations of peptides is whether the conformational space
of the system is adequately sampled. To meet this, all important conformational states
should be sampled as to resemble the real Boltzmann-weighted ensemble. When this is
the case one can calculate various thermodynamic properties. However for our purpose
it is important because MD is used as a conformational sampling technique, and we need
a good representation of the conformational space to make valid conclusions.
With modern computer power it is, however, still diﬃcult to obtain proper converged
ensembles [11]. This is especially diﬃcult if the PES of the system is characterized by
large barriers, which is the case when several biomolecules are present [85]. The system
at hand poses these kind if diﬃculties where the SGAGKT peptide is combined with an
anion.
One way of countering these diﬃculties is by accelerated MD (aMD), where a bias po-
tential is introduced. It lowers the height of local energy barriers and samples the con-
formational space faster than conventional MD (cMD). In order to obtain the correctly
weighed ensemble, a reweighing have to be introduced.
In work of the project supervisor, Mathias F. Gruber [11], MD and semi-empirical
quantum mechanics (QM) in a hybrid approach (QM/MM) (see the Amber 14 reference
manual [12]) has been applied to simulate how the phosphate anion HPO4
2– is bound
by the SGAGKT-peptide under the explicit inﬂuence of water molecules (thousands of
water molecules are included). The QM/MM approach enables a small part of the system
to be described more rigorously than the remaining system described by MD. In the
==PAGE:113==
Chapter 3. A Phosphate Binding Peptide
99
simulations the anion is described by QM, allowing it to polarize, which is traditionally
not allowed in MD.
The use of the QM/MM approach in combination with aMD in [11], enabled the in-
vestigation of the binding modes of phosphate anions by the SGAGKT peptide, and
provided well-converged ensembles of both the anion-peptide complex and the peptide
alone. See [11] for computational details. The resulting trajectory ﬁles where sorted
into clusters representing the most occurring conformations of the complex/peptide-
only simulations. The complex was clustered using the DBScan algorithm [86] and the
peptide using an agglomerative hierarchical algorithm. In this project, DBScan clusters
from four peptide-HPO4
2– 100 ns aMD simulations will be used to represent the con-
formational space of the many diﬀerent binding states possible. These are shown in ﬁg.
3.14a and amounts to 50 clusters. Hierarchical clusters from two peptide-only 1 µs cMD
simulations will be used to represent the conformational space of the peptide. These are
shown in ﬁg. 3.14b and also amounts to 50 clusters.
(a) Peptide-anion DBScan clusters.
(b) Peptide-only hierarchical clusters.

The
diﬀerent geometries will be relaxed in Gaussian and provide energies of the complex
and of the isolated peptide. The average energy will provide a more reliable reference
energy of the peptide and the complex, than used in sec. 3.4 and 3.5. This in turn
should provide a reliable binding energy where the standard deviation can be estimated.

3.7
Phosphate Binding Peptide with Foundation in Molec-
ular Dynamics and DFT
In the previous secs. 3.2-3.5, we have studied the interaction between the SGAGKT
peptide and a phosphate anion. The results of each section point to a favourable binding
energy, where the anion is bound by strong hydrogen bonds to the peptide. In this
section, the aim is to calculate an average binding energy using conformations obtained
by cluster analyses of MD simulations.
Clusters from four aMD simulations on the
complex and two MD simulation on the peptide are available, and the details of the
MD simulations were presented in sec. 3.6. Clusters of the peptide-anion complex were
obtained using the DB-scan algorithm, and in all clusters the anion is bound to the
peptide. The peptide clusters were obtained using the hierarchical cluster algorithm.
As the aMD/MD simulations converged (see sec. 3.6), the clusters can be considered an
exhaustive representation of the conformation space of the complex and of the peptide.
In total there are 50 clusters of the complex and 50 clusters of the peptide alone.
3.7.1
Method
The approach for calculating the binding energy is to relax every cluster and calculate the
resulting energy. The mean value of the complex and the peptide energies, will be used
to calculate the energy gain by binding. The variance of energies will be a measure of the
uncertainty of the binding energy. Additionally CP correction calculations are performed
on every relaxed complex-cluster. Unfortunately it was out of scope to minimize all the
100 clusters. For the complex, 4 clusters of the 1st aMD simulations were minimized, 4
of the 2nd, 5 of the 3rd and 8 from the 4th. In total 21 clusters of the complex, randomly
chosen. For the isolated peptide, only 15 of the 1st MD simulation were minimized.
As found in sec. 3.5 on the role of solvent in geometry optimizations, a few kcal/mol
can be gained by a preliminary relaxation in vacuum. Consequently the clusters are
ﬁrst optimized using B3LYP/3-21+G in vacuum and subsequently using B3LYP/6-
31++G(d,p) in water. For the peptide-anion complex clusters, some of the calculations
failed in vacuum. This can be ascribed to the initial geometries, as they are not optimal
for a vacuum calculation. The clusters are generated from MD simulations with explicit
water, and not in a gas phase (vacuum). All the complex-clusters that failed reported the
==PAGE:115==
Chapter 3. A Phosphate Binding Peptide
101
same type of error message related to an error in the optimizing algorithm. A discussion
of the error can be found in app. I. When a calculation failed it was directly optimised
using B3LYP/6-31++G(d,p) in water instead.
Setting up these many geometry optimizations of diﬀerent structures was done using a
Python script. As this is not relevant to the conclusions of this section, the set-up of
a Python script is introduced in app. E. It involves; generating Gaussian input ﬁles
from .pdb-ﬁles, and automatically submitting the Gaussian jobs to the HPC center.
The CP calculations were performed after the clusters were minimized. This required
a little trick as a new calculation using the optimized structures had to be set up with
fragments speciﬁed. Using the Gaussian utility newzmat, a ’.pdb’ ﬁle can be extracted
from the ’.chk’ ﬁle output of the ﬁnal optimization calculation, by the following Bash
command:
newzmat -ichk -opdb mychkfile.chk mypdbfile.pdb
From this ’.pdb’ ﬁle, a CP calculation Gaussian input ﬁle can be constructed. The
peptide and anion fragments are speciﬁed, and the calculations are performed at the
B3LYP/6-31++G(d,p) level. The energy of the anion was obtained by an optimization
using B3LYP/6-31++G(d,p) in water.
In addition, results are presented from another similar binding energy calculation. This
calculation uses clusters from earlier MD simulations, that did not reach convergence.
32 complex DB-scan clusters are obtained from single aMD simulation. They are relaxed
by a single minimization in water, using the 6-31+G(d) basis set on the peptide and
the 6-31++G(d,p) basis set on the anion. The SP energies and the CP corrections are
subsequently calculated, using the 6-31++G(d,p) basis set on the entire complex. 25
peptide hierachial-clusters are obtained from single MD simulation. They are relaxed
by a single minimization in water, using the 6-31+G(d) basis set, and the SP energies
are subsequently calculated, using the 6-31++G(d,p) basis set. This calculation was
performed before the system was thoroughly tested, and it was at the time unclear
whether the results of the calculation were valid. However, the resulting binding energies
are very close in the two calculations, which indicate that the system is not too sensitive
to the minimization path or the initial geometry.
==PAGE:116==
Chapter 3. A Phosphate Binding Peptide
102
3.7.2
Results
The results from the vacuum-water two-step minimization of the well-converged clusters
are shown in tab. 3.5. The resulting binding energy is −0.0378 ± 0.00646 hartree or
−23.74±4.05 kcal/mol. The standard deviation (SD) of the binding energy is calculated
as the square root of the average of the variances (SD’s squared) of the energies involved,
SD(Bind) =
p
(V ar(Complex) + V ar(Peptide) + V ar(BSSE)) /3.
Thus the binding
energy is signiﬁcantly negative. In addition to the average binding energy and the SD,
tab. 3.5 also includes the strongest and the weakest binding. The strongest binding
energy is calculated using the most stable complex energy, and the least stable peptide
energy.
Conversely, the weakest binding energy is calculated using the least stable
complex energy, and the most stable peptide energy. The strongest binding energy is
-47.76 kcal/mol and the weakest is -2.79 kcal/mol. Both energies are far outside the SD
of the mean energy, and they stress the importance of calculating the binding energy as
an average.

The validity of the MD clusters.
Having relaxed the QM/MM aMD clusters, we
can make an assessment of how well the MD simulations captures the conformations at-
tained by the peptide-anion complex. This is under the assumption that the B3LYP/6-
31++G(d,p)-relaxed clusters are closer to the true conformations than the QM/MM
clusters. The B3LYP simulation is clearly more detailed than a QM/MM aMD simu-
lation that employs forceﬁelds on the peptide. However, in the B3LYP simulation the
water is included implicitly, and the temperature is eﬀectively 0◦K. Thus the micro-
scopic interaction with a water-solvent at 300◦K is not well-produced, in contrast to
QM/MM MD methods.
With this in mind, the validity of the MD simulation was investigated by comparing
RMSD values between the structures before and after minimization. This quantiﬁes how
much the initial structure changes after a geometry optimization. This was done for the
21 complex structures that all came from converged QM/MD aMD simulations. The
average RMSD (in ˚A) is found to be 1.18 ± 0.55, and the maximum value was 2.33 and
the minimum was 0.50. Fig. 3.15a shows aligned before/after structures for the highest-
RMSD structure and ﬁg. 3.15b shows the lowest-RMSD structure. By inspection of the
22 clusters, it was found that the the lysine chain is responsible for most of the change
(also seen in ﬁg. 3.15a). As the lysine chain is highly active in the phosphate binding it
seems that the QM/MD simulation does not capture all details. As the average RMSD
is very low, the QM/MM aMD simulations are, in general, performing with an accuracy
close to a quantum level. It is noteworthy that the structure that changed the least, is
also the structure that ended up in the lowest minimum. The structures in the deepest
minima, are interpreted as the most frequent and stable structures. At least at low
==PAGE:118==
Chapter 3. A Phosphate Binding Peptide
104
temperatures, where entropy is less signiﬁcant. This shows, that one should sample as
many clusters as possible.
(a) Highest RMSD, 2.33 ˚A.
(b) Lowest RMSD, 0.50 ˚A.
Figure 3.15: Comparison of MD structures before and after structural relaxation.(A)
The structure that changed the most during the minimization. The biggest change is
in the bending of the lysine chain. It end in a minimum around the average energy:
-2488.767 hatree. (B) The structure that changed the least during the minimization. It
is also the structure that found the deepest minimum: -2488.780 hartree. This indicates
that the QM/MM aMD simulation, is capable of providing clusters with accuracy near
the B3LYP/6-31++G(d,p) level. In both ﬁgures, hydrogen is white, oxygen is red,
nitrogen is blue, phosphor is orange and the carbon atoms are teal in the initial structure
and green in the minimized structure.
3.7.3
Discussion and Conclusion
Using a large number of diﬀerent clusters, a representative average energy for the peptide
and the peptide-HPO4
2– complex has been calculated. A binding energy is found, which
is almost the same as was calculated for the hypothesised binding nest in sec.
3.4.
However, it is by far a more valid number, as the binding energy encompasses most of
the diﬀerent conformations, attained in the MD simulations. From two diﬀerent sets of
MD simulations, two binding energies were calculated, and the average binding energy
of the two calculations, is about 24.5 kcal/mol. The B3LYP is estimated, on average,
to have errors in binding energy of about 2 kcal/mol in systems highly governed by
dispersive forces [87]. Therefore in a conservative estimate, we conclude that the gain
in electronic energy, in the phosphate binding, is 24, 5 ± 4.5 kcal/mol.
==PAGE:119==
Chapter 3. A Phosphate Binding Peptide
105
We have veriﬁed, that the MD input geometries are only slightly changed in the B3LYP/6-
31++G(d,p) relaxation, with an average RMSD of 1.18 ± 0.55. Thus the QM/MM MD
methods provides a good description of the phosphate-peptide complex, and the many
statistical properties that can be extracted from MD, are therefore rendered more reli-
able. This is an important result as explicit water, included in MD, often play a vital
role in peptide and proteins systems.
The binding energy indeed shows a favourable binding, but it is far oﬀthe experimental
results of Bianchi et al. [1], reported in sec. 3.1. They report a favourable Gibbs free
energy of -4 kcal/mol and an endothermic enthalpy of 1 kcal/mol. So how does these
numbers relate? To answer this we have to understand the thermodynamics of a solute
peptide-anion complex.
3.8
Thermodynamics of the Peptide-Anion Complex
Here a link between the binding energy computed in this chapter, and the experimental
ﬁndings of Bianchi et al. [1] concerning enthalpy, entropy and Gibbs free-energy, will
be provided. Let us assume, for the sake of simplicity, that the binding energy was
calculated in vacuum. In that case, the binding energy is the gain in electronic energy,
∆Eel, at zero kelvin under the Born-Oppenheimer approximation. Thus in this kind
of energy calculation, the molecular vibrations are ignored. This is actually a severe
constraint, even at zero kelvin in vacuum, because we ignore the so-called zero-point
vibrational energy (ZPVE).
3.8.1
Zero-Point Vibrational Energy
We denote the binding-energy contribution of the ZPVE as ∆ZPVE, which is the dif-
ference in ZPVE between products and reactants in a reaction.
The ZPVE can be
understood from the nuclear Hamiltonian in the BO approximation:
[Tn + Ee(R)] Φ = EΦ,
(3.3)
where Tn is the nuclear kinetic energy operator, Ee(R) is the PES including electronic
energy and nuclear-nuclear repulsion, as function of nuclear coordinates R. Φ is the
==PAGE:120==
Chapter 3. A Phosphate Binding Peptide
106
total nuclear wavefunction. When the molecule is in a minimum on the PES, the PES
can be approximated by a harmonic potential for small displacements [51]. Thus the
energy in eqn. (3.3) is approximately the energy corresponding to the solution of the
harmonic oscillator problem: E = hν(n + 1
2), where the ground-state (n = 0) yields
a non-zero energy.
This means that within the BO approximation, the energy of a
molecule in vacuum at zero K is not only the electronic energy, but also the kinetic
energy of the vibrating nuclei.
As temperature rises, higher and higher vibrational
eigenstates becomes occupied, and the overall energy of the molecule increases from
absorption of thermal energy. This gives a third energy contribution, ∆Evib,therm, namely
the change in vibrational energy in going from 0 to 298.15 K. At ﬁnite temperature
the molecular energy levels arise from molecular translation, rotation, vibration, and
electronic excitation (assuming that the terms can be decoupled). At room temperature,
the thermal energy amounts to about 0.70 hartree, and electronic excitations are not
attained.
Returning to the enthalpy; in vacuum at 0 K, the change in enthalpy of a the peptide-
anion binding reaction, would be the change in electronic energy (calculated in the above
sections) plus the change in ZPVE.
3.8.2
Enthalpy
The enthalpy is a thermodynamic properties, related to a large number of molecules.
However, using statistical mechanics, the thermodynamic properties can be inferred
from quantum mechanical calculations of individual molecules. In fact, by the results of
statistical mechanics, the translational and rotational contribution to the enthalpy, can
be treated classically and calculated directly [88]. The translational contribution only
depends on mass and temperature, and the rotational only depends on geometry and
temperature, and we denote the energy contribution ∆Etrans + ∆Erot.
Thus from the point of a quantum mechanical calculation, the change in enthalpy of a
reaction at room temperature and 1 bar, can be calculated as [37]:
∆H◦
298.15 = ∆Eel + BSSE + ∆ZPVE + ∆Evib,therm + ∆Etrans + ∆Erot + ∆nRT, (3.4)
==PAGE:121==
Chapter 3. A Phosphate Binding Peptide
107
where ∆nRT accounts for the change in the number (∆n = −1) of moles of gas. At
298.15 K it amounts to 0,592 kcal/mol. The absolute enthalpy, H, of a molecule can
be obtained by the frequency job in Gaussian [51]. This is the enthalpy relative to
inﬁnitely separated electrons and nuclei. In an experiment, the enthalpy is calculated
as the diﬀerence in heat of formation ∆Hf of the products and reactants. The enthalpy
of a reaction is calculated from the diﬀerences:
∆H◦
298.15 = ∆Hf(product) −∆Hf(reactant) = H(product) −H(reactant)
(3.5)
Thus, for the peptide-anion complex, it is no surprise to ﬁnd discrepancies between the
calculated ∆Eel + BSSE = 24, 5 ± 4 and the measured ∆H◦
298.15 = 1.0 ± 0.5 of Bianchi
et al. [1].
In a study, from Kon´e et al. [37], on enthalpies of hydrogen-bonded complexes of
methanol with neutral bases and anions, the ∆Eel was found to be the dominant term in
the enthalpy expression. They also report, that the change in vibrational energy between
the products and reactants, ∆ZPVE and ∆Evib,therm, are positive quantities. Therefore
they tend to reduce the enthalpy. This explains why ∆Eel is much lower than ∆H◦
298.15.
Also, this means that if a diﬀerence in ∆Eel between the phosphate and the sulphate
binding reaction can be conﬁrmed, it could explain the selectivity of the peptide.
For a reaction to run, it must not only be favourable for the isolated molecule, but
also for the surroundings as a whole. We use the Gibbs free-energy to describe this
interaction.
3.8.3
Entropy and Gibbs Free-Energy
A good interpretation of the free-energy comes from Williamson [89]:
The entropy of a system is deﬁned as
S = k ln W,
(3.6)
where W is the number of ways in which the system can be arranged, and k is Boltz-
mann’s constant. The Second Law of Thermodynamics says that a reaction will run if
the overall entropy of the universe increase. In a molecule, every degree of freedom such
==PAGE:122==
Chapter 3. A Phosphate Binding Peptide
108
as bond rotation or vibration, has an associated entropy. Therefore entropy is often
described as the amount of disorder in a system. The Second Law says that you end up
in the state that is the most probable one, that is, the one with the highest entropy. The
entropy of a reaction comes in two parts: the entropy change of the reacting molecules
and the entropy change in the surroundings. We write the ﬁrst contribution as ∆S.
The change in the environment comes from the heat released (or absorbed) in the reac-
tion. This is the enthalpy ∆H. In an exothermic reaction, the surroundings are heated
which gives more random thermal motion and increases the entropy. This change in
entropy also depends on temperature T; the impact of the heat released in the reaction
is larger for a cold environment. Therefore the entropy change of the surroundings can
be described as −∆H/T. The minus sign indicates that an exothermic reaction (with
negative ∆H), increases the entropy. Thus the total change in entropy is the sum of
these contributions, and we denote this by −∆G. This is what determines whether a
reaction is favourable or not, and to put it in units of energy we divide by T and obtain:
∆G/T = ∆H/T −∆S or
∆G = ∆H −T∆S.
(3.7)
For a reaction to occur, the change in energy must be favourable (increases the entropy
of the surrounding) and it must be probable (increases the molecular entropy). For the
peptide-anion binding, this means that the binding will occur if the binding energy is
favourable, but not if it restricts the conformational freedom too much. A consequence
of the anion binding is that it tends to lock the complex in a binding conformation [90].
At standard conditions, the isolated peptide is a multi-conformational molecule, dynam-
ically changing state. Thus, there is a free-energy penalty for the loss of conformational
states when it binds the anions, which is taken into account by Gibbs free-energy.
This brings us to the role of solvent, which is most conveniently discussed in terms of
free-energy.
3.8.4
Role of Solvent
In the implicit water models, the free energy of a solvent is usually described by the
electrostatic interaction between the solvent and the solute and a set of non-electrostatic
contributions [61]:
==PAGE:123==
Chapter 3. A Phosphate Binding Peptide
109
Gsol = Gel + Grep + Gdis + Gcav.
(3.8)
Gel is, within PCM, composed of the electrostatic interaction energy between solute and
solvent, and the polarization work spent to create the charge density inside the dielectric.
Grep and Gdis represent the contribution from repulsion and dispersion interactions,
respectively. Gcav is the cavitation energy and it represents the work necessary to create
a void cavity within the dielectric to host the solute. This represents the partition of
energy contributions to the free-energy in the PCM. The accuracy of this model was out
of scope to investigate, as ab-initio thermodynamics within a PCM is a large topic, with
many comprehensive semi-empirical approximations.
In a more broad sense of the role of solvent, we can gain insight by considering a
water solvent where a hydrophilic molecule is introduced. The water molecules will gain
electronic energy by forming hydrogen bonds to the molecule. As the hydrogen bonds are
formed, the water molecules are locked, and the water solvent is forced to adopt a more
ordered state around the molecule and the bound water molecules. Thus favourable
enthalpy and unfavourable entropy terms are associated with hydrogen bonding.
A
hydrophilic molecule is dissociated in water if the enthalpy gain compensates for the
loss of entropy.
In a binding reaction of two hydrophilic species, the surface area for hydrogen bonding
to water is signiﬁcantly decreased in the binding. This feature is known as desolvation
as the binding energy is associated with the displacement of former hydrogen-bound
water molecules. If the conformation of the two binding hydrophilic species are not
too restricted, this process is associated with an increase in entropy. The change in
enthalpy is more complicated, as it involves a competition between the enthalpy energy
gain of binding and the enthalpy energy loss of loosing hydrogen bonds. It is thus balance
between large energies with small diﬀerences. In the case of the HPO4
2–-peptide binding
of Bianchi et al. [1], they reported an unfavourable enthalpy loss of 1 kcal/mol that was
countered by a favourable entropy gain of 5 kcal/mol. In their work they understand this
slightly unfavourable H and favourable S as being a consequence of desolvation eﬀects
in binding processes.
We can estimate the eﬀect of desolvation by calculating the change in enthalpy and free
energy of the HPO4
2–-peptide binding reaction, in water and in vacuum. However, using
==PAGE:124==
Chapter 3. A Phosphate Binding Peptide
110
the PCM could be a rough approximation in this case, as the solute-solvent interaction
seems to be a molecular-scale eﬀect of the ﬁrst solvation shell. In the following section
the result of a series of frequency calculations are presented to investigate the change in
enthalpy and free energy, and to estimate the eﬀect of desolvation.
3.8.5
Results of Frequency Calculations
Tab. 3.7 and tab. 3.8 shows the results of frequency calculations of the system in water
and vacuum, respectively.
It includes three calculations; one of the HPO4
2–-peptide
complex, one of the peptide and one of the HPO4
2–-anion. The frequency calculations
are performed at the B3LYP/6-31++G(d,p) level, and a scaling factor of 0.964 are
applied, which is appropriate for frequency calculations at this particular level of theory.
The structures used were the ones with the lowest energy, in the ﬁndings of sec. 3.7.
The diﬀerence between the average electronic energy of the corresponding ensemble, and
the energy of the particular structure is reported in the tables.
By comparing the values in the tables, several interesting properties can be derived.
The vibrational energy contributions are very close in the two models and the biggest
change when including the water model is in the ground-state electronic energy. There-
fore we also ﬁnd an enthalpy gain very close to the earlier calculated binding energies:
∆H◦
298.15 = 24.4 kcal/mol. From tab. 3.7 we ﬁnd that taking into account the thermal
corrections to the enthalpy, does not produce an enthalpy gain closer to the results of
Bianchi et al. [1]. However, it shows that the gain in electronic energy is by far the
most important contribution to the enthalpy change. In fact, the thermal corrections to
the enthalpy cancel out. The change in free-energy is higher than change in enthalpy
by about 15 kcal/mol. Thus a signiﬁcantly unfavourable contribution to the entropy is
predicted. Remember that here the microscopic eﬀects of the water is not included, and
thus this is the entropy change of the complex. As mentioned an unfavourable entropy
change expected in a binding reaction, which is countered by a favourable entropy in-
crease of the desolvated water molecules. This entropy contribution to the desolvation
eﬀect is thus not included in the model.
The desolvation aﬀect on the enthalpy change can, however, be obtained. By comparing
the change in enthalpy in vacuum and in water, we ﬁnd that the gain in enthalpy is
ten times larger in vacuum than in water. Water has a stabilizing eﬀect on both the

All in all, after including all the thermodynamic corrections to the gain in enthalpy,

to cancel. The change electronic energy is a good qualitative measure of the binding
energy.
We can use these ﬁndings in the study of the sulphate binding, as, according to Williamson
[89], the entropy contribution is almost between to similar structures binding diﬀerent
ligands.

3.9
Selectivity and Sulphate Binding
In the experimental work of Bianchi et al. [1], the phosphate aﬃnity of the peptide
was compared to the sulphate aﬃnity.
It was found that the peptide discriminates
the two anions and favours phosphate. As this feature has applications in a selective
phosphate-capturing device, it would be interesting to reproduce this feature.
The phosphate aﬃnity of the peptide has been established as a gain in electronic energy.
However, we have also seen that the endothermic enthalpy change reported in Bianchi
et al. [1], could not be reproduced. The discrepancies between the two numbers can be
ascribed to three contributions:
1. It seems that the ﬁrst solvation-shell of the water plays a large part in the enthalpy
change, as it is dominated by a desolvation eﬀect.
The desolvation is an un-
favourable contribution to the enthalpy change, and it could in fact be reproduced
by the PCM. However large uncertainty is expected as the desolvation involves the
breaking of an integer number of hydrogen bonds, and this is not captured in a
continuum model.
==PAGE:127==
Chapter 4. Sulphate Binding Energy
113
2. The experimental solution was a 0.1M NMe4Cl aqueous solution. Discrepancies
between this and a continuum description of pure water is to be expected.
3. The B3LYP functional does not capture dispersion energy. However, if the error
ascribed to the lack of dispersion is constant, we can safely compare results from
the phosphate-peptide complex to results from the sulphate-peptide complex. We
may not be able to provide an estimate for the measurable enthalpy or free-energy
change in the sulphate-binding reaction, but we can asses which of the two binding
reactions that are the most favourable.
Assuming that inferences can be made from diﬀerences in binding energies and en-
thalpies between the binding of the two anion species, the results of a binding energy
and a frequency calculation of the SGAGKT peptide, and the SO4
2– sulphate anion,
is presented. Fig. 3.16 shows the electron density of the binding complex. As for the
phosphate binding, short hydrogen bonds are observed, which suggests a strong binding.
However, compared to the phosphate electron-density, shown in ﬁg. 3.12, we ﬁnd that
there is less overlap in electron density. This suggests a sulphate binding that is weaker
than the phosphate binding.
3.9.1
Method
The method for calculating the binding energy is similar to the previous sections on
phosphate. Four clusters of the ﬁrst aMD simulation of the peptide-phosphate clusters
are used. Each pdb ﬁle is modiﬁed by changing phosphor (P) to sulphur (S) and by
removing the phosphate hydrogen. These four peptide-sulphate complexes are used for
estimating the complex energy. As the initial geometries were optimal for the phosphate
complex, they may not provide the best initial geometry as sulphate complexes. But
as seen in the previous chapter, the minimization is, in this case, not that sensitive to
initial geometry. The binding energy is calculated more as a quick estimate, rather than
an exhaustive study.
Each sulphate complex is minimized in two steps: First with B3LYP/3-21+G in vacuum
followed by B3LYP/6-31++G(d,p) in water.
CP corrections are used to obtain the
BSSE. The sulphate anion energy is found by building the anion in Avogadro and
==PAGE:128==
Chapter 4. Sulphate Binding Energy
114
Figure 3.16: Electron density in sulphate binding. The electron density is an iso-
surface containing 93% of the electron density. It is obtained from the ﬁnalized check-
point (fchk) ﬁle [70] of the Gaussian optimization calculation, and visualized using
Avogadro. Sulphate is yellow, carbon is grey, hydrogen is white, nitrogen is blue and
oxygen is red.
relaxing it with B3LYP/6-31++G(d,p) in water. The peptide energy has been calculated
in sec. 3.7.
Frequencies are calculated for the complex-cluster that was minimized to the lowest
energy. The peptide frequencies are identical to the ones calculated in sec. 3.8.5. The
calculations was performed at the B3LYP/6-31++G(d,p) level with a PCM for water
and a scale factor of 0.964, which is appropriate for this level of theory.
3.9.2
Results
The results of the calculations are shown in tab. 3.9, and the binding energy is −21.63±
2.91 kcal/mol. The binding energy of the phosphate complex, reported in tab. 3.5, is
−23.74 ± 4.05. Thus even though the average binding strength of sulphate is lower than
phosphate, the SD’s are well overlapping and they are essentially equal.
From the frequency calculation, shown in tab. 3.10, we can draw conclusions very similar
to the frequency calculations on the phosphate complex. The thermal correction to the

==PAGE:130==

the energy diﬀerence between the average electronic energy and electronic energy of the

complex structures were used, whereas in this sulphate investigation only 4 phosphate-
optimal sulphate complexes were in consideration. It is therefore to be expected that the
phosphate-complex ﬁnds a lower minimum than the sulphate-complex, and this error
should be corrected for.
From the table, we ﬁnd that the enthalpy of the binding reaction for phosphate, is lower
than the sulphate-enthalpy, by about 1.5 kcal/mol if we correct for the energy diﬀerence

draw conclusions from this.

Large uncertainties are present in this investigation. It is unclear how the standard devi-
ation of the electronic energies should be taken into account. It is the general consensus
that a molecule will be found in the lowest energy state as possible. But we know from
the MD simulations that the complex is dynamically changing its conformation. If we
accept the lowest energy state as the reference energy, then these results points to a high
binding selectivity of the peptide, favouring phosphate binding.
On the other hand, if we use the average electronic energy of both the phosphate and
the sulphate complex, the diﬀerence in the change in enthalpy for the two systems will
be of the order 1.5 ± 3 kcal/mol, slightly favouring phosphate binding. It could be that
this slight preference towards phosphate binding could change the overall macroscopic
dynamics of the system in such a way that the peptide overall favours binding phosphate
over sulphate.
==PAGE:131==
Chapter 4. Sulphate Binding Energy
117
To make sure, the eﬀect of desolvation should be explicitly included, to obtain the free-
energy of the reaction.
3.10
Phosphate Recovery Applications
From the studies presented, it is clear that the peptide has aﬃnity for binding phosphate,
and that the proposed binding nest of Bianchi et al. [1] is an energetically favourable
complex conformation. We have seen the vague indications of preference for binding
phosphate over sulphate, which is in line with the experimental ﬁnding of Bianchi et al.
[1], who reported aﬃnity for phosphate, but not for sulphate.
Assuming that we have a selective phosphate binding hexapeptide, we shall now address
how it could be employed in a device. Two ideas are presented:
• The ﬁrst idea relies on ﬁxing the peptide in a tank. The peptides can be bound to
the larger protein complex of streptavidin-biotin, allowing to ﬁx the hexapeptide
in a non-invasive manner.
The idea relies on saturating the peptide bound in
the tank, with phosphate anions from a contaminated solution, and subsequently
dissociating the anions in pure water. The device is sketched in ﬁg. 3.17; the
ﬁrst step is to ﬁll the tank with the phosphate-containing solution from which
the phosphate anions are to be extracted. This solution is allowed to circulate
for an optimal time, until the most viable percentage of the peptides bind one
or more phosphate anions. The solution can consist of all kinds of species, and
therefore selectivity is important. The solution is ﬂushed out, and the tank is
ﬁlled with pure water. The pure water is circulated for an optimal time, until
the most viable percentage of the peptides has dissociated their phosphate anions.
The water-phosphate solution is ﬂushed out to an appropriate storage container.
• The second consists of embedding the peptides in an interlocked matrix. As a
phosphate solution is ﬂushed through the porous matrix, the embedded peptides
will bind the phosphate.
The phosphate anions are now bound in the matrix,
and so loosely, that they can be released in a controlled manner by an external
perturbation. An example is shown in ﬁg. 3.18, that relies on using a hydrogel
matrix that e.g.
could contain the streptavidin-bound peptides.
Hydrogel has
==PAGE:132==
Chapter 4. Sulphate Binding Energy
118
tunable properties through a capability to hold large amounts of water. But the
choice of matrix material could in principle fall on any class of polymeric materials.
Figure 3.17: Sketch of a phosphate recovery device, that relies on ﬁxing the peptide
in a tank. A solution from which phosphate is to be extracted is ﬁrst introduced. After
the peptides have bound the phosphate, the solution is ﬂushed out and substituted
with pure water. The anions dissociate to the water and the water-phosphate solution
is ﬂushed out to an appropriate storage container.
Figure 3.18:
Sketch of phosphate recovery devices, relying on a hydrogel matrix
embedding the peptides. As an external perturbation is applied, the phosphate anions
are released. Modiﬁed from [91].
==PAGE:133==
Chapter 4
Conclusion
Motivated by the recent concern for phosphate depletion, this study investigated a
peptide with particular phosphate aﬃnity. Phosphor depletion may cause widespread
famine, and in order to avoid such crisis, the phosphor cycle must be extensively restruc-
tured. In essence, mineral phosphor is mined and used in fertilizers in the agriculture,
from which a large proportion is lost. The most viable ﬂow of phosphorous loss, to
recover phosphor from, is the loss of organic phosphor from animal wastes. The organic
phosphor must be converted to inorganic phosphate, and subsequently recovered, and it
is in this recovery, that the peptide can be employed. Thus the aim of this study, was to
characterize the potential for the utilization of this peptide, in a biomimetic phosphate
recovering device.
This thesis comprise a computational study of the phosphate binding mechanism of
the peptide.
The hexapeptide in question is reported, in recent literature, to have
experimentally demonstrated capability of phosphate binding. We ﬁnd that the peptide
indeed has aﬃnity for binding, but also that at this stage, large uncertainties delve in
the speciﬁc computational method used.
The proposed peptide, and its interaction with a phosphate anion constituted the system
that was modelled. In a computational study one has to make a trade-oﬀbetween the
system size, and the accuracy of the description of the forces and energy contributions
of the system. In this particular study, it comes down to deciding whether to include the
surrounding water molecules of the system, or to beneﬁt from a detailed quantum me-
chanical description of the polarized electronic density of the phosphate-anion complex.
119
==PAGE:134==
Chapter 5. Conclusion
120
Here, the choice falls on the latter, and the peptide-anion interaction was simulated
using density functional theory (DFT).
The binding mechanism is found to be dominated by hydrogen bonds. In some cases
so strong that they seem to classify as low barrier hydrogen bonds. The peptide-anion
complex is thus dominated dipole interactions, which can be captured by DFT methods.
The framework of quantum mechanics, and the theory of electronic structure has been
presented.
This included the theory of the Born-Oppenheimer approximation, the
Hartree-Fock theory and the DFT. In DFT, a number of methods exists, which are
deﬁned by their approximation to the exchange-correlation (xc)-functional. The choice
of xc-functional was based on the reasoning, that the system was so large that low
computational cost was necessary, while also a good description of hydrogen bonds was
required. These requirements were met by the hybrid-GGA functional ’B3LYP’. It per-
forms well for biological systems, although it has an insuﬃcient description of dispersion
energy, which is important for van der Waals forces.
The molecular wavefunctions of the peptide-anion complex are described by the Pople
style basis set, which is of the family of localized basis functions. Thus the concept of
basis functions was introduced, as well as the theory of calculating a binding energy,
and correcting for the basis set superposition error involved. The DFT simulations were
performed using the computational chemistry software, Gaussian 09 rev. D., which
was introduced along with the job types relevant for this work.
This formed the theoretical tool-box, for the study on the phosphate binding peptide.
The synthetic SGAGKT peptide was introduced, with starting point in an experimental
characterization from Bianchi et al. [1]. From their results, the optimal protonated form
of the peptide was determined. As a the peptide would likely have to bind phosphate
at neutral pH, for a phosphate recovery application, it was found that the zwitterionic
protonated form was optimal.
In addition to binding both HPO4
2– and PO4
3–, the
zwitterionic peptide also showed selectivity, in the sense that it did not bind sulphate.
Bianchi et al. also proposed a binding conformation of the peptide in the fully depro-
tonated form. In this hypothesised conformation, the peptide backbone formed a nest
around the anion, whilst the deprotonated lysine sidechain formed a hydrogen bond to
the HPO4
2–-hydrogen. This conformation formed the basis for describing the binding
==PAGE:135==
Chapter 5. Conclusion
121
mechanism in this study. During the work many similar conformations were found for
the zwitterionic peptide.
It had now been established that the zwitterionic peptide, binding an HPO4
2– anion,
was the system to model. How to model it, was the subject of the following section. A
study on the choice of basis set was presented using two systems; the isolated peptide
and the isolated anion. In the study on the peptide a geometry was used, that was
acquired from an MD simulation. The basis set convergence test on the peptide was
performed by minimizing the MD-structure using diﬀerent basis sets, and comparing the
ﬁnal geometries to a geometry minimized at a larger basis set. The measure of diﬀerence
between the geometries was root-mean-square deviation (RMSD). It was found that
the geometry optimization was quite insensitive to the choice of basis set. However,
conclusions could be drawn, and it was found that the basis set size should minimum be
the 6-31G basis set. Also the inclusion of diﬀuse functions was found more important
than the inclusion of polarization functions. The triple-zeta basis set should be avoided,
as it was computationally too heavy.
The basis set convergence on the HPO4
2–-anion was investigated by calculating the
ground-state energy using diﬀerent basis sets. Simulation time was also a parameter,
and the anion was in its equilibrium geometry. It was found the 6-31++G(d,p) basis
set was the best compromise between accuracy and simulation time.
For the complex, the description of the hydrogen bonds between the anion and the
peptide, was expected to require polarization functions on the peptide. Therefore it was
concluded that the 6-31++G(d,p) constituted an eﬃcient and reliable basis set for the
system at hand.
Additionally, a test on the optimal number of CPUs to use in parallel, in a geometry
optimization, showed that 10 cores performed better than 8 CPUs and just as good as
16 CPUs. Jobs using 10 cores are also better suited for the infrastructure of the HPC
center at DTU, and 10 cores are therefore the optimal number of cores to use in parallel.
With the theory, the model system and the simulation options in place, the phosphate
binding study was presented. It consisted of four diﬀerent simulations. The ﬁrst was a
proof-of-concept investigation, to test if the binding conformation, proposed by Bianchi
et al. [1], could be reproduced solely from the electrostatic interaction between the anion
and the peptide. The peptide was relaxed from a linear conformation with a phosphate
==PAGE:136==
Chapter 5. Conclusion
122
anion in the vicinity. From 24 diﬀerent anion positions, 12 out them resulted in a struc-
ture that resembled the hypothesised binding nest. The structure that most resembled
the proposed binding conformation, was also the structure in the second deepest mini-
mum. This preliminary investigation strongly supported the binding hypothesis, where
the SGAGKT peptide binds the phosphate anion in a backbone nest, and the lysine
residue forms a hydrogen bond to the anion-oxygen. This diﬀers slightly from the pro-
posed conformation where the deprotonated lysine N-terminus forms a hydrogen bond
to the anion-hydrogen.
As the binding nest was conﬁrmed to be a deep minimum in the potential energy sur-
face (PES), the next study consisted of a binding energy calculation on the proposed
nest conformation. The structure was minimized using B3LYP/6-31++G(d,p) and wa-
ter was included by the polarizable continuum model (PCM). The peptide structure
was obtained by removing the anion in the complex structure and relaxing the now
isolated peptide. It was ﬁrst relaxed in vacuum with the 6-31G basis set, and subse-
quently in water using the 6-31++G(d,p) basis set. The counterpoise corrected gain
in electronic energy was -25.24 kcal/mol, corresponding to ﬁve hydrogen bonds, which
also was observed. The phosphate binding energy was indeed favourable, at least in
this conformation. By examining the electron density, a low barrier hydrogen bond was
observed.
The third study of the peptide-phosphate complex, regarded how the minimum attained
in a geometry optimization was aﬀected by the PCM. The peptide complex was opti-
mized from a structure obtained from an MD simulation. It was optimized in four steps,
starting with an optimization in vacuum using the 3-21+G basis set. The three last
steps was in water, with gradually increasing size basis sets. The last basis set was the
6-31++G(d,p), and the ﬁnal structure was compared to a structure obtained by a direct
6-31++G(d,p)-optimization in water. The geometries obtained was almost identical,
but the thoroughly optimized structure had obtained a minimum about 1.3 kcal/mol
deeper. The conclusion was that the PCM model does constrain the optimization job,
but only slightly. It can most likely be corrected for, by a 2 step optimization, ﬁrst
in vacuum using the 3-21+G basis and subsequently in water using the 6-31++G(d,p)
basis set. The binding energy of this conformation was additionally calculated to be
-27.74 kcal/mol.
==PAGE:137==
Chapter 5. Conclusion
123
In the calculated binding energies, a rather arbitrary reference energy for the peptide was
used. The peptide exists as a dynamic ensemble of conformations, jumping in between
many local minima on the PES. In order to draw conclusions on the binding energy, the
peptide and the complex energies must encompass a reasonable sample of the diﬀerent
conformations, that the systems can attain. Therefore, results from MD simulations of
Gruber et al. [11] were used to sample the conformational space of the peptide and the
complex. Peptide-phosphate complex clusters were obtained from converged QM/MM
aMD simulations, where the anion was described by a quantum region. Peptide clusters
were obtained from converged cMD simulations. From a random excerpt of the clusters,
21 out of 50 complex clusters and 15 out of 50 peptide clusters were minimized. This
allowed for average complex and peptide energies with associated standard deviations.
The minimization followed the two-step procedure from above, and the energies were
calculated at a level of B3LYP/6-31++G(d,p), and included the PCM for water. The
standard deviation of the peptide was 4.2 kcal/mol and 5.3 kcal/mol for the complex.
Thus signiﬁcant variation in the ground-state energy as function of input structure, of
both systems, were observed. The average counterpoise corrected binding energy was
calculated to 23.74 ± 4.05 kcal/mol.
An additional similar study had been made using earlier non-converged aMD and cMD
simulations. The structures were minimized directly in water using the 6-31+G(d) on the
peptide and 6-31++G(d,p) on the anion. This less reliable binding energy calculation
resulted in an average counterpoise corrected binding energy of 25.29 ± 3.86 kcal/mol,
calculated at a level of B3LYP/6-31++G(d,p). This stressed that the geometry opti-
mization job is not too sensitive to basis set or PCM model.
The geometrical diﬀerence between initial QM/MM aMD clusters and corresponding
minimized B3LYP/6-31++G(d,p) structures were investigated, to evaluate the accuracy
of the QM/MM aMD model.
With 21 structures included, the average RMSD was
1.18 ± 0.55 ˚A. This RMSD is very low, and the clusters produced in the QM/MM aMD
are in general very reliable. The biggest change observed was in the lysine residue, and
the QM/MM aMD simulations might not adequately capture the role of the lysine in
the binding. This is, however, very uncertain as the MD simulation included the water
solvent explicitly.
In the publication by Bianchi et al. [1] a Gibbs free-energy of the binding reaction was
==PAGE:138==
Chapter 5. Conclusion
124
reported to -4 kcal/mol, and it included an endothermic enthalpy contribution of 1
kcal/mol. The calculated binding energy of about -24 kcal/mol deviated signiﬁcantly
from the experimental ﬁndings, and to understand this diﬀerence, the thermodynamics
of the peptide-anion complex had to be investigated.
The concepts of enthalpy, entropy and free-energy were introduced. It was found that
in the previous binding energy calculations, we had neglected the zero-point vibrational
energy, and the thermal vibrational, translational and rotational contributions to the en-
ergy. The binding energy, that had been calculated in the previous studies, represented
the gain in electronic energy in the binding. From frequency calculations at room tem-
perature, these neglected energy contributions to the enthalpy could be calculated, and
they amounted to about 0.6-0.7 hartree. The enthalpy of the binding reaction, taking
these thermal contributions in consideration, was calculated. It was found that the con-
tributions cancelled, as the enthalpy change was calculated as the enthalpy diﬀerence
between products and reactants. The enthalpy of reaction was calculated to 25 kcal/-
mol, which was virtually identical to change in electronic energy. Clearly this was not
the source of deviation from experiments. It was proposed that the diﬀerence originated
in way the environment was modelled, which lead to a discussion on the role of the sol-
vent. The idea was that the reaction was highly governed by the eﬀects of desolvation.
The binding event of the hydrated peptide and anion species, is associated with the dis-
placement of former hydrogen-bound water molecules. This process is associated with
a favourable increase in entropy of the water molecules, whereas the change in enthalpy
involves a competition between the gain in enthalpy of binding, and the loss in enthalpy
in the breaking of hydrogen bonds. This eﬀect signiﬁcantly lowers the binding energy
as compared to the binding in vacuum, which was also calculated and conﬁrmed using
the PCM for water. The binding strength within the PCM model was decreased to a
tenth of the vacuum binding strength. From these results and considerations, the diﬀer-
ence between the calculated enthalpy change, and the reported experimental enthalpy
change, could best be ascribed to the neglect of the ﬁrst solvation shell in the PCM, and
to the lack dispersion energy in the B3LYP functional.
These results motivated the investigation in the preferential binding of phosphate over
sulphate. If the errors identiﬁed in the PCM and the B3LYP method could be assumed to
be constant, then the diﬀerence in binding aﬃnity for the two anions could be accurately
estimated.
==PAGE:139==
Chapter 5. Conclusion
125
The method for calculating the binding energy was similar to the previous sections on
phosphate. Four clusters of from the aMD simulation of the peptide-phosphate were
used. In each cluster the phosphate anion was substituted with a sulphate anion. These
four peptide-sulphate complexes were minimized in two steps: First with B3LYP/3-
21+G in vacuum followed by B3LYP/6-31++G(d,p) in water. Frequencies were calcu-
lated at the B3LYP/6-31++G(d,p) level with a PCM for water and a scale factor of
0.964, using the complex-cluster that was minimized to the lowest energy. This was also
the parameters used in the other frequency calculation of the phosphate-complex, the
isolated peptide and the isolate anion species. The average gain in electronic energy of
the sulphate-peptide complex was −21.63±2.91 kcal/mol. Thus higher, on average, than
the phosphate-complex, but the uncertainties are too large for any strong conclusions.
On comparing the results from the phosphate and sulphate frequency tests, an enthalpy
favouring phosphate binding was concluded. However in this comparison, no standard
deviation could be evaluated.
Preferential binding for phosphate is clearly indicated, but the conclusions depends on
the interpretation of the calculations. Higher aﬃnity for sulphate can deﬁnitely be ruled
out.
As a ﬁnal perspective, two ideas on using the peptide in a phosphate recovering device
were presented.
The ﬁrst relied on ﬁxing the peptides in a tank where a solution,
from which phosphate should be extracted, is introduced.
After the peptides have
bound the phosphate, the solution is ﬂushed out and substituted with pure water. The
anions dissociate into the water and the water-phosphate solution is ﬂushed out to an
appropriate storage container. The second device-proposal relied on a porous matrix, in
which the peptides could be embedded . The phosphate-containing ﬂuid can be ﬂushed
through such matrix, where the peptide captures the phosphate. The phosphate anions
can subsequently be released by applying an external perturbation.
In the work it has been established, that predicting thermodynamic properties requires
a more detailed description of the solvation shell of the complex, than was attainable in
these calculations. Howerver, the conformations, the phosphate aﬃnity and the anion-
selectivity, of the SGAGKT zwitterionic peptide could be inferred from DFT simulations,
which was used in verifying the description of the QM/MM MD model. It has been
established that in addition to the electronic interaction, the environment of the peptide
==PAGE:140==
Chapter 5. Conclusion
126
plays an important part in the binding mechanism. It is highly governed by desolvation
eﬀects, from which the important energy and entropy contributions are diﬃcult to model
at this level of theory. The nest-like design successfully forms a strong binding to the
anion, and if the peptide could be augmented with hydrophobic residues, the enthalpy
cost of desolvation could perhaps be tuned.
The use of peptides show great potential in phosphate-capturing devices, and could likely
contribute to improving the techniques and practices for reusing the lost phosphorous.
In further computational studies in optimizing the binding strength or selectivity of a
phosphate-binding peptide, both QM/MM and DFT methods should be encompassed, as
both the electrostatic attraction between the peptide and the anion, and the free-energy
contribution of the solvent, plays critical roles in the binding.
==PAGE:141==
Chapter 5
Outlook and Perspectives
This thesis was concerned with a computational study of the phosphate aﬃnity of the
SGAGKT peptide. In the course of work, several subtopics could have been further
explored:
(1) As the quantitative ﬁndings of Bianchi et al. [1] could not be reproduced, it might
be that B3LYP functional does not provide an accurate description of the peptide-anion
complex. Thus the use of other functionals might be interesting to investigate. B3LYP is
by far not the only suitable functional. As the B3LYP lacks a description of dispersion,
xc-functionals that includes empirical dispersion could be assessed. In the selectivity
study of the peptide, with regards to phosphate/sulphate binding, signiﬁcant uncertain-
ties in binding energy where present. This was a consequence of small diﬀerences in
large energies. Thus the selectivity might be more accurately determined with a more
accurate functional.
(2) Another large uncertainty in the work has been on the role of solvent and the
eﬀect of the PCM. It seems that the environment provides energy terms to the free
energy and enthalpy in the order of the binding energy. Therefore it may be wiser to
pursue a description beyond the implicit solvent that can encompass more of the envi-
ronment. Hybrid implicit/explicit solvation models exists, where only the ﬁrst solvation
shell molecules are included. An account for entropy and calculating free energy in an
MD/DFT approach can be found in [92].
127
==PAGE:142==
Chapter 6. Outlook and Perspectives
128
(3) The necessity to employ the methods of thermodynamics was a late ﬁnding, and
consequently a literature study in the methods of ab-initio thermochemistry is a natural
next step.
(4) During the study, it was found that low barrier hydrogen bonds (LBHBs) are a part
of the peptide-anion interaction. LBHBs are associated with selectivity of large proteins
and could also be subject for further study.
(5) It could also be interesting to look into selectivity regarding arsentate anions. Pro-
teins exists that discriminate arsenate and the LBHB plays an important role here [93].
(6) According to Bianchi et al. [1], the phosphate aﬃnity of the peptide was highly
dependent on the protonated form of both the peptide and the anion. It could therfore be
interesting if this feature could be reproduced, and thus look into the diﬀerent protonated
forms of the peptide. However, this requires that a reliable ab-initio method, that can
reproduce experimental ﬁndings, can be established.
(7) Selectivity in anion binding is typically associated with large protein complexes [94],
and experimental work of group member and co- Master Student Sigurd Friis Truelsen,
indicated that simply binding phosphate, in a system with ﬂows, was challenging with
SGAGKT hexapeptide. Consequently it could be interesting to look into larger peptides
using QM/MM methods.

• Spyder - Interactive development environment (IDE) for the Python language.

Using variational theory, Hartree proposed in 1928 a set of self-consistent single-particle

and thus the wavefunctions must be solved in a self-consistent manner. One may start
from a ﬁrst approximation of n(r), construct vH(r) and then solve eqn. (B.9) for the
wavefunctions. Then n(r) is recalculated from the wavefunctions and the circle continues
until the input equals the output (up to a desired, and necessary, accuracy). The above
equations (B.8-B.10) are called the Hartree equations.

Appendix C
Functional Derivative
A functional refers to the mathematical operation of assigning a number to a given
function. Gelfand et al. [96] is a good reference to the theory of functionals.
Formally we say that a functional, F[φ] is a mapping from a normed linear space of
functions M = {φ(x) : x ∈R} to the real or complex numbers F : M →R or C. The
functional derivative, δF[φ]/δφ(x) measures how the value of the functional changes,
if the function φ(x) is changed at the point x. In this way it is the limit of a partial
derivative of a function of several variables, where the number of variables goes to inﬁnite.
In this limit, each point in space represents a variable.
The functional derivative is
deﬁned as
δF[φ] =
Z
dxδF[φ]
δφ(x)δφ(x).
(C.1)
According to this deﬁnition, the total change in F when φ(x) is varied is a linear su-
perposition of the local changes summed over the range of x values. It is common in
the physics community to represent the change in φ(x) as a Dirac delta function δ(x).
The delta function can be used to construct a variation of the function φ(x) which is
localized at point y with strength ϵ:
δφ(x) = ϵδ(x −y).
(C.2)
133
==PAGE:148==
Appendix C. Functional Derivative
134
With this variation the functional derivative become
δF[φ] = F[φ + δφ] −F[φ] = F[φ + ϵδ(x −y)] −F[φ]
=
Z
dxδF[φ]
δφ(x)ϵδ(x −y) = ϵδF[φ]
δφ(y).
(C.3)
In the limit of vanishing ϵ this leads to
δF[φ]
δφ(y) = lim
ϵ→0
F[φ + ϵδ(x −y)] −F[φ]

(C.4)
This formulation is suitable for practical applications.
An example now follows to illustrate the functional derivative. Consider the functional

(C.5)
From eqn. (C.4) we evaluate the functional derivative

It is important that the limit ϵ →0 is taken before the integration is performed.
