Appendix D
Aspects of the GGA-Derivation
This appendix will sum up the basic concepts of the derivation of the generalized gradient
approximation (GGA), where the xc-energy is given by (see eqn. (2.68))
EGGA
xc
=
Z
exc(n(r), |∇n(r)|)n(r)dr.
(D.1)
The most essential concept is the average xc-hole, based on the physical xc-hole given
by [26]
nxc(r, r′) = g(r, r′) −n(r′),
(D.2)
where g(r, r′) is the conditional density at r′ given that one electron is at r. It describes
a charge depletion near r, that is, a hole dug into the average density caused by the
electron at r. The hole is normalized
Z
nxc(r, r′)dr′ = −1,
(D.3)
reﬂecting a complete screening of the electron at r. The hole is localized due to the
combined eﬀect of Pauli principle and the electron-electron interaction.
The derivation comes down to the exact result that the xc-energy can be written as [26]
Exc = 1
2
Z n(r)¯nxc(r, r′)
|r −r′|
drdr′,
(D.4)
135
==PAGE:150==
Appendix D. Aspects of the GGA-Derivation
136
where ¯nxc(r, r′) is the average xc-hole deﬁned by an average between the physical xc-hole
in a non-interacting system (no electron-electron interaction) and in the fully interacting
physical system.
The average xc-hole is a functional of the density, and the term ¯nxc(r,r′)
|r−r′|
is expected to be
very localized. This motivates an expansion of n(˜r) around the point r, which eventually
leads to the gradient expansion of Exc [26]
Exc = ELDA
xc
+
Z
G2(n)(∇n)2dr +
Z
[G4(n)(∇2n)2 + ...]dr + ...,
(D.5)
where G2(n) is a universal functional of n.
When applied to real systems, the gradient expansion has not been particular successful
and has in fact often worsened the results compared to the LDA [26]. It turns out that
in the evaluation of the expansion, some exact conditions required for the xc-hole (for
instance the normalization condition), are easily violated. Imposing these requirements
to functionals that do not originally verify them leads to remarkable improvements
[26], and these modiﬁed gradient expansions have been named the generalized gradient
approximation. It is formally a result of a re-summation of eqn. (D.5) and leads to eqn.
(D.1) shown above.

Appendix E
Gaussian Input, Python Scripts
and Bash Commands
This appendix will go through the optimization procedure in sec. 3.7. The essential
Gaussian input ﬁles and Python scripts are presented, along with examples of data
manipulation in Bash. Most of these scripts are simple, and should give insight in the
practical work of the project and serve as inspiration for a future study.
E.1
Initial Relaxation
The ﬁrst step in obtaining the averaged binding energy, is to relax the MD-clusters in
vacuum. The clusters (also called centroids) are available as .pdb ﬁles. Each .pdb ﬁle
should be read, as it contains the input structure for the minimization.
Below is an example of a Gaussian input ﬁle. %NProcShared=10 means that it will use
10 cores, %Mem is the amount of memory reserved for the calculation, %RWF speciﬁes
the read-write ﬁle which contains enormous amounts of data.
The %NoSave makes
sure that the read-write ﬁle is deleted if the calculation is succesfully converged. %Chk
speciﬁes the checkpoint ﬁle, containing all relevant data of the ﬁnished calculation. The
structure is optimized at B3LYP/3-21+G.

converged and some will have encountered errors. As we need to further optimize the
converged simulations, it is very useful to make a script that can ﬁnd the converged
simulations and save the ﬁlepaths:

In this example, the ”Converged.txt” contains three diﬀerent types of calculations of
several diﬀerent structures. The calculations are the initial and the ﬁnal optimization,
and the counterpoise calculation. This ﬁle can be used to obtain summaries of all the
jobs using appropriate Bash commands. Examples of this will be shown throughout this
appendix.
==PAGE:165==
Appendix E. Gaussian Input, Python Scripts and Bash Commands
151
We now have a list of the converged Gaussian jobs and will proceed to the ﬁnal opti-
mization. For this we need a list of the converged initial geometry optimizations. This
can easily be obtained from the ”Converged.txt” ﬁle:
cat Converged.txt | grep ’/RelaxMDResult’ > ConvergedAndFurtherOptimized.txt
Now we have a list of the structures that need further optimization. The python script
is shown below. It is less commented as much is repeated from above.

”Energies.txt” will contain the energies of the ﬁnal step of the ﬁnal minimization job.
E.3
Counterpoise Correction
The ﬁnal step in calculating the binding energy is to calculate the BSSE of the complex.
In order to do this, we must set up a Gaussian input ﬁle with the ﬁnal minimized
structure. For this structure we have to manually specify the fragments to use in the
CP correction. Thus we cannot use Geom=Checkpoint or Geom=AllCheck.
The approach is to use the built-in Gaussian utility, newzmat, to produce a pdb ﬁle of
the minimized structure. The pdb ﬁle can then be used in a new set up of the input ﬁle,
where fragments are speciﬁed. An input ﬁle looks like:
Filename :
CP FinalOpt Case 0 cluster dbscan centroid 0 . com
%NProcShared=10
%Mem=2GB
%RWF=/SCRATCH/ s092989 / SCR CP FinalOpt Case 0 cluster dbscan centroid 0 . rwf
%NoSave
%Chk=CP FinalOpt Case 0 cluster dbscan centroid 0 . chk
#n B3LYP/6−31++G(d , p)
Counterpoise=2 SCF=(XQC, MaxCycle=512) Pop=Minimal
CP FinalOpt Case 0 cluster dbscan centroid 0

E.4
Bash Commands
SumLog
A really helpful Bash command is
egrep ’out of|SCF Don|Converged| NO |YES|exceeded|Predicted’ NAME.log \ | grep -v ’\\\
It makes a compact summary of each step in the geometry optimization with the log
ﬁle: ”NAME.log”. This can be saved as a script and implemented as a built in Bash
shell command in Linux, see [97].
Save the textﬁle ”SumLog” in /bin:
#!/bin/bash
egrep ’out of|SCF Don|Converged| NO |YES|exceeded|Predicted’ $1 \ | grep -v ’\\\\’
Make it executable using
chmod a+x SumLog
and add it to the PATH:
export PATH=$PATH:~/bin
Now a summary of a geometry optimization can be obtained by writing
SumLog NAME.log
Submitting multiple jobs
Submit from current folder:
for i in $(find . -name "sh"); do qsub $i; done
==PAGE:175==
Appendix E. Gaussian Input, Python Scripts and Bash Commands
161
This ﬁnds the submitting scripts with the suﬃx ”.sh”
One can also submit from subfolders:
for i in $(ls */ -d); do cd $i; qsub $(ls *.sh); cd .. ; done
Deleting multiple jobs
Sometimes a long queue of jobs can be faulty started. In
such case it can be tedious to delete every job, one job at the time. An elegant way to
do it is using the seq command to generate the sequence number:
qdel $(seq -f "%.0f" JOB-IDstart JOB-IDstop)
The seq will produce a sequence of numbers
$ echo ‘seq -f "%.0f" 7823111 7823118‘
7823111 7823112 7823113 7823114 7823115 7823116 7823117 7823118
which corresponds to the JOB-ID’s deleted.
==PAGE:176==
Appendix F
Peptide Basis Set Convergence
Test
This appendix presents the results of an additional basis set test convergence test for
the peptide, as a supplement to the study in sec. 3.2.1. As in sec. 3.2.1, the structure
resulting from the minimization with the 6-311++G(d,p) basis set is used as reference,
and the structures minimized using the other basis sets are aligned to this. Fig. F.1
shows the RMSD results, and ﬁg.
F.2 shows the resulting structures aligned to the
reference structure.
As in sec. 3.2.1 the general trend is that the larger the basis set, the better the per-
formance, but with many exceptions. For instance, the 4-31G basis set performs better
than the 6-31+G(d,p). As all atoms are included in the RMSD calculation, and not just
backbone atoms, one should keep in mind that an RMSD value below 0.6 is acceptable.
Most of the resulting geometries are below this threshold, which is also illustrated in ﬁg.
F.2 where it is seen that most structures are very similar.
162

The reference structure is shown with balls and sticks, while the other structures are
shown as lines. The ﬁnal conformations are generally very similar.

Appendix G
Letter from the Gaussian
Technical Support A
This appendix present an email correspondence with the tech support of Gaussian.
It describes the error occurring when basis functions becomes linear dependent, as dis-
cussed in sec. 3.2.1. It is mildly edited to remove information irrelevant to the reader
and to make the format more suitable for LATEX.
On Dec 4, 2014, at 7:32 AM, Thomas Østergaard wrote:
Dear Help@Gaussian
I have a question to an error message in Gaussian:
I am investigating the energy minima of a hexa-peptide with 74 atoms and 139 alpha
and beta electrons. In this process I perform a convergence test, too ﬁnd the optimal
basis set to use.
The speciﬁcation for each run is:
# n B3LYP/Basis Opt=(MaxCyc=480) SCF=(XQC,MaxCycle=512) SCRF=PCM Pop=Minimal
I mainly investigate the Pople basis set, and I ﬁnd that for basis set larger than 6-
31++G(d,p), many of the calculations fail. Not because they run out of wall-time (I
submit calculations over to an HPC), but because they get an error and terminate.
164

I don’t understand why calculations starts failing at high basis sets. Can you help me
to ﬁnd an explanation?
Best regards,
Thomas Østergaard

Error termination in NtrErr:
NtrErr Called from FileIO.
On 12/05/2014 04:35 PM, Gaussian Technical Support wrote:
Hello Thomas,
Although I do not have the part of the output that would allow me to conﬁrm the
problem that you are facing, my guess is that the problem is related to a bug that we
have found in G09 rev. D.01. This problem appears in cases where one ends up with
diﬀerent orthonormal subsets of basis functions at diﬀerent geometries. The ”Operation
on ﬁle out of range” message appears right after the program tries to do an interpolation
of two SCF solutions when generating the initial orbital guess for the current geometry
optimization point.
The goal here is to generate an improved guess for the current
point but it failed. The reason why this did not work is because the total number of
independent basis functions is diﬀerent between the two sets of orbitals. We will have
this bug ﬁxed in a future Gaussian release, so the guess interpolation works even if the
number of independent basis functions is diﬀerent.
Here are more details about the problem. If you scroll back from the end of the output
ﬁle to link 302 in the ﬁnal point (the one that failed), you should see the number
of independent basis functions used (NBsUse) for the ﬁnal geometry (in this example
1042):
NBasis= 1050 RedAO= T EigKep= 1.03D-06 NBF= 1050
NBsUse= 1042 1.00D-06 EigRej= 8.27D-07 NBFU= 1042
while, if you scroll back to the same part (link 302) but for the previous geometry (the
last one that completed successfully), you should see that the number of independent
basis functions used is diﬀerent than for the last point (in this example 1041):
==PAGE:181==
Appendix G. Letter from the Gaussian Technical Support A
167
NBasis= 1050 RedAO= T EigKep= 1.43D-06 NBF= 1050
NBsUse= 1041 1.00D-06 EigRej= 8.95D-07 NBFU= 1041
So, for the example shown above, at the ﬁnal geometry there were 8 eigenvalues of the
overlap matrix that were just below the threshold (1.00D-6) and the program reduced
the number of basis functions used in that SCF calculation by 8 from 1050 to 1042.
At the geometry before the ﬁnal one, there were 9 eigenvalues of the overlap matrix
below 1.00D-6 and thus 9 basis functions were dropped. The result is that the number
of linearly independent basis functions is diﬀerent for these two geometries and the
interpolation of guesses done by Link 401 could not handle such situation. We will ﬁx
this in the next revision of Gaussian so the program can handle this case as well.
There are a few things one may do and which one is best for your case it may depend
on the speciﬁc case or whatever works best for you:
A) Use “Guess=Always” to turn oﬀthis guess interpolation feature, but I would only
recommend it for cases that are simple enough where the ground state SCF would be
easily achieved even with a new guess at every optimization step.
B) Just start a new geometry optimization from that ﬁnal point reading the geometry
from the checkpoint ﬁle.
C) Lower the threshold for selecting the linearly independent set by one order of mag-
nitude, which may result in keeping all functions. The aforementioned threshold is con-
trolled by ”IOp(3/59=N)” which sets the threshold to 1.0D-N (N=6 is the default). Note
that because an IOp is being used, one would need to run the optimization and frequency
calculations separately and not as a compound job (”Opt Freq”), because IOps are not
passed down to compound jobs. You may also want to use “Integral=(Acc2E=11)” or
“Integral=(Acc2E=12)” if you lower this threshold as the calculations may not be as
numerically robust as with the default thresholds.
D) Use fewer diﬀuse functions or a better balanced basis set, so there aren’t linear
dependencies with the default threshold and thus no functions are dropped.
Option ”A” would work in many cases, although it may not be a viable alternative in
cases where the desired SCF solution is diﬃcult to get from the default guess and one
has to prepare a special initial guess. You may try this for your case.
==PAGE:182==
Appendix G. Letter from the Gaussian Technical Support A
168
Option ”B” should work just ﬁne although you may run into the same issue again if,
after a few geometry optimization steps, one ends up again in the scenario of having two
geometries with two diﬀerent numbers of basis functions.
Option ”C” may work well in many cases where there is only one (or very few) eigenvalue
of the overlap matrix that is near the threshold for linear dependencies, so it may just
work ﬁne to use ”IOp(3/59=7)”, which will be keeping all the functions. Because of this
situation, and because of potential convergence issues derived from including functions
that are nearly linearly dependent, I strongly recommend using a better integral accuracy
than the default, for example ”Integral=(Acc2E=12)”, which is two orders of magnitude
better than default.
Option ”D” is good since it would avoid issues with linear dependencies altogether,
although it has the disadvantage that you would not be able to reproduce other results
with the basis set that you are using.
Try adding ”Guess=Always” to your case and see if you get to the desired optimized
geometry.
Sincerely,
Fernando R. Clemente, Ph.D.
Technical Support
Gaussian, Inc.
help@gaussian.com

I am investigating the energy minima of a complex of 80 atoms and 164 alpha and beta

#n B3LYP/3-21+G Opt=(MaxCyc=480) SCF=(XQC,MaxCycle=512) Pop=Minimal

Lufeng Zou wrote
Hello Thomas: Thank you for giving us a chance to comment. There is an error because
1N-2H—76O becomes linear during optimization to form the hydrogen bond. As the
angle gets close to linear, there are two bending degrees of freedom rather than one
for a regular angle, which causes a problem in the internal coordinate system.
You
may use geom=NewDeﬁnition to request the program to generate a new set of internal
coordinates, replacing those read from the checkpoint ﬁle.
-------------------Begin Input---------------------
%NProcShared=10
%Mem=2GB
==PAGE:186==
Appendix I. Letter from the Gaussian Technical Support B
172
%RWF=/SCRATCH/s092989/SCRATCHDATA_RelaxMDResult_Case_0_cluster_dbscan_centroid_1.rwf
%NoSave
%Chk=RelaxMDResult_Case_0_cluster_dbscan_centroid_1.chk
#n B3LYP/3-21+G Opt=(MaxCyc=480) SCF=(XQC,MaxCycle=512) Pop=Minimal
guess=read geom=(check,newdefinition)
RelaxMDResult_Case_0_cluster_dbscan_centroid_1
-1,1
-------------------End Input---------------------
To avoid future problems like this, you may provide a better initial geometry by aligning
the atoms for hydrogen-bonding. Please let us know if there is further question.
Lufeng Zou, Ph.D.
Technical Support
Gaussian, Inc.
help@gaussian.com
